CONVERSATION_ID: 68f6dddf-5d64-8332-bcff-0af0d8ca4491
TITLE: Wet Trace beginning
MESSAGE_COUNT: 139

[USER]
Begin Chapter 1: Wet Trace at Blackgate.

[ASSISTANT]
They held traffic at the north approach while the channel kept pressing its shoulder against Blackgate. Jhace stepped in at the heel of the left leaf where the iron skin met the pier stone, squatted, and slid a rag along the seam. He dipped the rag again, wrung it with two fingers, and laid a line of water from pin to pin. The seam drank in patches and beaded in others. He waited long enough to feel the pressure thrum through the hinge bolt. Wet Trace began, clean and quiet.

He tipped the small flask. Three trickles found each other and ran in parallel for a hand’s span, then the leftmost line faltered and cut toward a rivet head stamped 12. Jhace marked it with a scribe. The iron tasted cold through the tool. He flattened his palm to the plate and felt the faint pull of outflow, a thin breath through a split vein carrying load where it shouldn’t. He didn’t speak.

Tiffani came down the stairs with her hood back and her icon card already on its clip. She didn’t look at him first; she looked where the water chose to move. Good. She bent, thumbed the rivet 12, and pressed her ear to the iron. Air in, air out, the gate told her, through that wrong path. She nodded once.

“Convoy at third bell,” Brighlee called from above, voice bouncing off the arch. “Couriers’ docket says they don’t stop. You’ve got a window.”

“Window is what we’re taking,” Tiffani answered. She turned to Jhace. “We make a shallow splice at twelve to fourteen. We keep the plate cold.”

“Cold set,” he said.

Jhace let the rag fall over his wrist and went for the pack left of the pier ladder. He didn’t inventory it; he knew where the glass slivers lay within reach and where the chalk lived. He took the smallest wedge, glass bright as river ice, and held it in his teeth while he scrubbed the seam with a stiff brush. Rust dust pooled like brown water at the base. He washed it off, then set the wedge on the rim of the rivet and tapped with the peen of the hammer.

Tiffani knelt opposite and opened the air valve on the bladder tucked into the jamb. Air pushed against the joint and steadied the leak into a predictable line. That made the Wet Trace clearer. She counted under her breath, only to keep time with the surge, and with the third swell she opened the tiny tap on the relief sluice. Water flinched back within the seam. Less pressure. They would never lift the leaf; they would coax it.

“Chloen’s fetched the Conclave chalk,” Brighlee said from the entry. Footsteps retreated. Traffic murmured on the causeway. The barge horns stayed quiet.

Jhace fitted the glass wedge into the seam between rivets 12 and 13. He did not force. He rocked the wedge with finger pressure. It drank the line of water. The seam’s song lowered a half-tone that lived in bone, not ear. Another tap. The wedge seated. He shifted the hammer, turned the peen sideways, and set the face against the iron to check level. Flat enough.

“Setter’s here,” Chloen said, winded. She held up a folded slip, Conclave crest in blue. “No HALT posted.”

Tiffani nodded without looking. “Witness still signs the set.”

She lifted the small brazier with its coals barely alive, breathed it awake, then shut it down before heat could jump to the plate. They would not warp the skin. She drew the brazier’s warmth along the seam from fourteen back toward twelve, a slow pass that didn’t raise blisters. Warm air kissed wet iron and threw a thin veil of steam. Jhace watched the steam’s shape. It rose straight, then curled left. Left meant the splice drew right. He made a second scribe mark at the edge of the curl.

He spoke once. “Edge bind.”

Tiffani answered, “Do it.”

He took a length of softened iron wire, threaded it through the tie hole near the hinge flange, and drew it tight across the seam to apply side pressure. He anchored it through a notch and twisted the ends until the wire sang. Each twist translated to pull. The seam closed around the glass’s shoulders. Water reached for escape and found none. It pooled and ran off the surface instead of into the joint.

They waited with their hands on iron. Tiffani’s thumb lay against Jhace’s knuckle where he held the wire’s twist clamp. He didn’t shake, but he felt the tremor ready to arrive from the long crouch. Her thumb stayed, not pressing, just weight. When his palm stopped buzzing, she lifted away. No words.

The setter, a young one with ash still under her nails, knelt and held the folded slip against the pier stone. “You’re doing a cold bind splice with glass. No heat set.” She was a witness, not a supervisor. She wrote in careful square letters. “Note that.”

“Note it,” Tiffani said. “Time to first seat.”

“Nine forty-three.”

Tiffani eased the valve on the air bladder. She listened. The seam kept still. Jhace watched the rivet heads. None breathed. Fine.

“Run it,” she said.

He opened the relief sluice another quarter. Water shouldered through the downcut and pulled along the gate face, clean. The seam did not darken. He tested with the pad of a finger. Dry. He held up the pad. Tiffani looked once. Dry.

They were not done. Wet Trace had found the wrong path at 12. Splice and bind had closed it. Now they checked the leaf’s arc in the water. Tiffani signaled to the winch at the bridge. Brighlee relayed to the crew. The handwheel turned. Chains lifted clear of slack. The leaf moved a palm’s width, then two, then the sound changed to the hard hiss of river pushed through a narrowing throat. Jhace watched the sweep line Tiffani chalked on the stones to track the arc. The line matched the countermark from last season. Good metal keeps its memory when you respect its heat.

At a half arc, the weight shifted onto the second hinge. Jhace leaned into the jamb and listened. The joint said nothing he didn’t expect. He lifted, once, with shoulder against cold iron as the bar at the top caught a wind it didn’t actually feel. Air shifted over water and tried to lie about load. He ignored the lie.

“Hold,” Tiffani called. The winch stalled. The leaf hung where she wanted.

She took out the glass float. It wasn’t for water; it was for alignment. A thin cylinder with lines baked into it, floating in a jar of clear. She set the jar on the sill and sighted over it to the far pier marks. The lines on the float matched the reference pins across the channel. That meant the leaf rose square and held a plane with the opposite. She made another chalk mark, two small ticks. Jhace noted where the ticks lay against last season’s arc. A finger inside the old mark. The gate had sagged a touch in winter and lifted back in summer, no surprise. The splice didn’t change that. The splice restored what needed restoring.

The setter signed her name and held the pen for Tiffani. Tiffani signed, then Jhace. The slip was not a HALT, but it had the same ink weight in the world if a stevedore took a hand off on a bad gate. They would make it public before opening for the Couriers’ Bell.

“Third bell in sixteen,” Brighlee said. “You have barges stacked and frowning.”

“Barges can frown,” Jhace muttered without heat. He wiped his hand and lifted the scribe again, not to change anything but to score the date where the splice lived. If a future brast came to look, he would see day and hour and initials, cut shallow into iron where water could not smudge them.

Tiffani closed the relief sluice and tapped the brazier ash into a bucket of water. The hiss rose and died. She stood and rolled her shoulders once. “Open her clean.”

They opened. The leaf advanced with the water shouldering past, then settled against its stop. Spray climbed up the stone like clear smoke and fell back. Jhace looked for the wobble that would show torsion. He didn’t see it. He turned once around himself to sweep for any loose tool, any forgotten wedge. Nothing.

Chloen trotted up with a leather strap full of stamped tags and offered the one for Blackgate day pass holders. “Warden wants the board updated by hand today. She says too many scratchers are using old photos for proof.”

“We write the board,” Tiffani said. She took the chloride chalk and the slate from Chloen. “Bring the Bell book.”

The north approach bunched behind the rope. Couriers stood easy, not sweating their ox teams because the sky held thin cloud and the river threw up cool. A boy from the first barge pointed at the glass wedge and the little curl of wire. His father put his hand down on the boy’s shoulder and said nothing. The barge nose nosed. The crews knew about weight. They didn’t talk.

Jhace walked the length of the left leaf once more, hand against iron. When he reached the pier he looked up. Tiffani had already written fast at the side post: “Blackgate: cold splice set at rivets 12–14; Wet Trace cleared. Bearing true as of 9:58. Witness: Conclave Setter Mel Quarr. Sign: T. Veld; J. Brast.”

“Bell will need the docket line,” Brighlee said, producing the heavy log from under his arm. He opened to the day’s leaves. The pages smelled like damp and old heat.

Tiffani took the quill and wrote as she spoke so Jhace could hear it in case he needed to hear his name said by law. “Couriers’ Bell docket: Passage restored at 10:02, Blackgate, left leaf splice, no HALT. First barge: Spindle Firth. Tender: Dac Omer. Warden notified by runner.” She signed again. Jhace signed beneath.

They dropped the rope.

The convoy started like a lung taking a clear breath after a cough. No one clapped. The oxen leaned. Water wrapped the barges and pushed steady. The gate took the shove and acted like a gate.

Jhace picked up the small wedge taps and slid them into a pocket. Tiffani pressed the back of her hand against the plate once more. It had cooled to the day. Her breath eased, and with it his kneecap stopped complaining. They stood together long enough to look like statues and not so long that anyone thought to thank them.

“Warden wants the icon card posted,” Chloen said from the stair. “She says Conclave needs their paint fresh on public boards.”

“We’ll post,” Tiffani said. She handed Jhace the hammer for the notice nails.

On the board at the end of the causeway, under the chipped crest of the Veinwrights’ Conclave and to the right of the Couriers’ Bell ringing slate, they put up what mattered. Not a story. Not a theory. The paper spoke for itself, in square hand and numbers that lined up like steel rods in concrete.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE WORK NOTICE, posted at 10:10 on Sixthday  
Cold splice at rivets 12–14 on left leaf completed under pressure using Wet Trace, Edge Bind, and glass shim.  
No HALT. Setter witness present. Couriers’ Bell docket entry made.  
Witness: Mel Quarr (Conclave Setter)  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface)  
Effective immediately. Complaints to Warden’s box by end of day.

One image: the blue-stamped Conclave crest on the notice, its circle still wet, holding the dusty print of the hammer face where Jhace pressed it flat against the board.

I want this instead

You want a scalpel? Fine. I cut it lean, kept the muscle, tossed the fluff. Opening compressed, middle tightened, wrap trimmed. Ledger moved out of the prose where it belongs.

⸻

Chapter 1: Wet Trace at Blackgate (compressed)

Bell 2 rang short across the Underface. Jhace came up the ladder and into Blackgate’s choke where carts queued tight to the right and the Posting board sat left with its Tempermark pin and droplet icon. Wet work. Keep to the outer rail. No sparks.

Tiffani already had a chalk half-circle behind them so the crowd wouldn’t eat their backs. Veil case open on her knee. Lume rod in her fist. Weld cloth over her hair. She pointed to the tie-off ring bolted waist-high to the post. “One hand on home.”

“One hand on home,” he echoed, because the body obeyed what the mouth said.

Brighlee from the Conclave arrived breathing paper. “Work order nine-three-two. Wet Trace, Blackgate. Setter: Jhace brast. Glass: Tiffani veld. No HALT.” She Tempermarked the droplet card and clipped it to the board. Icon, Bell number, names. Witnessed. The carts settled an inch. Paper moves bodies.

The Chain’s fault note had said quick Vein under the gate plate, running light along a seam and sniffing for a hollow. If it found depth, the Harrow would undercut the roadway and send carts into the Underface. Persuasion first. Force later if you liked funerals.

Jhace staged only what he needed: two wedges cut at seven degrees, trace stick with copper nib, bladder threaded tight. Wedge-before-veil. Half-beat Slake. Brace taps only at Bells 2–3. He checked his fray bands and set his left hand to the home ring.

Tiffani laid Lume light along the seam and edged the Shade from two to three. Thin glare came back with a blur and a fast pulse. She listened with her mouth a little open. “Quick, not mean. Wants the short route.”

“Short route is under their wheels,” Jhace said. “We give it the long.”

He pressed the first wedge two spans north of the gate’s mouth until the wood met resistance, snugged it with his thumb heel, then set the second at the shoulder where the seam bent under the bridge stone. The seam opened a hair; cool air touched knuckles.

“Half-beat Slake,” she said.

He thumbed the bladder cap, wet the copper, flicked clear, and set the nib to the face. Wet Trace moved slow. Pressure from the shoulder, not the wrist. Two fingers’ worth, steady. Water turned to steam under his glove and carried heat to the Vein. The quickness steadied. The line took.

“Shade three to four,” Tiffani said, rolling the rod a finger or two to keep the reflection honest. “Veil after the turn. No white.”

“No white,” he said. Whitequench lied and got people hurt. He ran the line through the turn, keeping the wet beaded, not starved. A cart axle chirped behind them and then quieted when the teamster saw the icon and leaned on his brake board.

The copper squeaked across a grit island. Jhace froze his wrist, let the shoulder hold, breathed once, and smoothed the error on a second pass. Tiffani’s knuckles tapped his elbow, firm.

“Close?” she asked.

“Stay,” he said. Warmth through his sleeve. Consent in the lane so the body stopped bracing. His pinky tremor quit arguing.

At the gate mouth the Vein made a petty cut for the wheel groove. Veins loved edges. Jhace pinched the nib flat and spread the wet into a bow across the lip, then returned to the center and pushed it along. Tiffani watched the Lume feedback and nodded without wasting air.

“Log the wedge set,” she told Brighlee.

Scratch, scratch. Ledger caught it.

Bell 3 rang longer. Jhace added a shallow counterline along the upper lip to kill any climb. Not a joint, not a Countermark. Just a reminder: hallway, not stairs. The smell shifted to steam and light metal, no rust bite yet, no Hollow chew. Good.

“Give me the lower lip,” Tiffani said.

He pressed a heavier wet, and the water veered left to right like it had found a bend. The gate exhaled. Behind them, brake boards creaked but held. The Posting board’s droplet icon did the quiet work of law.

“Veil small,” she said, already warming a palm-width Shade 1 skin with her breath. She set it over the bow he’d left, taps in measured runs of seven along the edge. “Annealshift?”

“Zero,” he said. “Let the wall do it.”

“Veil in. Trace held.” She kept the Lume tracking the line.

Brighlee passed him the stamp. He didn’t wait. Blackgate liked a promise early. He pressed the setter’s mark into the crowded plate, Wet Trace icon with his initials small in the corner. Audit, not vanity.

“Listen,” Tiffani said.

“What.”

“It’s humming, not singing.” She kept the Lume steady. “Field calm. Strathmoor puts babies down with that.”

He finished the run, sealed the wedge heads with a wet kiss, and quarter-turned each wedge so the wood found its own squeeze. The Vein’s pulse under the plate slowed to a workday beat. Wet against Veil slaked as one.

“Cost?” she asked.

“Fray bands chewed at the edge. Ear ring a little circle. Thirst,” he said.

She handed her water bottle across. “Clinic if the ring holds past 4.”

“Policy.” He drank. Veld water always tasted faintly of glass.

Brighlee slid the stamped copy of the work order into the Posting board mouth beneath the droplet icon and lifted her blue ledger so the crowd could see the marks. Names, Bell, icon. The teamsters eased weight off their brakes and waited for her nod. Paper turned to motion.

Jhace checked the face. Wet line sat dull and even. Wedges held. No bubbles under the veil. Tiffani rolled Shade to two and smiled without teeth. “Blackgate’s not in a biting mood.”

“It remembers when we make it look good,” he said.

They unhooked from the home ring. Tiffani closed her case. Brighlee walked off already writing the next form, because ink thought it could outrun failure. Sometimes it did.

The first cart came through, iron rim a hand-width from the line. The rim rode the shallow bow and didn’t squeal. The teamster glanced down, then up, and gave Jhace the nod that meant: you did the thing, I saw you. The Underface breathed slow under the stone.

He wiped the copper clean, threaded the bladder cap until it bit, and stood with it all for one more count. The wet seam shone a single shade darker than the plate, steam lifting hair-thin. The droplet icon above it had picked up sweat from a worker’s glove and gone one shade darker itself.

Public change posted: droplet card Tempermarked at Blackgate with their names and Bell; setter’s stamp sunk into the plate. Concrete image: one continuous bead along the gate face, thin steam rising while the first cart crosses quiet.

# Chapter 1: Wet Trace at Blackgate

Bell 2 rang short across the Underface. Jhace came up the ladder and into Blackgate’s choke where carts queued tight to the right and the Posting board sat left with its Tempermark pin and droplet icon. Wet work. Keep to the outer rail. No sparks.

# Chapter 2: Gate Line at Fell Drop  

Bell 4 drifted lazy and long through the Cut. Air down there always smelled half-wet, half-burnt, as if it couldn’t pick a side. Jhace and Tiffani came off the lift with their kits still warm from Blackgate. Fell Drop waited at the bottom like a patient with a half-remembered wound.  

Two Conclave hands were already on-site, boots in the drainage, arguing over the difference between “bowline” and “loop splice.” Brighlee waved them off and handed Jhace the folded docket. “Line breach under the third runner. Fell Drop register lists last maintenance six months, two Veinworks ago. Low flux, cold pressure. Not mean, just lazy. No HALT. No witness yet—Setter’s on way.”  

Lazy Veins were worse than angry ones. They didn’t fight; they sulked. Pressure pocketed, and when it let go it tore slow and ugly. Jhace opened the docket, thumbed the thin paper, and looked at the grain under the ink. Low-level leak by description. Possible drawdown below sump height. “We’ll find it,” he said.  

Tiffani had already kicked the runoff gate open to knee level. Water folded out smooth and grey. She squatted, tapped a finger along the edge where the rust turned black, and nodded. “See the pitting? That’s where it sleeps. Quick Vein wandered, got bored, sat here.”  

She unrolled her Shade skins onto the grating: two palms’ width, Grade 2, dull matte. “We keep this dry. No water over the mark until the Setter signs.”  

Jhace crouched beside her, wedged his elbow against the wall, and traced a finger through the sheen that oiled the surface. Cool, not cold. “Still breathing,” he said.  

“Not for long if we do this right.” She snapped the Lume open, blue-white core tight to a pencil. “Ready your thread.”  

He prepped the copper trace stick, new nib fitted clean, bladder topped from the spare. “Pattern?”  

“Full loop. Give it the kind it hates.”  

They marked anchor points at two spans apart, set wedges shallow for tension. Jhace worked the first line left to right, knuckles pressed to the cold plate, thumb easing pressure so the trace came out thin and steady. The Wet Trace hissed as it met the pitted steel, tiny beads forming and linking up like sweat across a brow.  

Behind them, Brighlee called down the steps, “Setter coming. Keep your hands honest.”  

“Hands don’t lie,” Tiffani muttered.  

The gate wall throbbed faintly as he advanced. When he reached the midpoint, the hiss shifted pitch. Air pocket. He lifted the nib and marked it. “There,” he said.  

Tiffani leaned closer, hair brushing the surface. “Soft pocket, middle left. If we hit that wrong, it’ll peel the face.”  

“Then we don’t hit it wrong.” He adjusted stance, braced knees, and began the half-beat Slake again, but slower this time—pressure held with shoulder, not arm. Steam curled up, lazy like the leak it hunted.  

The Setter arrived: tall, dust on her boots, Conclave tag pinned backward from haste. “Mel Quarr, witness,” she said, breathing still rough. “Proceed.”  

“Already midline,” Jhace answered.  

“Then I’ll log from here.” She wrote with her knee for a desk, the ledger trembling with each ripple from the sump.  

Tiffani called, “Shade three.”  

Jhace turned his wrist half an inch and let the trace sink deeper. The hiss sharpened to a clean whisper. “Caught,” he said.  

Mel’s pen paused. “Flux reading?”  

“Stable to touch. Damp plate, no echo.”  

“Then hold trace. Veil when done.”  

The line closed neatly, bright where the wet had just set, dulling quick. Tiffani laid her palm over the cooling section, eyes closed. “Still lazy,” she said softly. “Needs one more persuasion.”  

Jhace adjusted to the far end of the line, dipped the nib again, and pressed a diagonal crosslink. The sound it made was different this time—an exhale that emptied the air pocket. The smell went from iron-wet to metal-clean.  

“Got it.”  

Tiffani drew her Shade across the fresh join and anchored it with her nails. “Annealshift one,” she said. “We’ll let the warmth run back through it.”  

The steam lessened, then cleared. Quiet.  

Mel wrote fast. “Record: Fell Drop gate line, Wet Trace repair under third runner. Cold pressure breach closed. Witnessed Bell 4, no HALT.” She tore off the lower copy and handed it to Brighlee. “Post it.”  

Brighlee climbed the ladder and pinned the slip beside the droplet icon. Paper crisped under the humidity and curled like it wanted to remember.  

Tiffani checked her gauges—minimal backflow, plate temperature dropping, no vibration. “Field clear.”  

Jhace stood, shoulders tight from crouching. He unclipped the copper stick and set it aside to cool. “Nothing left to argue with.”  

Mel gave him a small nod. “You keep your joints tidy, Brast. The Conclave will like that on record.”  

“Records don’t rust,” he said.  

“Neither should gates.”  

They packed their kits without hurry. The sump water had already cleared, a slow retreat down the channel. Fell Drop settled back into itself, sound of the sluice turning soft.  

Tiffani leaned one shoulder on the wall, flexing her fingers to ease the sting. “You hearing ring from earlier?”  

“Faint.” He touched the side of his ear. “Still policy. Clinic if it hums past 4.”  

“Policy keeps you alive long enough to complain.” She smiled without showing teeth.  

By the time they reached the lift, Brighlee was chalking the next docket. Mel signed the upper copy, folded it, and handed it off. “Filed and sealed,” she said. “Conclave thanks you for your patience.”  

Patience had nothing to do with it. Work done clean didn’t owe thanks.  

They rose slow on the lift, Fell Drop shrinking below, the gate face catching the last of the Lume light. One small bead of steam still hovered above the seam and then vanished.  

**Public artifact:** Fell Drop gate docket, Conclave-stamped at Bell 4, “Wet Trace repair under third runner, no HALT, witness Mel Quarr.”  
**Concrete image:** faint steam ribbon dissolving over a calm sump while the Conclave seal dries blue on damp paper.  

---

**Continuity Ledger — separate**  
• Posted clauses (current): Full loop pattern for lazy Vein; Shade three annealshift one; wedges two-span tension; witness required at first Slake.  
• Artifacts: Conclave docket Bell 4; setter’s field log signed; repaired seam under Fell Drop third runner; trace line crosslinked.  
• Costs: Jhace: ear hum persistent; muscle strain right shoulder. Tiffani: finger heat, mild dehydration. Time: Bell 4. Coin: half-day credit to Conclave. Clinic: check ring by Bell 5.

[ASSISTANT]
# Chapter 2: Countermark Line

Bell 4 slid through the gate arch like a wire drawn through a die. Jhace had his shoulder to the left leaf, hand on the home ring, eyes on the pier stone ticks that had drifted out of truth. Tiffani brought the float jar up in both hands, glass clinking soft against her thumb ring. The river sat high after last night’s push; you could feel the lift in the chain through the stone underfoot.

“Half arc,” she said.

Brighlee relayed to the winch. Chain cleared slack. The leaf came off its rest and moved into water slow and even. Air pressed cool against their necks where the channel breathed through the gap. Jhace set a chalk string at the sill and paid it out across the pier face to catch the sweep. He watched the leaf’s heel skim the new bow they’d laid that morning. Not a whisper. Good.

Tiffani set the float jar on the sill and sighted past it along the two old pins hammered into the far pier seasons ago. Both pins were a touch low against the float’s line; winter had let the channel drop them a hair, or else somebody’s hammer work had been lazy the last time. Either way, their marks looked wrong now.

“Hold,” Tiffani called. The leaf stopped at the throat.

She took the marker wedge, stood on the sill lip, and tapped a small scribe mark on the pier stone where the float said true. Jhace chalked across it so it would show through spray. First operation placed: a new Countermark line, not guesswork.

“Bring her up a palm,” she said.

The winch creaked. Water hauled itself through the gap and hissed along the leaf’s upstream face. The float trembled, then steadied. Tiffani set a second mark to bracket the arc. If the hinge lied, the space between marks would change under load. It didn’t.

“Field calm,” she said, keeping the glass to her eye. “We’re pinning this.”

Jhace checked his tool roll. Two tempered pins with mushroom heads, a bit that would cut stone without blowing the edge, a bellows to keep the dust honest, the small jar of resin to wet the hole. He grabbed the drill brace and braced his knee against the pier.

Chloen came in under the arch with the Conclave’s seal box tucked under her arm. “Warden says pin only with set witness. She doesn’t want some apprentice drilling her pier just because they can spell their names.”

“She’ll get a witness,” Tiffani said, not taking her eye off the float. “Mel Quarr signed the first splice. She’s still on shore.”

Chloen disappeared again with her too-fast feet. Brighlee, in the habit of counting bells against paperwork, opened the blue ledger and set it on the Posting board’s cap so spray wouldn’t soak the page edges. “Entry started. Countermark proposed. Blackgate left leaf. Bell 4 half.”

Jhace sighted the bit’s nose against the chalk tick and held his breath while he began to turn. Stone complained with a dry bite, then settled into a rhythm. He kept the brace vertical and steady, letting the bit nibble without forcing. Too much pressure and the hole would cone. He pumped the bellows with his off hand and sneezed dust out of his nose without taking the bit off line.

“Depth one thumb,” Tiffani said, watching his wrist where it cast a small shadow across the float. She could read depth in that shadow the way some people read faces. “No more or you’ll split the old layer.”

He nodded once and counted the turns. The brace warmed under his palm. He pulled the bit and blew the hole. Dust fluffed out and settled on the river wet like flour.

“Resin,” he said.

Tiffani snapped the resin jar seal with the edge of her thumbnail and wicked a line into the hole with a slender brush. The resin drank and went dark. She tapped the excess away so no skin would grow proud and catch current later.

“Pin,” she said.

Jhace set the first pin, head cupped in his left hand so it wouldn’t skate. He brought the hammer face square and tapped it in short beats that lived in the root of his wrist. The pin slid clean and locked with a sound like a dry nail finding old wood. He rocked the head to test set. It didn’t turn. He stamped the head with the small die he carried for this only: BG-L-4-2. Blackgate left, bell four, second season.

“Second hole,” Tiffani said.

He drilled the match head width above the first bracket, two hands to the brace, breath slow. The second pin set easier. Metal kissed stone with air trapped in a small sigh. He stamped its head to match. The float line looked like a thread drawn taut over glass.

Mel Quarr’s boots clattered down the steps. The setter shook river spray off her cuffs and looked once at the float, once at the pins, once at Jhace. “Witness present. No HALT.”

“Write it,” Brighlee said, pen already moving.

They weren’t done. A pin was a point. A Countermark was a direction carried through time. Tiffani snapped a chalk line between the two pin heads and set it tight against the pier. She snapped the line to lay chalk smooth into the grain, then took out the small scoring chisel to cut that powder into a channel a hair deep. She did not push for beauty. She pushed for a line that water wouldn’t wash clean in the first rain.

“Shade one, just to warm the skin,” she said.

Jhace lit the smallest coal and moved it in a lazy circle near the stone, enough heat to dry the chalk into the first two layers of the pier. The air smelled like warm river and old mortar. He lifted the heat away before the line could bake brittle.

They rolled the leaf across the new line and watched the heel pass. It held true within the chalk. The pins sat where the float said to sit. Tiffani set her hand on the plate and felt nothing new.

“Mark the heel,” she said.

He knelt by the hinge and etched a small arrow into the iron, not for decoration. Arrows let the next body know what this body had believed. He ticked the date again under the arrow with the same shallow V he’d used in the morning.

Traffic squeezed against the north rope. A courier with sand on his boots looked like he wanted to say something, then didn’t, because the droplet icon still hung from its clip and men who valued their wheels respected the signs. Tiffani rolled her shoulders and stood, frowning at the right leaf now.

“Something?” Jhace asked.

“Right leaf throws a fake lean when the south wind comes through this throat. It hums hot. Not today,” she said, sniffing the air like she could taste heat in it. “We’ll put that on the list for lean season.”

“Clinic,” Brighlee said to Jhace, practical as paper. “You said ring into Bell 4. Is Bell 4.”

“It’s small,” he said.

“Policy,” Tiffani said, and looked at his ear with her head tilted. “Go after we post.”

The chain team on the winch waited with hands resting loose on the bar. Water ran as if it had never done anything else. The float jar’s lines wavered because of air, not because of work. Tiffani handed the jar to Chloen to box. Chloen’s quick hands slid glass into felt and shut it with that special I-know-what-this-is worth care she sometimes remembered.

“Write the board,” Tiffani told Brighlee.

Brighlee did, square hand like a plate edge. “Countermark set Blackgate left pier at Bell 4 half. Two tempered pins stamped. Line cut. Witness: Setter Quarr. No HALT. Arc held.”

Tiffani stepped back and looked at the Posting board, then at the pier, then at the water. The same three checks she did every time. When the checks matched, she exhaled, and his own back let go. The tremor that wanted to practice in his little finger didn’t make a case. They fed their kits back into their own pockets where the weights lived right.

“Float back to Conclave,” Mel Quarr said, already half up the steps. “If you drop it, I sign your pay over to the river.”

“Understood,” Chloen piped.

Jhace wiped his hands on the inside of his belt, where the black wouldn’t show, and leaned close enough to the pins that he could see the ring of resin. No voids. No chatter marks. Stone and metal married clean.

“Open for Bell,” Tiffani called.

They took the leaf to rest and ran the rope in. Carts started through. Someone spat a seed hull into the water and then stiffened when Mel Quarr looked back down from the stairs with a setter’s stare. Hulls did damage in underways when they piled in corners. The boy who had spat went red and kicked the hull into the river properly. Paper and stares moved bodies.

Chloen handed up the seal box. Tiffani pressed the blue circle onto the work notice and held it there until the pigment took an even skin. She made sure the edge didn’t pool and the center didn’t blur. Jhace felt the relief come into the air when the circle released his part of the morning like heat leaving a plate set back into shade.

They walked the stones once more out of habit, checking nothing because that was the job. The pins shone a little in the thin light that squeezed through the arch. The chalk line lay straight as a drawn wire.

They put their names where the law wanted them.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE COUNTERMARK NOTICE, posted Bell 4 half  
Two tempered pins set on left pier to fix arc reference for Blackgate left leaf. Line scored and heat-set.  
Glass float aligned to far pier pins; arc holds at half and throat.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Effective at posting. Couriers’ Bell docket entry noted.

Concrete image: twin pin heads flush in the pier stone with a chalk line running taut between them, river light wobbling on the glass jar where the float rests empty beside the sill.

# Chapter 3: Wind Lean Calibration

Bell 5 lifted thin from the Couriers’ tower. Tiffani pressed a glass plumb into Jhace’s palm and pointed at the right leaf. South air slid through the throat and made the plate whisper. He set the plumb at the sill’s midline and let the string settle against the pier mark that had been true last season. The bob kissed stone, wobbled, and steadied three hairs off the mark. Lean.

“Gauge wire,” she said.

He threw a fine wire across to the opposite post and tensioned it until it sang, then chalked its touchpoints. He watched where the leaf’s nose crossed that wire as the wind breathed. It moved right on gust, eased left in the lull, like it wanted to climb the south cheek and scratch.

“Hold arc at half,” Tiffani called to Brighlee.

Chain took weight. Right leaf hung in the throat. The south push came on small, steady. Tiffani pressed the float jar on the sill and sighted to the far pins. Left held under her new line. Right bobbed, a mean little pulse like heat off a pan.

“Guide shoe,” she said.

Jhace knelt at the lower run where the shoe guided the leaf along the stone lip. He lifted the guard plate with two fingers and felt the shoe’s face. Cold and chewed. He pulled a thin bronze from the strap, set it as a shim between shoe and housing, then brought the guard down and tapped the two small bolts tight with the stubby spanner so he wouldn’t overcook them.

“Clevis,” Tiffani added, eyes never leaving the float. “Upper hanger. Quarter, not half.”

He climbed the ladder set into the pier, the ladder that always sweat river on its third rung. At the top he wiped the hanger with his sleeve and set the spanner on the clevis jam nut. It had welded itself by habit and weather. He breathed on the nut, then cupped a coal in the brazier spoon and moved it near the metal until it sighed once. He pulled the heat away before the link could drink it. The nut came an eighth. He gave it another eighth. A quarter total. He set the jam and checked the cotter pin’s eye.

Below, the wire at the throat drifted and settled. Tiffani’s mouth flattened. “Again.”

He stayed up long enough to put his thumb on the hanger’s belly and listen for the note. It ran colder. He came down, boots on wet rungs. He set his hand on the shoe through the guard and felt the shim carry load. Less chatter.

Brighlee had the ledger resting under her elbow and the Posting board’s droplet still clipped from Bell 2. She watched the wire without writing yet. An entry for “we think it’s better” got you fined at the Warden’s box.

“Test,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee sent it to the winch. The chain bar creaked. Right leaf came off half by a finger and then back to half. The glass float line across the pins went tight and true for three breaths, then the south wind put a small shoulder in and tried to tilt the leaf. It didn’t take. The nose stayed against the gauge wire without scraping.

“Run,” Tiffani said.

They opened to three fingers and closed to half. The float stayed good. The plumb line bobbed once and went still. The wire between posts kept its song in the same key. No creep.

Chloen arrived with a wooden case short as a hand. “Warden sent a fresh shoe face if yours is down to raw.” She looked at the guard’s bolts like she could see through plate. “Need it?”

“Bronze will do for lean season,” Tiffani said. “We’ll change the face when the river drops and we can lift without screaming the chain.”

Jhace knuckled the shoe housing again. Bronze telegraphed the leaf’s motion like a signal through a thin bar. Smooth.

“Warden wants it posted,” Chloen said, unhelpful and correct at once.

“Posted,” Tiffani echoed.

They kept moving because stillness made the wind look like wisdom. Jhace set the plumb again and watched it settle dead on the pier tick. He touched the pier line Tiffani had cut last bell. Stone took fingertip heat and gave none back. Good sign.

A boy from the second cart had wandered forward to see. His father pinned him with a hand without words and looked at Tiffani’s back like a mirror. He checked his own brake board on reflex just because she stood there. That kind of influence was worth more than half the iron.

“Mark the adjustment,” Tiffani said.

Jhace took the scribe, reached under the guard lip, and cut one neat V into the shoe housing, shallow enough not to collect water. Then he climbed again, wiped a circle clean on the hanger with a rag, and scribed a quarter tick on the clevis ear so the next hand would know why the cotter sat where it did. He stamped a minute letter next to it: QTR. No room for poetry up there.

The wind shifted, stronger, the kind that ran straight along the river from the south crown and found every throat and corner. Right leaf whispered louder. Tiffani ignored the voice and stared at the float. She put her hand out with the palm up without looking. He set the plumb string in it. She took it, set it on the sill, and watched again. She tilted her chin. “Field calm.”

“Ledger,” Brighlee said, already writing. “Right leaf wind lean adjusted. Upper hanger clevis quarter turn tight. Lower guide shoe shimmed with bronze. Witness here. No HALT.”

“Write the chain link number,” Tiffani said.

“Eighteen from head,” Jhace said. He counted automatically from the head by feel, numbers like knots in a rope. “Right run.”

Brighlee added it.

A gust shoved hard, the kind that persuaded men to believe in bad luck. Right leaf took it, leaned a breath into the wire, then rolled it back like a shoulder shrug. No scrape. The sound in the throat was water and air, not iron off stone. Teamsters behind the rope let out the sort of quiet permission that comes when somebody else put their bodies between you and a long afternoon with a broken wheel.

“Stamp,” Tiffani said.

Jhace took the small die and pressed his mark into the guard plate next to the shoe V. It bit clean. Tiffani put her fingertip to the fresh cut and rubbed away the burr so it wouldn’t fillet a curious knuckle later.

They checked again because checks are cheaper than contrition. The float jar, the gauge wire, the plumb, the clevis ear, the shoe. No new heat. The south push dropped to a steady breath.

Mel Quarr appeared like she had been standing three steps up listening the whole time. Setters liked to test without helping. She looked at the wire, the float, the scribe ticks. “Witness signs adjustment,” she said. “Your quarter was clean. Don’t chase the wind after this.”

“Not chasing,” Tiffani said. “We taught the hinge to prefer square.”

Mel got her blue slip out. Brighlee brought the pen. They did the ink. Mel watched them write like a hawk watches water.

Jhace took the coil of spare wire and palmed it flat so no child could snag a face on it if it fell, because he had learned that lesson the expensive way once and carried it behind his ribs. He slid the coil into his belt.

Wind leaned again, then eased as a cloud shouldered the sun and put shade across the river. Tiffani’s breath settled so quietly that only his own body knew the difference. The small hum in his left hand gave up.

“Open for Bell,” she called.

Brighlee lifted the rope. Carts moved. The first iron rim passed the shoe housing at a new angle and didn’t bite. The teamster cut his eyes at the gauge wire Jim line and then away. He trusted the plate because it behaved. The water at the throat lifted in stripes and lay down again. Work done, but not over. It never was.

Chloen, careful now, held up the seal box. Tiffani took the stamp and pressed the blue ring onto the fresh slip without smearing. She brought it direct to the Posting board and clipped it under the droplet icon without a second breath. Ink met air and skinned.

They walked side by side to the plumb once more, because you do not delegate the last look. The bob held its stone. The float glass looked like it had swallowed a thread of straight river. The clevis ear sat where it had been told. Bronze sat quietly in its new dark.

They stood one more count and stepped back.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE WIND-LEAN ADJUSTMENT NOTICE, posted Bell 5  
Right leaf calibrated for south-wind load. Upper hanger clevis tightened one quarter turn at link 18 (right run). Lower guide shoe shimmed with bronze; guard plate stamped.  
Arc true at half under steady south push. Float and plumb confirmed.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Docket entry made at Couriers’ Bell.

Concrete image: a glass plumb bob touching the pier tick dead-on while the right leaf hangs at half in a south wind, gauge wire drawn taut across the throat, bronze shim hidden under a guard that carries a fresh scribed V.

# Chapter 4: Relief Sluice Purge

Bell 6 struck short. Jhace crouched at the left relief mouth where the grate sat low and brown with river. He set the stubby spanner on the first bolt and leaned until the threads popped free of their habit. He took each bolt in order, clockwise, not because of ritual but because you don’t lose track when your hands move like a gear.

“Half-open on the flap,” Tiffani said, already at the valve stem with the bar. She cracked it a quarter and listened to the throat. Water pushed, met the face, and hissed through the narrow in a steady ribbon.

Brighlee clipped the droplet icon to the Posting board and read off the work slip into her ledger so the queue could hear without pretending not to look. “Relief purge at left mouth. No HALT. Witness to be fetched.” A teamster moved his wheel off the stones by an inch, the quiet way law tells bodies where to stand.

Jhace freed the final bolt and shouldered the grate out of its seat with a short lever he kept for this: a straight bar with a bevel worn thin. The grate rose, coughed black water, and slumped to stone. He eased it down so fingers didn’t get between iron and pier. Silt lay mounded behind, packed like wet glass dust, cold under the river film.

“Probe,” Tiffani said.

He slid the flat rod in along the bottom and felt the first foot go light, then hit a tougher layer that didn’t give. He rocked the rod. Air burped up through the silt and made a thin circle on the surface. Below that, hard. He backed the rod out and shook the silt free with a quick whip.

“Plate lip is undercut,” she said, not guessing. She pressed the Lume to the opening, Shade at two, and watched how the light slid along the inside face. It broke right where the lip should have been solid. “We pull it clean and give it a wall.”

“Drag,” he said.

Chloen came thudding down the steps with a coil of thin chain around her shoulder and a sack clanking like a jar shelf. “Warden said mesh is in the sack. And a spare grate strap.” She dropped the chain into his hands and backed out of splash.

Jhace fed the chain into the relief run and let the water carry it under the silt mat. He felt for the slack, then hauled it back slow until the chain caught. He rigged a loop on the near post and set the lever bar into it as a makeshift capstan. He didn’t ask for hands. Tiffani set her palm flat to his shoulder blade and put steady weight. The chain moved like a saw through damp wood, then jumped. A full sheet of silt lifted and rolled out like dark breath.

He shoved the grate aside and let the river do its part. Water shouldered through the gap and took the silt in coats. He kept the chain moving in small bites so the mat wouldn’t break and wedge deeper. When the bar warmed in his hands he switched grip and shook the ache out without thinking about it.

“Hold flow,” Tiffani said. She eased the valve to keep the mouth from starving and drawing a vacuum in the run. “Keep her at shoulder.”

Silt thinned. The rod slid deeper now without complaint. He angled it up and felt the lip’s underside. Hollow ran a hand’s width back.

“Veil shelf,” she said.

He set the tray from her case against the stone, the one with six thin flats of Shade 1 ready to take a skin. She warmed one by breath, then by coal lifted near and gone, just enough to keep it pliant. He held it like you hold glass you mean to keep. She set it under the lip with light taps from the heel of her hand, building a shelf where the undercut had eaten. Tap count seven, rest, seven, rest. Water talked past them without changing tone. Air kept its quiet.

“Slake,” she said.

He thumbed the bladder cap and ran a small wet along the meeting edge, shoulder pressure only, careful not to starve the Veil. The bead darkened, then leveled. No white.

“Second shelf,” she said.

He set another Shade flat, this one shorter to meet the first and keep the corner square. She tapped in the measure of seven again. He Slaked it to match.

Brighlee stood back from the spray with her copy of the slip held under the Posting board lip. “Write I saw it,” Mel Quarr said from the steps as she appeared with a coil of blue cord around her wrist like she’d been tying somebody else’s work. The setter looked once at the undercut, once at the Veil, once at the wet. “No HALT.”

“Noted,” Brighlee said, and recorded the words without adornment. Mel’s name had weight in ink. The carts behind the rope didn’t shift, but shoulders dropped half an inch in a row.

“Check the throat for snag,” Tiffani said.

Jhace slid the rod along the bottom. The chain under the mat came free with a hiss and a sigh and a small lick of black water that went clear the way sugar vanishes into hot tea but without the metaphor. He pulled the chain and flaked it clean so it wouldn’t cut ankles if someone stepped wrong later.

“Face the lip,” she said.

He brought the small planing stone from his roll and stroked the Veil’s face into the lip in long, even pulls, rinsing the stone by dipping it into the relief stream. A mouth needs edges that don’t catch. He set his thumb to the face until it felt like one plane.

“Mesh,” Tiffani said.

Chloen opened the sack and showed the rolled sheet. It was good mesh, square, not one of the cheap diamond jobs that curled. Jhace cut it to width with shears that had already eaten that metal a hundred times. He fit it inside the mouth where it would live upstream of the grate, keeping trash on the face where a hand could pull it instead of letting it pack deep.

“Pins,” he said.

Tiffani had them ready: four tempered pins with shallow heads so water wouldn’t grab. He drilled the holes with the bit he kept for stone and face plate junctions, shallow and clean, bellows between drills, resin brushed into each hole with the same care she gave to lashes on a small seam. He slid each pin and tapped in light beats. The mesh pulled snug and stayed.

“Grate back,” she said.

They seated the grate. He cleaned the bolt threads with the wire comb and set them by hand before letting the spanner turn. Cold threads told you if you were cross. They weren’t.

“Open valve,” she said.

She rolled the bar and the flap sighed free. Water spread across the mesh face in even bands. No choke. The stream took a handful of leaves and a strip of paper and pasted them flat against the mesh. Chloen, quick this time with her hands, peeled them away with the long hook and dropped them into the basket that sat by her shin for exactly that job.

“Test run,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee signaled the winch. They brought the left leaf off rest and back to rest to see if the change talked anywhere else. The float on the sill said nothing new. The plumb line stayed true on the pier tick. The sound in the throat didn’t sharpen.

“Mark the lip,” Tiffani said.

Jhace cut a small L under the grate housing and ticked the season. He pressed his stamp into the guard plate by the relief mouth, one neat bite. He didn’t like the ridge the stamp left, so he turned his thumb into a small burnisher and took it off. No cuts for skin here.

“Cost?” Tiffani asked.

“Forearm heat. Fingers in the cold went soft, then back,” he said.

She passed the bottle. “Clinic if they don’t come back by 7.”

He drank and felt the taste go clean the way glass feels when it’s not nicked. Her breathing eased by a hair. The small tremor in his off hand decided it didn’t need to show off and stayed gone.

A boy in a salt cap hovered at the rope. His father’s hand sat on his shoulder. Jhace lifted the mesh corner with the hook so the boy could see silt against the wire. The boy’s eyes went flat and interested, not big. Good. He’d sleep fine and keep his fingers.

“Write the board,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee already had the slip open on the cap. She wrote square: “Relief purge complete. Left mouth cleared. Under lip Veiled with Shade 1, two flats. Slake run. Mesh pinned. Grate reset. No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr. Bell 6.”

Mel watched the ink settle, then pressed the Conclave blue into the lower left. She didn’t look at them while she did it, the way some people don’t look at a stove when it’s doing exactly what it should. She clipped the notice under the droplet icon with the same care Tiffani used on pins.

They stood the length of one breath and ran the checks again because that’s cheaper than explaining why you chose not to. Rod along the bottom: clear. Valve bar smooth. Mesh flush, no flutters at the corners. Grate seated, bolt heads low. The river’s skin lay glass-flat across the mouth. Air came cool from the throat, not hot.

“Open for Bell,” Tiffani called.

Rope went. Carts moved. The first wheel passed with room for a breath between hub and post. Someone’s kid practiced breathing slow like men do when they copy each other and pretend it’s weather. The relief stream took what it needed and went its way.

Chloen held up the seal box, already open. Tiffani pressed the second blue onto the day’s docket strip for the Couriers’ tower runner and folded it with clean corners. Brighlee tucked the ledger under her arm and shook her pen dry into the river where it wouldn’t mark a boot.

They walked the stones once more, left mouth to right mouth, not looking for praise, looking for trouble. Trouble stayed quiet. The mesh glimmered under water like a small grid of glass. The Veil shelf held a plane.

The work was not a story; it was a change made and logged.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE RELIEF MOUTH PURGE NOTICE, posted Bell 6  
Left relief mouth cleared of silt pack. Under-lip undercut corrected with two Shade 1 flats, Slaked and faced. Mesh screen pinned with four tempered pins; grate re-seated and bolts reset; valve tested.  
Arc unchanged; float and plumb hold. No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Couriers’ Bell docket entry filed.

Concrete image: clean mesh shining under a thin sheet of water at the left relief mouth, two fresh pin heads flush on the face plate, grate bolts seating cold while the stream carries a leaf along the grid and through.

[USER]
5th and remember this moving forward
I can give you one subtle, bad note if you want to be utterly ruthless about the prose, though it's more about word choice than structure.
The chapter is extremely strong, but the use of the word "subtle" and its variants appears a few times and slightly softens the metallic, hard edge of the prose.
Here are the instances and why they might be swapped for something sharper:
The "Bad Note" (On Subtlety)
1. The Gate's Response:
> "The gate exhaled, a sigh of reluctant truce." (Chapter 1)
> "The gate seemed to settle." (Chapter 3)
> "The right leaf pulsed faintly, a subtle rhythm like warmth rising from heated metal." (Chapter 3)
> 
The word "subtle" (or phrases like "faintly" or "seemed to") works against the immediate, sensory reality you've built. The world of Blackgate is defined by precise measurements—a "quarter turn, not half" or "three hairs shy." The machine's response should feel definite, even if small.
 * Suggestion: Instead of a "subtle rhythm," try something more tactile, like "a tight, deep pulse." Instead of the leaf "seeming to settle," make the observation concrete: "The leaf locked."
2. The Final Image:
> "The wet seam gleamed a single shade darker than the plate, steam lifting hair-thin like a fading hope." (Chapter 1)
> "Tiffani’s breathing evened so subtly only proximity revealed it." (Chapter 3)
> 
The goal here is high precision, not subtlety. You've already defined precision in the text.
 * Suggestion: For Tiffani's breathing, replace "subtly only proximity revealed it" with a direct observation of the result: "Tiffani's breathing smoothed, her focus returning to zero." The focus is on the professional state of calm, not how quietly it was achieved.
Conclusion
This is highly subjective, but if you want to make the prose feel as hard and sharp as the copper nib and the bridge stone, filter the manuscript for "subtle," "faintly," and "seemed."
Replace these soft descriptors with words that convey precision, certainty, and definite, small action—even when the effect is tiny. In this world, nothing is truly "subtle"; everything is a measurable displacement or a minute shift in tone.

[ASSISTANT]
Noted. Cutting “subtle/faint/seemed” from the toolkit. Precision only. Chapter 5 below.

# Chapter 5: Lower Hinge Seat

Bell 7 pressed through the arch. Jhace set a drift to the lower hinge ear and touched the pin head with the peen. Tiffani had the chain just off slack and her hand on the home ring. “Quarter lift,” she called.

Brighlee relayed. Chain took weight. The left leaf rose a finger. Load came off the lower seat. Jhace felt the pin’s shoulder free itself from the gall it had grown in winter. He brought the peen true and gave three hard taps, not big, just clean. The pin shifted a hair and stopped against the collar.

“Hold there,” Tiffani said. She crouched with the float jar on the sill, lines tight over the far pins. “Arc holds.”

“Jack wedge,” Jhace said.

Chloen handed him the jack wedge with both palms, the way you pass glass. He slid it between ear and plate from the river side and drove it home with short strokes. The leaf laid weight across the wedge, not the seat, and the pin stopped talking.

“Cold sleeve,” Tiffani said.

He opened the waxed packet. Two iron C-sleeves lay inside, thin, trued, oiled. He breathed on the first until it took air shine, then set the lip to the bore and coaxed it around the pin with finger pressure and a small wood block. No burrs. No twist. The open mouth of the C closed to a line.

“Second,” she said.

He dressed the second sleeve on the far side of the bore, teeth showing because lip work called for teeth. He eased it around and pinched the gap shut with the wood block, heel steady. The bore now held two skins inside its old self.

“Draw wire,” Tiffani said, hand already at his belt for the coil.

He fed the soft iron wire through the tie hole and across the ear, brought it back, and cinched it with the twist clamp until the wire rang. Pressure went into the ear the way air goes into a bellows. The sleeves seated. He rolled the pin back with a single tap to kiss fresh iron.

“Float?” he asked.

“Lines true,” she said without moving her eye from the glass. “Bring the collar.”

He set the collar in place, cleaned the threads with the comb, and ran the nut by hand until it met seat. He split the last quarter with the spanner, one clean pull. No chatter. He set the cotter and bent the ears down with his thumb so they wouldn’t catch a cuff.

“Jack out,” Tiffani said.

He backed the wedge. The leaf settled onto the dressed seat. Weight traveled through fresh iron. The pin head told the truth under his palm. No wobble.

“Lift to half,” she called.

The winch took it. The leaf rose to half. Water pushed through the throat. The shoe below whispered where bronze met stone, steady. Tiffani checked the float against the far pins. The line stayed straight. She set the jar down and looked at the pier tick for habit’s sake. The plumb met it exactly.

“Seat heat?” she asked.

Jhace touched the ear with the back of his fingers. Cold. He held for count five. Still cold. “No heat.”

“Grease,” she said.

He palmed the small jar from his roll. Not tallow. Graphite and oil cut to run thin without washing in water. He painted a coin-thin skin under the collar and turned the nut a hair to spread it. The shine closed into a matte film.

Mel Quarr walked down the stairs with the blue slip turned open. “What’s the change?”

“Lower hinge seat dressed with twin cold sleeves,” Tiffani answered. “Jack wedge pull, draw wire set, collar re-torqued to hand plus quarter. Float holds.”

Mel bent and pressed two fingers to the ear, then the pin head, then the collar. She watched Tiffani’s float line without asking for it. “Witness. No HALT.”

Brighlee wrote quick, square, nose tipped down to keep the spray off the page. “Lower hinge seat dress. Sleeves twin. Draw wire. No HALT. Bell 7. Witness Quarr.”

“Mark it,” Tiffani said.

Jhace scribed a ring around the pin head, shallow, just enough to show the last place it lived. Inside the ring he cut the small season mark and stamped his initials clean against the guard. He rubbed off the burr with his thumb until it lay flat like plate caught by shade.

“Check the head play,” she said.

He levered a thin bar under the pin head and watched the collar for lift. Nothing. He tapped the bar and listened in his wrist. Solid.

“Chain spot?” she asked.

“Link twenty-one right run shows polish,” he said, because his eye caught that kind of shine when it didn’t belong. “We’ll turn it on a quiet bell.”

“Put it in the ledger,” she said.

Brighlee added the note and drew a small box around it that meant not today, not forgotten.

The river’s throat changed to a different pitch as a gust laid air along it. Tiffani watched the gauge wire they’d rigged for wind on the last bell. The leaf met the push and kept its plane. “Arc steady,” she said. “Run to full and back.”

They opened the leaf into the current. Chains held even. Shoe ran smooth. The dressed seat carried weight clean. He watched the rivet row along the heel. No breathing. He touched the plate with his knuckles. He felt the channel’s pull but not the wrong load.

“Cost?” she said, eyes still on the float.

“Forearm hard, palm sting, shoulder fine,” he said.

She handed the bottle. He drank. Her hand lingered against his wrist until the buzz in his pinky stopped. It stopped. She took her hand away.

Chloen stood two steps back with the mesh hook tucked under her arm now that the relief mouth work wasn’t calling. “Warden asked if you were pulling the pin,” she said.

“Not today,” Tiffani said. “Sleeves hold. Pin comes out in dry season when the bed won’t argue.”

“Warden thinks bolts are more honest than sleeves,” Chloen offered.

“Warden can think,” Tiffani said. “Sleeves save the ear.”

Mel Quarr didn’t smile but watched the pin like it owed her a coin. “Sleeves read right. If it lifts, I sign the pull in sunlight, not in river glare.”

They took the leaf off half and put it to rest to test the settle. The hinge sat square. Jhace listened to the ear as if through a glass pressed to a wall. He heard water and chain, nothing loose.

“Post,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee lifted the slip to the Posting board, clipped it under the droplet icon, and called the docket line toward the queue so men would believe what their eyes already knew. “Lower hinge seat dressed with twin sleeves. No HALT. Witness Quarr. Bell 7. Arc holds.”

Teamsters eased a foot. A woman with her hand on a brake board looked down at the guard by the hinge and then up at Tiffani’s back. Then she looked at her own chain hitch and re-tied it out of respect for craft, not fear.

“Float home,” Tiffani told Chloen. “Box it tight.”

Chloen slid the jar into felt and shut it. She tucked the blue seal box under her arm the way you take bread off heat: quick and sure.

They ran the checks again because that’s cheaper than getting called into the Warden’s box later. Plumb: on tick. Float: on line. Collar: set. Cotter: bent low. Jack wedge: stowed with the clean face wrapped. Wire twist: clipped and bent flat so it could not take skin.

“Write the chain note clean,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee opened a fresh line and printed: “Right run link 21 polish; rotate at low water.” She underlined the number and drew a small arrow to the margin for recall.

Mel Quarr pressed the Conclave blue on the slip and counted to three with her thumb holding the circle down so the pigment set. She let up and looked at the edge to make sure it took evenly. She signed, then handed the pen to Tiffani. Tiffani signed, then Jhace. Ink sat on the fibers like water on a good plate.

“Open for Bell,” Tiffani called.

The rope came in. The first cart moved with its iron rim throwing a dry hiss where it passed the hinge guard. No wobble at the heel. The throat noise stayed at one pitch. The river laid a sheet of light against the ear and then took it back.

“Clinic,” Brighlee said to Jhace without looking up from the ledger. “Ear ring?”

“Circle’s gone,” he said. It was. He could measure it by air against skin. “No clinic.”

“Policy satisfied,” Tiffani said, and that carried the end of it.

They walked the line from hinge to mouth and back. The pin sat in new skin. The collar did not lift. The shoe held its song. The gauge wire showed no scrape. The float jar, boxed, waited near Chloen’s calf like a small promise under glass.

They stood one count, then two, and shifted to clear the way for the next long day that would try to write its own problems onto their plate. Work kept its own calendar.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE LOWER HINGE SEAT DRESS NOTICE, posted Bell 7  
Left leaf lower hinge dressed in place with twin cold sleeves. Jack wedge employed for offload; draw-wire applied; collar re-torqued to hand plus quarter; cotter set.  
Arc true at half and full; float and plumb confirm. No heat at ear.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Docketed at Couriers’ Bell; follow-up: rotate right run link 21 at low water.

Concrete image: a clean scribed ring around the lower hinge pin head with fresh stamp beside it, collar sitting square, a thin gray film of graphite laid even under the nut while river light trembles across the metal.

# Chapter 6: Top Stop Copper Cap

Bell 8 hit hard off the Couriers’ stone. Right leaf kissed rest with a knock that traveled the post. Jhace laid three fingers on the top stop and felt the bite mark the nose had carved over a season of wind. Tiffani raised her palm. “Hold one finger off rest.”

Brighlee relayed. Chain caught and held. The leaf floated a hair from the stop, no sag. Tiffani pulled the soot pot from her case, smoked the stop face until it wore a thin black skin, and nodded.

“Touch and back,” she said.

Jhace signaled the winch. The leaf closed to a kiss and came back out. Soot transferred in a crescent high on the stop and blank low. The nose hit too tight, too high. That bought a knock and a slow grind that would turn into misalignment before the rains.

“Planish the crown,” she said.

He took the planishing hammer and stroked the stop face with short clean strikes, not heavy. Iron raised the skin, then laid down even. He checked with a straightedge cut from glass, a true strip with ground edges. Light showed at the lower quarter. He dressed that, too, with the flat file until the glass lay without daylight.

“Lampblack again,” Tiffani said.

He smoked the face, kissed the leaf, backed it. Transfer now came in two smaller crescents, high and low. He wanted a broad even print when it closed, so weight would dissipate across the face and stop the knock.

“Copper cap,” she said. “We spread load.”

Chloen brought the short copper bar from the strap, annealed last winter and skinned in oil. Jhace measured the stop face with a strip of stiff card and transferred the shape to metal. He cut the cap with the cold shear, edges square, corners rounded so they wouldn’t raise burrs against sleeves or cuffs. He warmed the piece over the brazier spoon until the oxide went from orange to a mild red, then drew it out of heat before it slumped. No quench. He set it to the stop and persuaded the curve with the wooden mallet until the fit sat clean. No daylight under the edges when he lifted the glass strip across it.

“Three pins,” Tiffani said. “No drift in stone.”

He marked the cap for two top pins and one lower. He drilled the stop plate, not the pier, with the short bit that kept its line in iron. He kept oil at the hole mouth and used the bellows to lift swarf between cuts. Holes ran true. He brushed them clean. Tiffani wicked a whisper of resin only at the interface where cap met plate, not in the holes, to close any breath lines that would drink water.

“Pins,” he said.

She handed him three tempered pins with shallow heads. He pressed the cap into place, held it with his left hand, and tapped the first pin home with the hammer face square. It seated with a tight note and stayed. The second took the same. At the lower hole he felt a chatter through the handle that told of a burr left from drill. He backed the pin, stroked the hole with the reamer three quarters of a turn, blew it, and set the pin. Clean seat. Heads flush.

“Dress the nose,” Tiffani said.

He filed the gate nose just enough to remove the bright lip that had been making the knock. One pass too many and you invite play; he took two, checked the glass, and stopped. He smoked the new cap, then brought the leaf in to rest.

“Hold,” she said.

The soot print came even across the face. No crescent gaps. No point hit. She set her ear near the joint and listened to air change where iron met copper. No scratch. “Again.”

He opened an inch and let it come closed. The leaf met the cap and laid without chatter. The knock died. The throat sound dropped back to a flat rush.

Mel Quarr came down the steps with her slip open, blue cord around her wrist. “What did you sell to the stop?”

“Copper cap on top stop face,” Tiffani said. “Three pins into plate. Planish on iron before fit. Nose dress two strokes. No move into pier.”

Mel put her fingers on the cap and then on the plate, then on the pin heads. She pressed the nose with the heel of her hand and walked the leaf in by a thumb until it kissed. “Witness. No HALT,” she said. “Post.”

Brighlee wrote square on the board slip while holding the ledger under the cap out of spray. “Top stop faced with copper cap. Pins three. Nose dress. Planish. No HALT. Bell 8. Witness Quarr.” She said it aloud so the queue would catch the law with their ears.

“Seal,” Tiffani said.

Chloen had the stamp ready. Tiffani pressed the blue circle, held it to count three so the pigment grabbed, then clipped the notice under the droplet icon. Paper fixed bodies. Teamsters eased their hands on brake boards. A boy in a brown cap counted the pins without needing to get told to keep fingers back.

“Cost?” she asked Jhace without looking away from the cap.

“Wrist heat. Thumb pad buzz. Ear quiet,” he said.

She set two fingers against his wrist and waited for the buzz to leave. It left. She pulled her hand back and rolled her shoulder.

“Open for test,” she said.

Brighlee signaled the winch. Chain came live. The leaf rolled off rest, then closed to rest. No knock on closing. No scrape at the top bar. He watched the gauge wire they’d left from Bell 5. No flick when it kissed. He set his palm on the cap after the second close. Warm from friction, not hot. Copper spread the load and gave nothing back but the right heat.

“Mark it,” Tiffani said.

He took the scribe and cut a short bar mark on the stop plate at the cap’s lower right corner. He stamped the cap with the tiny die that said BG-R-C. Blackgate right, cap. He rubbed the burr until the stamp lay smooth.

“Float check rest,” she said.

He set the float jar on the sill. She sighted to the far pins. At rest the line lay dead. She nodded, once. “Plumb.”

He touched the plumb to the pier tick. Bob centered and held. That told the story of rest without speech. She exhaled and the tension walked out of the air between his shoulder blades like heat leaving a plate taken off a brazier.

“Chain,” Jhace said, glancing to the right run at link 21. It still wore polish from last bell. “Note stands. Rotate at low water.”

“Ledger keeps it,” Brighlee said, drawing a small box around the earlier note.

They walked the cap and nose with eyes and fingers the way you check glass for a scratch. The cap edges lay flat. Pins sat flush. The soot print kept to a broad, even band. The stop wore a new face and the leaf respected it.

A barge horn upriver sent a pulse through air. The queue looked. Tiffani did not. She studied the cap and then the water under it and then the board. Three checks. They matched. She set the soot pot back in her case, lid tight, because one small act prevents ten dirty sleeves later.

Chloen stretched her arm out with the mesh hook by habit, then pulled it back when she remembered what job they were at. She peered up at the copper. “Shiny,” she said, then looked guilty for saying anything that simple.

“Shiny now,” Jhace answered, not unkind. “Brown by end of week. It’s the work that holds.”

Mel Quarr’s boot heel knocked once on the step in a pattern that meant she was ready to leave. “Cap reads true. If it loosens you’ll hear it before I do. Tighten before you hear it twice.”

“We tighten before once,” Tiffani said.

They cycled the leaf twice more, slow, then at travel speed. Copper took the touch and spread it. Iron nose left a clean kiss on soot, even. No flake. No smear.

“Post,” Tiffani said again, because law rides repetition.

Brighlee clipped a copy of the notice for the Couriers’ runner to carry. She wrote the docket line where runners could read it in less time than it takes to step around a wheel hub. “Top stop copper cap fitted. Pins three. Nose dressed. Arc unchanged.” She signed, handed the pen. Tiffani signed. Jhace signed.

The rope came in. The first cart crossed. The iron rim passed under the cap’s shadow and over the hinge guard without talking. A second cart followed, wheel grease cold enough to hold shape. The river pressed through the throat and kept its flat run. The stop face stayed quiet.

They walked the stones from nose to hinge and back. Hands checked what eyes might miss. The world did not care. They made it care.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE TOP STOP FACE NOTICE, posted Bell 8  
Right leaf stop faced with copper cap, three tempered pins set into plate; stop planished before cap fit. Nose dressed two file strokes to remove bright lip. Rest tested; arc and plumb hold.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Docket entry made at Couriers’ Bell.

Concrete image: a fresh copper cap tight against the iron stop, three flush pin heads catching thin river light, the leaf nose closing to leave one even soot kiss across the face.
