# Chapter 7: Winch Pawl Dress

Bell 9 ran flat off the tower. Jhace slid the safety dog into the winch rim and dropped the block pin through the chain head. He tugged twice. Steel said locked. Tiffani put her palm on the home ring and nodded at the ratchet guard. “Plate off.”

Brighlee clipped a work slip to the Posting board and read so the queue would hear what they were standing for. “Winch ratchet dress and pawl spring change. No HALT. Witness to be fetched.” The rope line eased back a foot without anyone looking like they did it.

Jhace cracked the four guard bolts with the stubby spanner and lifted the plate. Grease, dust, a tooth chipped a hair high on the ratchet’s outer face. The pawl nose showed a burr bright as a fish scale.

“Chain slack quarter,” Tiffani called.

Brighlee relayed. The chain bar gave one grunt and sat. Jhace pushed the pawl off the ratchet. It swung and stopped against its pin. He touched the spring with a fingernail. Weak. He caught the coil with pliers and eased it loose so it wouldn’t whip. He set it aside in the tray where old parts died with their names on them.

“Dress the wheel,” Tiffani said.

He set the fine file into the ratchet tooth face and pulled with straight strokes. No sawing. Steel warmed under his palm through the handle. He counted ten strokes on the chip, then three on each neighbor so the pitch stayed constant. He blew filings clear with the bellows and checked the angle against the small gauge cut from glass. The glass told the truth. He kept the face at that truth.

“Pawl,” she said.

He stoned the burr from the pawl nose with the hard slip, then rubbed the face on a cloth charged with graphite until it wore a dull gray skin. He tested the hinge play at the pin with a thin bar and tapped the bushing once to seat it. Slop gone.

Chloen arrived with a small wrap of springs bound in paper. “Warden said copper-tempered only,” she said, then pulled a face at herself for quoting. “I brought three. Two firm, one heavy.”

“Firm,” Tiffani said.

Jhace seated the firm spring, eye to pin, coil centered, tail in its notch. He took the tension up one hole past last season’s mark and set the keeper. The pawl pressed the ratchet with a clean bias. He dragged the wheel by hand against the pawl. No skip. A dry tick, steady, even teeth.

“Chain take weight to half,” Tiffani called.

They bled the dog and brought the chain live while Jhace kept his hand on the pawl so it couldn’t chatter. Ratchet took load. Pawl set and held. No bounce. He rolled the wheel one tooth and let it catch. He listened in the bone at his wrist. Clean.

“Throw test,” she said.

Brighlee gave the signal. The winch crew lifted the right leaf two hands and held. Pawl carried. Jhace rocked the bar a fraction to watch the nose ride into the gullet. The nose landed on the file’s new skin and stayed. He checked the spring seat with a fingertip. Warm, not hot.

Mel Quarr came down the stairs with a new slip already open. “You touch the pier?”

“Only the plate,” Tiffani answered. “Ratchet teeth dressed to gauge. Pawl face stoned. Spring renewed one hole up. Guard off and on. No HALT.”

Mel put two fingers on the pawl and leaned, listening for lift. Nothing. She checked the tooth faces with her eye the way good setters do when they trust their hands more than drawings. “Witness. Post.”

“Guard back,” Tiffani said.

Jhace wiped the plate’s edge where old grease had made a grit stripe, then refit the guard. He ran bolts in by hand and seated them with the spanner in a cross pattern so the plate wouldn’t warp. He thumbed each head for level. No proud iron to catch a sleeve.

“Grease,” she said.

He painted a thin line of graphite oil across the pawl pin and a touch at the tooth tips. No flood. Oil draws grit when you get greedy.

“Test cycle,” Tiffani called.

Brighlee relayed. Chain came alive. Ratchet clicked clean without the long note that meant chatter. They lifted to half, paused, then rolled the leaf to three fingers past half where bad teeth used to talk. Quiet. The pawl nose rode the gullets like it was meant for them. He rolled the bar by hand through one catch-and-fall. No back-slip.

“Mark,” she said.

He took the die and pressed his stamp into the guard’s lower right corner: BG-W-P. Blackgate winch pawl. He rubbed down the burr. He scratched a tiny stroke across the pawl pin boss to show the spring hole set used today. The next hand would know which notch to count from.

“Float at rest,” she said, habit that kept bodies paid.

Jhace set the float jar. She sighted past to the far pins. Line true. “Plumb,” she said. He touched the bob to the pier tick. Centered.

“Write it,” Tiffani told Brighlee.

Brighlee printed on the slip with the careful pressure of a person who had been taught to fear smears. “Winch ratchet teeth dressed to gauge. Pawl face stoned; spring replaced and set one hole up. Guard reinstalled. No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr. Bell 9.”

Chloen held up the seal box, already cracked. Tiffani set the blue circle and counted while it took. She clipped the notice under the board’s hook. The queue watched without staring, as if they hadn’t all learned by now that a board changes how your day runs.

“Cost?” she asked Jhace.

“Forearm tight, ear quiet,” he said. “Thumb pad hot from file.”

She set the back of her hand to his palm, firm. The buzz in his little finger gave up. She took her hand away like heat leaving iron when air presses it flat.

They cycled again at travel speed. Ratchet took it. Pawl kept its hold. No kick. He put two fingers lightly to the guard to read vibration. A thin, even tremor that matched tooth count. Good.

The winch crew stood easy now. One of them, an old one with a face nicked like a used anvil, looked at the guard and then at Tiffani. He tightened the band on his wrist unconsciously, as if being squared at the winch required being squared at the body. He returned to his bar with the look of a man who would not drop a pawl out of habit again.

“Chain note stays,” Jhace said, eye flicking to link 21 on the right run. “Low water rotation.”

“Ledger holds it,” Brighlee answered, and drew a rectangle around the earlier line.

Mel Quarr lingered one beat like she wanted the pawl to lie, then turned without a word when it didn’t. Her boots left wet ovals that vanished as air lifted them.

“Guard check,” Tiffani said.

Jhace put a fingernail to each bolt head again. Solid. He slid the hook tool under the guard lip to test for any snag. Clean. He backed up one step and looked at the wheel as if he could see heat. None.

Rope came in. The first cart crossed. The leaf moved off and on rest while the ratchet carried without complaint. A boy with a cap too big for him counted clicks until his father squeezed his shoulder and he stopped because counting is fine, but learning why the counting matters is better and takes longer.

They walked the stair up and down once more. The float jar went back into felt. The seal box returned to Chloen’s elbow. Grease pot lid set tight. Tools took their places by weight.

They stood one count at the board while air moved along the arch and laid cool on their sleeves. Work held.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE WINCH RATCHET SERVICE NOTICE, posted Bell 9  
Ratchet teeth dressed to gauge; pawl face stoned; pawl spring replaced and set one hole tighter; guard plate refit and bolts reset; graphite oil applied.  
Arc and rest confirmed by float and plumb under load.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Docket entry made at Couriers’ Bell. Follow-up unchanged: rotate right run link 21 at low water.

Concrete image: the ratchet guard back in place with a fresh stamp at its corner, the pawl beneath catching each tooth without bounce while the winch bar holds steady and the chain lays a straight line into the leaf.

# Chapter 8: Right Run Link Rotation

Bell 10 carried thin through the arch. Wind had gone crosswise, and the river shaved a hand off its push. Jhace tapped the right run with the hook and watched the polish at link 21 catch light like a bad idea. Tiffani lifted her palm. “Low water enough. Rotate it.”

Brighlee clipped a fresh slip to the Posting board and said it for the queue so the law moved like air: “Chain service: right run link twenty-one rotation. No HALT. Witness to be fetched.” Bodies eased a step and made room without looking like they had.

“Dog in. Block the head,” Tiffani called.

Jhace slid the safety dog into the winch rim and drove the block pin through the chain head. He pulled twice. Steel told him locked. He set the stop bar under the right leaf’s belly, snug at the shoe, so the hinge wouldn’t drink weight while the run went slack.

“Slack one hand,” she said.

Brighlee relayed. The crew bled the bar. Chain let go a hand’s worth and lay with a new curve across the guide. Link 21’s bright wear sat at top dead. Jhace put his finger on the polish. Smooth like glass where teeth had kissed it too long.

“Mark orientation,” Tiffani said.

He cut a short V on the link’s outer face to show where top had lived. He stamped the side ear with a tiny BG-R-21 so the ledger could read metal if paper blew off the board. He set a drift at the link pin and breathed once to keep his shoulder from jumping. Three taps with the three-pound. Pin walked. Chloen’s quick hands caught the washer before it learned to dive.

“Lift the run,” Tiffani said.

He set the small jack under the chain three links down and brought it up one turn to take sag. The link opened its throat by a finger. He slid the mandrel through to hold alignment. The chain kept its line. No twist.

“Pin,” she said.

He rolled the pin in his palm to feel for burrs, scrubbed it with the oily cloth, and stood it like a short steel candle in his fist. He turned link 21 a half revolution so the wear lived opposite now. He put the pin through, washer to seat, and kissed it home with the hammer face square. The sound under the head told him true. He turned the jack out, pulled the mandrel, and ran his thumb across the ear to check for proud metal. None.

“Spring check at pawl,” Tiffani called out of habit, eyes on the winch crew.

Jhace touched the pawl boss through the guard plate and felt the coil’s bias live and even from Bell 9. It held. “Set.”

“Bring it to half,” she said.

They bled the dog and took weight clean. Chain came live. The rotated link took load without telegraphing a bend. Jhace put two fingers on the run and read the vibration. Tooth count beat, nothing else. He watched the bright line at 21 dull by a breath under load, the way a glass sheen goes to a matte when you scuff it right.

“Hold there,” Tiffani said. She laid the float jar to the sill and sighted to the far pins. Line straight. “Plumb.”

He set the bob to the pier tick. Centered. He checked the shoe guard from the morning’s bronze shim. It hummed the same thin work note and nothing sharp hid in it.

Mel Quarr came down the steps with blue slip open and the cord wrapped to keep it from grabbing. “You pulled the pin in water?”

“Run blocked, dog in, head pinned,” Tiffani answered. “Slack to hand. Rotate twenty-one half. Mandrel alignment. Pin reset and seated. Half lift tested.”

Mel touched the link with a nail, then the pin head, then the washer. She lifted her chin at the winch. “Throw it.”

Brighlee signaled. The leaf rolled off half by a finger and back. Chain ran smooth over the head. No catch at 21. Mel listened with her teeth together like she could measure with bone. “Witness. No HALT,” she said, and didn’t waste more speech.

“Dress the ear,” Tiffani said.

Jhace rubbed the pin ear with the cloth loaded with graphite oil until it wore a slick film that filled only scratches and not the chain’s bite. He pressed his die into the outer side bar: a clean ½ mark to tell the next hand the rotation had been done, half-turn. He rubbed the burr smooth so it wouldn’t peel skin.

“Test to rest,” she said.

They ran the leaf to rest, then off, then to rest. The rotated link took it. No off-beat. He watched the ratchet guard for lift and saw none. He pressed his palm to the run and read the heat. Warm from work, not hot from friction.

Brighlee’s pen scratched. “Right run link twenty-one rotated half-turn under dog and block. Mandrel alignment used. No HALT. Witness Quarr. Bell 10.” She read it so the teamsters had something to nod to besides weight and rumor.

“Cost?” Tiffani asked Jhace.

“Forearm steady. Palm sting gone fast. Ear quiet,” he said.

She set two knuckles against the inside of his wrist, firm. The small buzz that liked to live there decided not to arrive. She withdrew her hand and looked at the plumb without changing her face.

“Mark the head,” she said.

He cut a small arrow on the chain head plate toward the right run and nicked the number 21 into the margin so the ledger note matched steel. He tapped the block pin once with the mallet and pulled it free, slow, so the head would not kick. He walked the safety dog out and hung it on its hook with the open side against the wall so spray wouldn’t fill its throat.

“Cycle at speed,” Tiffani called.

The winch crew ran the bar clean. Ratchet clicked even. Chain flowed. The right leaf went to half and home as if nobody had ever polished a link where it shouldn’t be polished. Jhace watched the gauge wire at the throat; no scrape or twitch. He listened to the plate breathe water. You could tell when a chain asked for mercy by a kind of sob. There was none.

Chloen held the seal box with both hands; she’d learned. Tiffani pressed the blue circle on the slip and counted three, then four, because wet days take longer. She clipped it under the droplet icon where people who cared more about oats than metal could see and still respect.

“Warden asked if you mean to chase the left run next bell,” Chloen said, trying to sound like a messenger and not a gossip pipe.

“Left run is honest today,” Tiffani said. “We do not fix metal that is telling the truth.”

Mel Quarr stayed just long enough to watch one more pass. She looked at the stamp on the side bar, nodded once, and went up the steps like a person who knows where she puts her feet.

They made the last checks because that’s cheaper than standing at the Warden’s box breathing paper later. Float: glass line on pins. Plumb: bob on tick. Pin head: flush. Washer: seated. Nut at head plate: stayed. Chain: line straight, no twist. Shoe: bronze steady. Stop: copper cap warm, not hot. The run at 21 lost its mirror and took the right kind of dull.

Brighlee folded the ledger dry side out and tucked it under one arm. She had ink on her little finger that would print on her cuff if she forgot. She did not forget. She shook it into the river where marks belong.

The queue flowed. The first cart crossed. Wheel iron went past the hinge guard without talking. A boy with a cap too large by a size tried to count clicks again and stopped after four because his father’s hand told him to watch the chain when it was still, not when it moved. Tiffani glanced once at the boy. The boy swallowed and kept his hands behind the rope.

“Glass,” Jhace said softly, meaning the float jar.

Chloen boxed it in felt and cinched the strap until it squeaked. She smiled without showing teeth, like she had done a real job that mattered and wanted to keep the feeling.

They walked the stones one more length. Water under the arch kept its even voice. Air came cool off the river and took heat off their sleeves. The day leaned toward Bell 11, and the metal in their hands kept the line.

They put names where law wanted them.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE CHAIN SERVICE NOTICE, posted Bell 10  
Right run link 21 rotated one half-turn under dog and block. Slack to one hand taken; mandrel used for alignment; pin withdrawn and reset with washer; ear dressed with graphite oil.  
Arc and rest confirmed by float and plumb; ratchet hold verified under load.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Couriers’ Bell docket entry made.

Concrete image: link 21 on the right run showing a fresh ½ stamp on its side bar, the bright polish turned down and gone, while the chain lies straight and carries load with a clean tooth beat.

# Chapter 9: Sill Plate Stitch

Bell 11 struck thin. Jhace lay on his side at mid-throat with his cheek almost to stone and his eye across the sill plate. The Lume threw a white edge along the metal. A hairline ran cross-grain, shallow, not open yet, but ready to walk under cart weight. He pressed his thumb nail into the line; it caught. “Crack,” he said.

“Stop-bores,” Tiffani answered. “Two.”

Brighlee clipped the slip to the Posting board and read it to the rope. “Sill plate stitch, mid-throat. No HALT. Witness to be fetched.” The queue leaned off their brakes together by a hair and held.

“Half-open,” Tiffani called.

Chain took weight. Water came up through the throat and pushed cool across their sleeves. The gap opened enough to live, not enough to invite stupidity. Jhace rolled the bit between finger and thumb, checked the point, and set it at the crack’s upstream tip. He breathed once to steady his shoulder and turned the brace. The bit bit clean. He kept it square and light so the mouth wouldn’t cone. He took it down one nail’s depth, pulled, blew dust, and ran a finger inside. Round, true.

He crawled downstream on elbows, set the second bore at the far tip, and drilled the twin. He laid the Lume again. The line between bores still read, but trapped. “Strap,” he said.

“Take a gauge of four,” Tiffani said, already pulling the strap roll from her case. She handed him a tempered strip as long as his palm, edges dressed, holes blank.

Chloen thudded down the steps with the blue cord around her wrist and the Conclave case under her arm. “Setter on the way,” she said. “Warden says watch your depth near the joint; she’ll count chips if you don’t.”

“Warden can count,” Tiffani said. “We’ll give her nothing to count.”

Jhace scribed the strap outline across the crack, marked two bolt centers equidistant from the crack and offset from the stop-bores. He took the short bit again, oiled it with a smear off the rag, and cut the first hole through plate only. No stone. He kept the swarf out of the throat with the bellows. He drilled the second to match, then countersank both with the conical so a flush head would sit without proud.

“Deburr,” Tiffani said.

He kissed the edges with the fine file until they wouldn’t catch a cloth. He sat the strap and checked the fit with a strip of ground glass across all four points. No daylight. He wiggled the strap sideways to read tolerance. None.

“Pins or bolts?” Chloen asked, eager to be part of naming.

“Bolts. Pins drift in a throat,” Tiffani said. She held out two low-head bolts with square shoulders cut for plate only, thread short so stone stayed out of the work. She brushed each hole with resin to close air paths. Resin drank and went dark.

Jhace seated the strap. He pressed each bolt through, set the square shoulders into prepared corners by thumb and a mallet wink, then took the nut up from below with the thin spanner. He turned until the strap sat flush. He pressed the glass strip across again. Flat all around.

“Open the crack for Slake,” Tiffani said.

He lifted the strap off enough to show the line, then took the trace stick and ran a wet along the seam, shoulder pressure only. The water turned to steam and carried heat into the hairline. He counted seven slow and let the vapor work. The line went from bright to a darker gray and settled. He laid the strap back down and ran the nuts again. He checked torque by wrist, not elbow; one quarter turn past seat, both sides.

Mel Quarr came down the steps with her boot soles clicking like rivet heads. “Where,” she said.

Tiffani pointed with her chin. “Mid-throat, cross-grain crack. Stop-bores drilled. Strap four. Slaked. Countersunk bolts.”

Mel pressed her thumbnail to one stop-bore and then the other, then rubbed the strap edge with the pad of her finger until she felt the flush. “Any lift under cart iron, your strap sings,” she said. “I don’t want song. Witness here. No HALT.”

“Log it,” Brighlee said, and her pen found its groove.

“Second strap on the downstream face,” Tiffani said. “Same gauge. Same set.”

Jhace cut a twin from the roll and repeated the measurements on the downstream lip, drilling only plate, not biting stone. He countersank, deburred, laid resin, set bolts, and brought them to seat. He pressed the glass strip over both straps in turn and read the line where metal met metal. The glass told him the straps wore the same plane as the sill.

“Veil bead at the edges,” Tiffani said.

He thumbed the bladder cap, wet the edge of each strap where rain liked to find an invitation, and ran a tight bead. He did not starve the wet. He did not flood. He held his shoulder steady and let the water carry just enough heat. The bead darkened and closed the breath line without gloss. No white.

“Open and close a finger,” she said.

Brighlee called the throw. Chain rolled the leaf across the stitch and back. Jhace put his hand to the strap and read the vibration. Same as base metal. No chatter. He set his ear near the sill and listened for air unwinding through the stop-bores. None.

“Mark it,” Tiffani said.

He scratched a small S across the strap’s upstream end and stamped a tiny BG-S-11 near the downstream corner so the ledger could read steel if rain took paper. He rubbed down the burr so it would not peel skin later.

A teamster with dust ground into his cuffs leaned forward past the rope and then leaned back when Chloen lifted the mesh hook without saying a word. His eyes read the straps and the bolts and the way the glass strip came out of Tiffani’s pocket clean and went back clean. He eased his brake board by one hole.

“Test under wheel,” Tiffani said.

They opened for the first cart. Brighlee called the trial to the queue. “One at a time over the mid-throat. Keep to the north line. Slow.” The teamster nodded. His lead wheel rolled the seam. Iron came on the stitch, stayed, and went off. No squeal. No punch. Tiffani’s eye stayed on the strap. Jhace’s palm stayed on plate. The strap stayed part of plate.

“Second cart,” she said.

The second wheel did the same. The stitch held. Water under the arch kept its one pitch. The float on the sill sat boxed, but in Jhace’s head the line stayed true.

“Cost?” Tiffani asked, eyes still on metal.

“Wrist burn from countersink. Ear ring small, already gone. Thirst,” he said.

She passed the bottle without looking away. He drank. She kept her hand on his wrist until his finger stopped trying to show off. It stopped. She took her hand away.

“Dress the stop-bore mouths,” she said.

He set the planing stone to the stop-bore edges and stroked until they wore a clean circle without tooth. He ran a glove over them to make sure cloth would not snag. He rinsed dust into the throat with a cup of clean water.

Mel Quarr crouched and set her ear to plate at the stitch line. She tapped once with a nail. The sound came back flat and short. “Good. If you catch creep in a week, you add a third bolt and write my name next to it.”

“We won’t ask a third bolt to do a second bolt’s job,” Tiffani said.

Chloen hovered with the seal box open like a small sky under her arm. Brighlee held the slip under the Posting board lip and wrote square so spray would slide off. “Sill plate stitch mid-throat. Stop-bores two. Strap four gauge both faces. Slake line. Bolts countersunk flush. No HALT. Witness Quarr. Bell 11.”

“Copper wash,” Tiffani said.

Jhace wiped a breath of copper wash across the bolt heads to match the plate’s heat tone and keep rust from making their work look cheap before it lived a week. He did not flood. He rubbed the wash hard enough to seat it.

“Run it,” she said.

They cycled the leaf to half and back twice so the straps learned the work. The bolts did not walk. The stop-bores did not weep. The bead sat tight. He checked the strap edges with a fingernail again. Smooth.

“Post,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee clipped the slip in under the droplet icon. Tiffani pressed the blue ring with her thumb flat to spread the pigment and counted three. She lifted, checked the edges, and pressed once more to catch a dry corner. The circle skinned even.

“Clinic?” Brighlee asked Jhace out of habit.

“Ring gone,” he said.

“Policy satisfied,” Tiffani said, and that closed it.

The queue took the rope’s motion like water finding a new low. First cart went. Second, third. Wheels crossed the stitch. No pitch changed. The sill read as one piece.

A runner from the Couriers’ tower showed up with the docket strip. Tiffani stamped blue in the lower left and folded clean corners. The runner vanished the way people vanish when they live on bells.

They checked again because checks cost less than apology. Upstream strap: flush, bead tight, stamp clear. Downstream strap: same. Stop-bores: round, edges smooth. Bolt heads: level, wash set. The glass strip read flat. Air out of the throat touched their wrists and went on.

Jhace boxed the glass strip in felt. Chloen tied the case with two turns so it wouldn’t spill. Brighlee shook her pen over the river and brushed a drop off the cuff where it would not print her later.

They stood one count while river light lay across the sill like a sheet of ground glass. Work held.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE SILL PLATE STITCH NOTICE, posted Bell 11  
Mid-throat cross-grain crack arrested by two stop-bores; plate stitched with 4-gauge straps set both faces; countersunk bolts seated flush; Slake run at seam; strap edges dressed; stop-bore mouths faced.  
Arc and passage tested under wheel; no change in pitch; float and plumb unchanged.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Docket entry filed at Couriers’ Bell.

Concrete image: twin low-head bolts sitting flush in a narrow steel strap across the sill, both stop-bores reading as clean circles, the glass strip laid over the stitch and showing no daylight while a cart wheel rolls slow across and leaves no sound.

# Chapter 10: Guide Shoe Bolt Shear (HALT)

Bell 12 pressed hard. A grind lifted off the throat like sand under a blade. Jhace dropped to the right leaf’s lower run and slid two fingers under the guard. The shoe face rocked. One bolt head sat bright and wrong; its mate had sheared and left the shank buried in the housing.

“Post HALT,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee snapped the black-bar card onto the Posting board and read so the queue would hear and stop pretending: “HALT at Blackgate. Right shoe fastener failure. Passage stopped. Setter witness required.” Ropes held. Teamsters planted boots. Weight shifted back a hand.

“Dog in. Block the head,” Tiffani called.

Jhace drove the safety dog into the winch rim and pinned the chain head. He set the stop bar under the belly so the hinge wouldn’t drink weight. “Slack a hand.”

Chain eased. The leaf came off the shoe. He cracked the two guard bolts and lifted plate. Shoe face canted open and showed the broken shank, grain dull where the bolt had torn.

“Left-hand bit,” Tiffani said.

He seated the extractor in the sheared shank and leaned steady into the turn. The shank grudged, then gave. He backed it out slow so the threads stayed honest. He chased the hole with the chaser, one turn in, back a quarter, clear, repeat. Clean threads. No burr.

“Face the socket,” she said.

He stroked the bore mouth with the fine stone until the lip ran smooth. He wiped it with oil and checked depth with the ground gauge. Shoulder true.

Chloen arrived with the fastener roll and a jaw set to urgency. “Warden sent tempered twins and a spare shoe face.”

“Bolts only,” Tiffani said. “Face stays.”

Jhace seated the first bolt through the shoe and into the cleaned socket. He ran it down until it met the shoulder and split a quarter with the spanner. He set the twin in the second socket to match. He shook the shoe by its edge. No play.

“Spring washer,” she said.

He slid a spring washer under each head. He brought both to final with equal pull so the face stayed square on the stone lip. He checked with the glass strip across the guard stalls. Flat.

Mel Quarr hit the steps fast, blue cord biting her wrist. “Cause.”

“Head shear on right shoe bolt,” Tiffani answered. “Shank extracted, threads chased. Twin bolts replaced with springs. Guard to be refit. HALT stands until test.”

Mel put two fingers on each bolt head, then pressed the shoe face. “Witness here. HALT holds.”

“Grease,” Tiffani said.

Jhace wiped a coin-thin film of graphite oil under the heads and across the face. Not flood. He refit the guard. He brought the four bolts in hand-tight and crossed them to seat. He thumbed each head level.

“Chain to half,” she called.

They bled the dog and lifted. The shoe took load. Jhace laid two fingers on the guard lip and read the note. Even, thin. He breathed and felt the line hold.

“Throw a finger,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee signaled. The leaf rolled one finger, back one. No scrape. No knock. Gauge wire in the throat didn’t twitch.

“Full test,” she said.

They ran to half and back. The shoe hummed the same work note. Bolt heads kept their temperature: warm, not hot. Jhace marked each with a small scribe tick so any walk would show.

“Post clearance,” Mel said, voice flat.

Brighlee raised the pen. Tiffani spoke for the board. “HALT cleared Bell 12 and seven. Right shoe fasteners replaced; guard reset; test at half. Witness Quarr.” Brighlee wrote square and clipped the clearance strip under the HALT card, then turned the HALT card sideways per rule. Law made space again.

“Cost?” Tiffani asked Jhace, eyes on bolts.

“Palm burn off the extractor. Ear quiet,” he said.

She set her hand to his wrist, firm. The small buzz that liked to live there decided it had no work. She took her hand away.

“Mark the housing,” she said.

He scratched a neat V on the shoe housing at each new bolt and pressed the die into the guard corner: BG-R-SH. He rubbed the burr flat.

“Open for passage,” Tiffani called.

Rope went. First cart rolled slow. Iron rim passed the guard by a clean finger. The shoe kept its song. Second cart followed. No shift. No lift.

Mel watched one more cycle and then pressed the Conclave blue into the clearance strip. She held it to count three. She nodded once and turned up the steps, leaving water prints that dried as air took them.

Chloen boxed the seal and cinched the lid. Brighlee shook her pen over the river, not her cuff. The queue moved like water finding the low again. The HALT card hung turned, its face still wet.

They walked nose to hinge and back. Guard: tight. Bolts: seated, scribe ticks unchanged. Shoe: line true. Gauge wire: untouched. Float on the sill: line on pins. Plumb: bob on tick. The throat kept to one pitch.

They put names where law wanted them.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE HALT AND CLEARANCE, posted Bell 12  
HALT: Right guide shoe bolt sheared; passage stopped.  
Remedy: broken shank extracted; threads chased; twin tempered bolts installed with spring washers; guard refit; graphite oil applied.  
Test: leaf cycled to half; gauge wire unchanged; shoe note steady.  
HALT cleared at Bell 12 and seven. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Docket entries made at Couriers’ Bell.

Concrete image: the black-bar HALT card turned sideways on the Posting board beside the fresh clearance strip, while two new bolt heads sit flush under the guard and the shoe hums thin and even.

# Chapter 10: Guide Shoe Bolt Shear (HALT)

Bell 12 hammered off the tower, dull from humidity. The first cart hadn’t cleared the north rope when a low grind came through the throat. It wasn’t the bronze shoe’s normal hum but a grind with weight behind it, teeth on grit. Jhace dropped to the run and shoved his arm under the guard. The shoe face rocked under his knuckles. He twisted his wrist to feel the play. A bolt head cold and bright under his thumb, a mate missing—shank sheared and lodged deep.

“Post HALT,” Tiffani said, already standing, voice cutting through the river’s hiss.

Brighlee reached for the black-bar card without being told. She pinned it high on the Posting board so everyone in the queue could see the rule and the risk. “HALT at Blackgate. Right guide shoe fastener failure. Passage stopped pending Setter witness.” Her voice didn’t raise. It didn’t need to. Sound carried through respect faster than through air. Teamsters braked. The queue froze a cart-length short of the rope.

Jhace’s hand stayed under the guard. He could feel the gap where the face had lifted. The shoe was doing its best to hold shape, but you could smell hot oil and grit where two metals had started lying to each other.

“Dog in. Block the head,” Tiffani said.

He slid the dog through the winch rim, locked it, and hammered the block pin into the chain head. He brought the short stop bar up under the belly of the leaf, snug on the stone shoulder, so the hinge wouldn’t drink load when they took slack.

“Slack one hand,” she called.

Brighlee relayed. The crew on the winch bled weight until the chain sagged enough to talk. The right leaf came off the shoe face by a thumb. Jhace spun the spanner in his palm, cracked the four guard bolts in a cross pattern, and lifted the plate away. The air smelled like wet iron and oil gone sour. The shoe sat cocked. The intact bolt wore a strained shine at the neck. The broken one left a black mouth in the housing.

“Left-hand bit,” Tiffani said, already kneeling beside him.

He seated the extractor in the shank, turned until he felt it bite, and leaned his weight slow. The shank grudged once, then turned. He backed it out smooth, no chatter, so the threads stayed clean. He held the stub up to the light. Sheared along the grain—fatigue, not abuse.

He chased the socket with the chaser, one full in, quarter back, lift and clear, until the mouth read smooth and the shoulder caught a clean shine. “Threads good,” he said.

“Face the bore,” she said.

He took the fine stone and licked the lip with small circles until it felt like new plate. He ran a finger across the edge. No hang. He checked depth with the gauge rod; the shoulder sat true.

Chloen hit the steps two at a time, the fastener roll under one arm, the seal box biting her other wrist. “Warden sent twin tempered bolts, matched pair. She said you know better than to change the shoe midseason.”

“Bolts only,” Tiffani said. “We keep the face. It’s still talking straight.”

Jhace wiped the socket with oiled cloth. He tested each new bolt through the bore to be sure the threads met honest. They did. He ran the first down to the shoulder, turned it a quarter with the spanner, then took the second in beside it. He brought both up in rhythm so the face drew square to the stone.

“Spring washer,” Tiffani said.

He set one under each head. They bit clean, no squeal. He tested tension by ear; matched pitch. He rocked the shoe with his thumb. It didn’t move.

“Guard check,” she said.

He scraped the guard’s edge with a file to lift any burr, set it back over the housing, ran bolts by hand, and crossed them down tight. He touched each head with the back of his hand—cold meant even. He brushed on a film of graphite oil under the heads and along the joint to give it breath against water.

“Grease applied,” he said.

“Witness inbound,” Brighlee murmured, watching the steps.

Mel Quarr came down fast enough to shake dust off the blue cord around her wrist. She looked once at the HALT card, then at the open guard, then at Tiffani. “Speak it.”

“Right shoe fastener shear,” Tiffani said, clipped and square. “Shank extracted clean. Threads chased. Tempered twins installed with spring washers. Guard refit. Slack one hand. Test pending.”

Mel crouched, touched both bolt heads, pressed the shoe face with the heel of her hand, and listened. The housing gave her nothing. “Witness here,” she said. “HALT holds until test.”

Brighlee wrote the line down the middle of the card, exactly as spoken. Wet ink on black.

“Lift to half,” Tiffani called.

The dog stayed; the block stayed. The chain took light weight. The shoe began to carry again. Jhace put two fingers on the guard lip and read the note. Not song, not scrape. Work sound. “Set,” he said.

“Throw one finger,” she said.

Brighlee relayed. The leaf rolled an inch, came back. The bolts took it. The hum stayed constant. No pitch shift. The gauge wire across the throat didn’t move.

“Hold there,” Tiffani said, watching the float line along the sill. Straight. “Plumb?”

Jhace set the bob to the pier tick. Centered. “On,” he said.

She nodded once. “Run full throw.”

The winch bar turned. Chain sang its even measure. The right leaf moved clean to half, back to rest. The shoe face stayed seated. The note stayed level. Jhace leaned in and touched each bolt head again. Warm, not hot. He marked each with a shallow scribe tick for future eye. Any walk would show.

“Guard faces true,” he said.

“Post clearance when Setter agrees,” Tiffani answered.

Mel Quarr pressed her thumb to the shoe, then the guard bolts, then looked up. “No lift. No drift. HALT clears at twelve and seven.” She signed the slip with her initials sharp.

Brighlee pulled a fresh strip from her pad and read for law. “HALT cleared Bell twelve and seven. Right shoe bolts replaced, guard reset, test to half, float and plumb hold. Witness Quarr.” She clipped it under the HALT card, turned the black bar sideways per code, and stepped back.

Teamsters heard the word *cleared* before they saw the card turn. A chain of small exhales moved through the line. One boy tried to lean forward; his father caught his collar before the rope lifted.

“Cost?” Tiffani asked Jhace, still kneeling by the guard.

“Forearm heat from extractor, no ring, no sting,” he said.

She pressed her knuckles to his wrist. The tremor that liked to wake there didn’t. She pulled away, satisfied. “Mark housing.”

He scored a small V beside each new bolt, then pressed the die into the guard corner: BG-R-SH. He rubbed the burr until it read as smooth as plate shadow. He ran a cloth over the whole to wipe oil prints. “Clean,” he said.

“Test cycle,” Tiffani said.

They ran the leaf to half, to rest, to half again. The bolts held, threads sang. Water moved past their knees cold and fast. A gust from the river sent a scatter of spray across their sleeves. Tiffani squinted at the float jar, still on sill duty. Line true.

“Post,” she said.

Brighlee finished writing before the seal. *Blackgate HALT clearance. Right shoe fastener shear replaced. Guard and seat tested under load. No pitch change. Float and plumb confirm.* She handed the paper to Tiffani. Tiffani checked for smudge, pressed the Conclave blue in the lower corner, counted to four for the pigment to skin, and clipped it under the turned HALT card.

The blue ring glowed wet against black. Law visible.

“Clinic?” Brighlee asked by rote.

“Policy met,” Jhace said.

“Policy met,” Tiffani echoed, which ended that.

Chloen boxed the float jar and cinched the strap. Mel Quarr lingered long enough to watch one more pass. She stood with her hands on her knees, listening to iron take weight and give it back. The sound stayed flat. “Sign your docket and be done,” she said, then climbed toward daylight.

Rope came in. The first cart rolled across the guard, iron rim a hair from shoe lip. The sound came low and even, the kind that meant no apology due. The second cart followed. Water licked the plate. River gave back the rhythm.

Brighlee raised her pen to note: *HALT cleared; test complete; docket filed.* She shook the ink into the river where it could do no harm.

Tiffani ran her thumb over the black card’s corner. “Turn it down after third cart,” she told Chloen.

“Down and dried,” Chloen said.

They walked the stones together, hinge to nose. Bolt heads cool. Guard level. Shoe note unchanged. Float in the box showed no line drift. Plumb bob quiet. The day slid toward Bell 13. They let it.

Jhace took a last look at the bolts. “They’ll live the season,” he said.

“They’ll live if the river doesn’t get ideas,” she said, but her mouth softened at the corners.

They stood one breath longer than the job needed, long enough for the chain to settle and the water to lose the edge of disturbance. The HALT card on the board dripped once, then dried square. Work had memory; the gate held it.

---

**Public artifact:**

Remedy: Shank extracted, threads chased, twin tempered bolts installed with spring washers, guard refit, graphite oil applied.  
Test: Leaf cycled to half and rest; gauge wire steady; float and plumb confirm.  
HALT cleared Bell 12 and seven. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Couriers’ Bell docket entry filed.

**Concrete image:** the black HALT card turned sideways above a wet blue seal, two new bolt heads dull and even under a rinsed guard, the shoe carrying load in one thin, perfect note that hums through the stone and fades clean into water.

# Chapter 11: Counterweight Rebalance  

Bell 13 ran low and steady. The air down the arch tasted of graphite and rusted chain. Jhace and Tiffani stood at the west gantry where the counterweight box hung out over the river like a stone lung, breathing wrong. The box had sunk a finger lower on its right corner, pulling chain off pitch and tugging the gate crooked on open.  

“Half-drag on lift yesterday,” Tiffani said.  

Jhace nodded. “We’re off twenty kilos, maybe thirty. Enough to make it complain.”  

Brighlee read the Conclave docket at the board. “Weight box rebalance, west gantry. Bell thirteen start. No HALT, supervised load shift. Witness required.” She clipped the slip under the blue icon. The queue could pass north under watch, but no one moved without her word.  

Tiffani pointed at the iron lid over the counterweight shaft. “Pull it.”  

Jhace knelt, cracked the locking nut, and heaved the lid open on its corroded hinge. Inside, the weight stack sat packed in wet sand—forty rectangular blocks, each marked in chalk, the rightmost row dark from river breath.  

“Block four gone soft,” Tiffani said.  

“Sand leaked down,” he said. “Corner load shifted.” He tapped the block with a spanner; dull note. He tapped its twin on the left side; bright. “Mismatch confirmed.”  

“Dog the head and lock chain,” she said.  

He drove the safety dog through the winch rim, set the stop pin through chain head, then hooked the chain latch at the throat. All movement fixed. “Safe,” he said.  

“Bleed one hand of tension,” she said.  

Brighlee relayed. The crew slackened the winch bar. The counterweight box trembled, then stilled, hanging on its ropes like a thought held halfway.  

“Extract block four,” she said.  

Jhace slid the pry hook under the block’s lip and lifted. The block came up heavy with water, sand oozing from its underside. He tilted it to show the fracture line that ran through the lower quarter. “Cored,” he said.  

“Split for measurement,” Tiffani said.  

He took the hammer, gave one square strike on the fracture, and the block broke clean. He weighed each half in his hand, felt the wrongness—a mismatch of three fingers’ weight. He slung the heavier half onto the measure board. Brighlee read from the tally rod: “Ten point six kilos.” The lighter half: “Seven point eight.” He wiped sand off his knuckles. “Three short.”  

“Counterweight must carry thirty-two exact,” she said. “Replace with solid twelve and rebalance.”  

Chloen arrived, arms full of Conclave crates. “Two new ingots, twelve each, tempered and marked.” She set them down, breath thin. “Warden says keep the old halves for assay. She’ll want to see the split.”  

“Leave them,” Tiffani said.  

Jhace dropped the halves into a canvas sack marked BLACKGATE ASSAY and cinched it shut. He wiped the seat for the new block clean with an oiled cloth. “Seat one,” he said.  

He set the first new ingot into the gap, square to frame, then the second above it crosswise so they’d lock like joined teeth. He braced both with a thin copper wedge and tapped until the sound ran flat. “Set,” he said.  

“Chain check,” she said.  

He walked the run with his palm on links. No drag. The polish on 21 still held from last bell, dull and even. He set his hand against the head plate. Cold. He watched the box swing once on its ropes, then stop square.  

“Bleed half-hand,” Tiffani said.  

Brighlee relayed. The winch crew took up half-hand. The gate shivered against water pressure, then found its line again. “Float,” she said.  

Jhace set the jar on the sill and let the line settle. The float’s thread cut clean across the far pins. “Arc back to true.”  

She leaned and looked down at the plumb. The bob hung centered, still. “Confirm.”  

“Confirm.”  

Mel Quarr stepped from the stair shadow, blue cord wrapped tight, slip already open. “You pulled weight live?”  

“Dogged, pinned, bled to half-hand, replaced one fractured block with twin twelves,” Tiffani said. “Box seated level. Chain line confirmed.”  

Mel put her hand on the new ingot, pressed down. “Cool already,” she said. “Witness. No HALT.” She checked the chain run with her eye, then scribbled a quick note on her own slate: *Weight box rebalanced; split halves retained.*  

“Repack sand,” Tiffani said.  

Jhace poured the dry sand mix through the funnel, tamped it level with the handle of the hammer, and brushed stray grains off the lip. He closed the lid, set the locking nut, and tapped once for seal. He watched the sand drink a line of water from the corner and hold it.  

“Cycle to half,” she said.  

The dog came free, chain live. The gate rolled open to half. Counterweight box dropped a hand, stopped clean. The winch bar ticked even. Tiffani sighted the float again. Line straight.  

“Rest,” she called.  

Chain reversed, water filled the throat, leaf kissed stop. The cap’s copper face caught sunlight and threw it back dull. The shoe hummed same note as before. The sill straps held.  

“Mark box,” she said.  

Jhace pressed the die into the counterweight frame: BG-WG-13. He rubbed the burr flat. He stamped each new ingot on its upper edge with small numbers—13A, 13B—then cut a single bar notch between to show they lived as a pair.  

“Weight check,” Tiffani said.  

He lifted the small scale, set it against the chain tail tension gauge, and read. “Thirty-two kilos, plus two percent buffer. Within spec.”  

“Seal lid,” she said.  

He pressed the Conclave wax disk against the locking nut, rolled it flat with the stamp that read Q-SET, and blew once to cool it.  

Brighlee wrote quick, keeping her sleeve off the wet: “Counterweight box right side rebalance. One fractured block removed; twin twelves inserted; sand repacked; seal applied. Arc and plumb confirm. No HALT. Witness Quarr.” She read it to the rope. Teamsters shifted, listening for permission.  

“Cost?” Tiffani said.  

“Forearm tight. Shoulder bruise small. Ear ring light,” Jhace said.  

She placed her hand on his wrist, steady, not gentle, until the pulse leveled. “Policy satisfied.”  

Mel Quarr leaned over the river rail and looked down where the counterweight box hung steady now, ropes vertical, no sway. “You’ll log it twice,” she said. “One for Conclave, one for Warden.”  

“Logged,” Brighlee said, showing both copies.  

“Good. Let the queue breathe,” Mel said, and stepped back into sunlight.  

“Rope clear,” Tiffani called.  

Brighlee lifted the board’s black bar sideways and left only the blue slip showing. The first cart crossed. Iron rim rolled over the sill, across the new straps, under the right leaf’s quiet shoe, and on. Water in the throat carried a cleaner tone than it had at dawn.  

Chloen crouched by the railing, chin on wrist, watching the counterweight box rise and fall as the gate cycled. “It moves like it wants to stay square,” she said softly.  

“That’s the idea,” Jhace answered. He wiped a film of sweat from his palm and ran it along the chain. The links sang one smooth line of metal against air.  

Tiffani tilted her head toward the float jar. “Line steady?”  

“Steady,” he said. “No lift, no drag.”  

She nodded, then checked the board. “Write the docket line clear.”  

Brighlee printed on the strip:  
*Blackgate Counterweight Rebalance — Bell 13. One fractured block replaced with twin twelves; box reseated, sealed, arc and plumb confirm. Witness Quarr.* She signed, handed it to Tiffani. Tiffani signed, Jhace beneath her.  

They stood a count longer than law required. River breath came even through the throat again. The ropes over their heads hung straight, humming low.  

“Close the box lid, clean the step,” Tiffani said.  

Jhace bolted the lid down, wiped sand off the stone lip, and stood. The afternoon light turned the counterweight’s iron red along one edge, made the wax seal shine dull blue.  

“Run final check,” she said.  

They cycled the leaf open and shut one last time. The counterweight box dropped and rose like a lung working easy, chain true, no sway. The float line didn’t flicker.  

The job’s sound held clean and finite—the measure of something restored to balance without thanks.  

---

Fractured right-side weight block removed. Twin 12-kg tempered ingots installed. Sand repacked, lid sealed under Conclave wax. Chain line, arc, and plumb confirmed true under test cycle.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Filed at Couriers’ Bell; duplicate to Warden’s box for weight audit.  

**Concrete image:** the counterweight box hanging even over gray water, new ingots stamped 13A and 13B visible through the open lid, wax seal cooling blue on the locking nut while the chain hums low and true along the gantry.

[ASSISTANT]
# Chapter 11: Bridge Spindle Repack  

Bell 13 thudded off the Couriers’ tower like a hand striking a hollow drum. The morning shift had gone, the heat had flattened, and the air smelled like graphite and river lime. Jhace knelt at the west bridge bearing where the spindle came through the side housing and met the chain sprocket. A thin gray crescent wept along the spindle’s shoulder—a seal failing, grease gone watery.  

Tiffani dropped beside him, veil case bumping her knee. “It’s bleeding oil. We do it now before the next lift cooks the race.”  

Brighlee unrolled a slip against her knee and read aloud for the queue even though the line was quiet. “Bridge spindle service, west bearing. No HALT. Witness to be fetched.” The words put shape to what everyone already saw—the rope stayed up but nobody tried to cross.  

Jhace thumbed the leak. “Seal ring soft,” he said.  

“Pull the cap,” Tiffani said.  

He set the drift against one of the four cap bolts and broke them loose a quarter turn at a time in a star pattern. The cap eased off with a sigh that came from air, not pain. He caught the old packing on the hook—black, soaked, and slick with watery oil—and dropped it into the disposal tin. The spindle shoulder showed a light scar along its turn face, nothing deep.  

“Dress that,” she said.  

He took the smooth slip stone and rubbed cross-grain until the scar blurred into the same dull silver as the rest. He wiped the face dry.  

“Packing mix?” he asked.  

“Two flax, one graphite.”  

He rolled the flax strips on his thigh until the strands caught the oil from his glove and darkened. Tiffani tipped a thumb of graphite grease from the tin and spread it along the twist. They pressed the mix into the race with a wooden wedge, layer by layer, tight but not choking.  

Chloen came down the steps with the small tool chest from the Conclave store and the blue cord biting her wrist. “Setter’s up top signing another docket. She said to start, she’ll count the layers when she gets here.”  

“Good,” Tiffani said. “We’re at two and a half turns.”  

Jhace ran the last coil into the seat, trimmed the tail with the short knife, and pressed it down with the wedge heel. He set the brass seal ring back against the shoulder and seated it with the light hammer, just enough to hear a change in note when the edge met its seat.  

“Cap,” Tiffani said.  

He brushed the threads with fresh oil and set the cap on. He finger-tightened each bolt, then brought them down a quarter in pattern until the gasket took load. He thumbed each head for level.  

“Spin,” she said.  

He pulled the bar across the sprocket by hand. The spindle rolled smooth, no hitch, no grind. The first squeeze of packing grease appeared along the race, dark and slow. He took a rag and wiped the weep. “Set,” he said.  

“Warm the collar,” Tiffani said.  

He lit the brazier spoon and moved it near the housing, letting the air touch metal but not glow. The heat coaxed oil into the new packing and seated the fibers. A faint smell of flax and grease moved up the stairwell.  

“Hold,” she said.  

Mel Quarr came down at that moment, the blue cord still wrapped once around her wrist. “Show me,” she said.  

Tiffani pointed at the collar. “Old packing out. Race dressed. New set three turns. Flax and graphite mix. Cap reseated.”  

Mel put her thumb to the spindle’s end and pressed. No play. “Witness. No HALT,” she said, then added, “Test at load.”  

“Dog out. Chain live,” Tiffani called.  

Brighlee relayed. The winch took weight. The spindle spun a full turn. Jhace laid two fingers on the housing and felt the vibration pass even through the stone lip. The race sang one low note and then quieted as the grease settled.  

“Add one more quarter on each bolt,” Tiffani said.  

He gave each bolt a clean pull. He checked that the heads stayed even. “Level,” he said.  

“Cycle half and back.”  

The leaf moved, chain engaged, and the spindle turned under full pressure. No squeal, no heat spike. Jhace pressed his palm to the bearing face. Cool. He left his hand there for five counts to make sure it wasn’t lying.  

“Mark it,” she said.  

He cut a small arc into the housing lip to note the packing’s set, then stamped BG-WB-13 beside it—Blackgate, west bearing, Bell 13. He rubbed the ridge flat.  

“Log,” Tiffani said.  

Brighlee wrote square. “Bridge spindle west bearing repacked. Old packing removed, new flax-graphite mix three turns. Race dressed, cap reseated. No HALT. Witness Quarr. Bell 13.” She held the slip against the board so the air could dry the ink.  

Chloen handed over the seal box, fingers careful. Tiffani pressed the blue ring into the slip, counted three, and clipped it under the droplet icon. Law back in view.  

Mel crouched once more, touched the housing with the back of her hand, and listened. “If it weeps again in two bells, pull and repack dry,” she said.  

“It won’t,” Tiffani said.  

Mel gave the smallest nod, then turned and climbed the steps.  

“Cost?” Tiffani asked.  

“Knuckles split from stone. Shoulder fine. Ear quiet,” Jhace said.  

She dabbed the split with oil from her thumb. “Clinic if it opens again by night.”  

He flexed his hand once. “Policy met.”  

They stayed in the crawl space long enough to watch the grease bead once more and stop. The packing held. The spindle’s hum dropped to the low even sound of a bearing that wanted to work.  

“Check arc,” she said.  

They lifted to half. The chain took weight; the float line across the sill stayed true. Tiffani turned her head, measured the leaf’s heel against the pier tick, and nodded. “Arc true.”  

Brighlee loosened the rope on the Posting board so the queue could see the sideways HALT from the last bell still drying next to the new blue. The two cards together told the story of work done in sequence, one problem answered, another prevented.  

A teamster shifted on his bar. “You finished the shoe?”  

“Finished, tested, signed,” Brighlee said without looking up. “You’ll know if it wasn’t.”  

Jhace replaced the guard bolts on the bearing face and tightened each by feel until the wrench note matched the others. He wiped the edge dry. “Run it,” he said.  

They cycled full once, then again. The bearing turned like a wheel on glass, and when it stopped, it stopped exactly where the tick mark said it should.  

“Post complete,” Tiffani said.  

Brighlee read the final docket aloud, each line straight. “Bridge spindle west bearing repack complete, tested under load, float and plumb unchanged. No HALT. Witness Quarr.” She clipped a duplicate for the Couriers’ runner.  

The runner showed up in the next breath, took the copy, and vanished the way they always did—boots leaving small wet crescents on stone.  

Tiffani watched the leaf a moment longer, eyes reading temperature through air. “She’ll run quiet again,” she said.  

“Until next melt,” Jhace said.  

“Until next melt,” she agreed.  

They packed the kit: Lume, wedge, trace stick, grease tin, each in its pocket by weight. The brazier spoon hissed out in the bucket with a single puff of white. Brighlee folded her ledger dry side out and tied it shut with cord from her belt.  

When they stepped back into daylight, the river’s surface looked like brushed metal, rippled but sure. The queue started forward again. Carts rolled. Wheels touched stone without complaint.  

The bearing behind the guard gave off one low work hum, even and certain. It was the sound of metal remembering where it belonged.  

---

Bridge spindle west bearing repacked; old packing removed; race dressed; flax-graphite mix installed three turns; brass seal ring reseated; cap torqued and tested under load.  
Arc and rest unchanged; float and plumb confirm; heat within tolerance.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Docket entry made at Couriers’ Bell.  

**Concrete image:** the west bearing’s brass ring faintly oiled, graphite smear even at its rim, a single dark bead drying in the groove while the chain hums steady through the arch.

# Chapter 12: North Gate Rise Test  

Bell 14 struck long and clean across the Underface. The air was dense enough to make each ring sound like it carried work in it. Jhace stood knee-deep in shadow on the north side where the gate pairs hadn’t been touched since melt season. The rise chain drooped against its guide, bright in two places, dull in three, and oily where it shouldn’t be.  

“Conclave notice said drift in the north float,” Tiffani said, scanning the board’s last line with one thumb pressed hard along the margin. “If the gate doesn’t hold at half, we reset the float rod and test rise.”  

Brighlee adjusted her ledger strap. “No HALT. Witness to be fetched.” The words moved down the queue faster than paper ever did. Teamsters looked up once, then settled back into waiting.  

“Dog in,” Tiffani said.  

Jhace set the safety dog, ran the pin through the head, and drove it home. The sound made the throat echo, a metal truth that reached up through the pier. He leaned on the lever bar. The chain’s slack burned out of it; every link straightened.  

“Float’s off three hairs low,” he said, eye on the glass jar along the sill. The plumb line drifted right of its tick. “She’s favoring the downstroke.”  

“Rise chain first,” she said.  

He walked the chain’s run, noting the two bright links that had eaten polish where they lay over the sprocket crown. He set a chalk mark across each and across its neighbor—repair by halves, not singles. “Set block,” he said.  

Chloen dragged the short timber under the run and jammed it until it took weight. The chain rose off the sprocket enough for him to work. He lifted one bright link free and turned it in his palm. The oil film caught light, showing how the polish had gone to shine instead of matte.  

“Overstress at edge,” he said.  

Tiffani nodded. “File it before it gets ideas.”  

He worked the file in quick precise strokes, cutting the ridge off both sides of the link’s throat until the edges wore round again. Metal dust drifted into the oil and disappeared. He checked by touch—smooth.  

“Turn it a quarter, drop back in,” she said.  

He turned the link and set it into place. It fit cleaner now, no tooth catching. He brushed the pin seat with graphite oil and reseated the lock pin. “Reset,” he said.  

“Chain to half tension,” she called.  

Brighlee signaled. The bar creaked; chain took weight; oil hissed. The leaf rose until the float’s meniscus split the line. Still low by a breath.  

“Float rod’s bent,” Jhace said. “Half degree, maybe less.”  

Tiffani knelt, loosened the rod’s upper collar, and rolled the brass joint with her thumb. The rod shifted a whisper and straightened. “Check the line.”  

He looked into the float jar. The line now sat exactly on the ink mark that had been true last season. “Set.”  

“Bring the leaf to half,” she said.  

The winch took up weight. The right leaf climbed steady; the north gate answered slower. Water breathed under the arch in a different key, higher. The float line trembled but held.  

Mel Quarr appeared from the stair’s middle shadow, blue cord tied in a clean double loop. “Testing or mending?”  

“Rise test on north gate,” Tiffani said. “Chain filed, float rod realigned. No HALT.”  

Mel set her boot heel on the sill edge and leaned forward until her coat brushed the glass jar. She watched the line while the chain moved. “Give it one full lift,” she said.  

“Lift to shoulder,” Tiffani called.  

The crew hauled. The north leaf rose until the hinges sang once and stopped. Jhace put his hand to the chain guide; the vibration was even, a fine shiver like metal remembering what straight feels like.  

“Hold,” Mel said. “Drop slow.”  

They eased it down. The leaf settled smooth. The float’s meniscus met the tick and stayed. No bounce, no drag. Mel nodded once. “Witness. Post it.”  

Brighlee wrote fast but neat. “North gate rise chain filed, float rod realigned, lift tested to shoulder height, arc confirmed. No HALT. Witness Quarr. Bell 14.”  

Tiffani read the line aloud so the teamsters heard the word *no HALT* again. The rope relaxed its own tension like muscle after labor.  

“Lock the set screw,” she said.  

Jhace tightened the float rod collar, then marked the brass with a single scribe stroke to show the position. He rubbed the ridge smooth with thumb heat.  

“Check for climb,” Tiffani said.  

He leaned into the chain’s belly and pressed his ear against the guard housing. He could hear the rise chain breathing through oil and steel: an even pulse, no scuff, no overpull. “None,” he said.  

“Grease the crown,” she said.  

He loaded the finger can and spread a dark stripe along the sprocket’s teeth, each motion careful enough to avoid flood. The smell turned sharp, good metal working smell. He checked the bright links once more; their shine was gone, replaced with honest dull.  

“Post it,” Tiffani said.  

Brighlee clipped the slip under the droplet icon and pressed the blue ring herself while Tiffani watched. “Law says float set to prior mark, chain filed and tested, no HALT.”  

“Law correct,” Tiffani said.  

Mel tapped the guard once with her knuckle. “You’ll run clear until the next cold. After that, I expect a replacement chain before it asks for mercy.”  

“Logged,” Brighlee said, writing a small box beside the note for *cold season chain renewal*.  

“Cost?” Tiffani asked.  

“Forearm heat, thumb pad sting, no ring,” Jhace said.  

She pressed her knuckles to his wrist, light. The small buzz that meant fatigue didn’t bother showing. She drew her hand back and checked the float line again, habit more than doubt. “Still on.”  

They cycled one more time, to half and back. Chain rolled smooth. Float stayed dead level. The gate breathed in rhythm with the river’s run.  

“Clinic if the sting stays,” she said.  

“Policy satisfied,” he answered.  

Brighlee closed her ledger with a cord wrap. Chloen boxed the float jar. Mel Quarr climbed the steps, slow now, satisfied with quiet.  

The rope line lifted. First cart crossed. Its wheel struck water on the sill and didn’t echo. A small boy leaned to see the glass float on its pin until his father’s hand closed around his collar. The boy didn’t look away—his eyes fixed on how still the water in the jar stayed while the river moved wild beyond.  

“Gate remembers its line,” Jhace said under his breath.  

Tiffani didn’t answer, but her mouth eased, which meant she agreed.  

They walked the length of the north gate from hinge to post. The chain’s oil line gleamed even. The filed links read dull gray. The float rod bore its fresh scribe, the brass collar tight.  

When they turned back toward daylight, Bell 15’s first strike was still a minute out. The metal between them and the river had stopped lying.  

---

Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Follow-up: replace rise chain at first cold season inspection.  
Docket entry made at Couriers’ Bell.  

**Concrete image:** the north float jar’s meniscus sitting exactly on its ink tick while a chain dull with honest oil runs true up the guard, and the water under the gate glides level without sound.

# Chapter 13: Mid-Span Seal  

Bell 15 carried thick through the arch, the kind that made air hum before metal joined in. Water at the mid-span licked the gate’s seam in a pattern you didn’t want to hear twice—soft at first, then sharp, like breath through a split tooth. Jhace dropped to the center platform and braced a hand on the stone lip. He could feel the water’s pulse through the plate.  

“Not surface,” he said.  

Tiffani nodded once. “Mid-seal creep. Two flats, Shade 2. We close it before it writes itself bigger.”  

Brighlee read from the fresh slip so everyone had the rule before they saw the work. “Mid-span seal, inner face, no HALT. Witness to be fetched.” She clipped the card under the droplet and stepped back. The line of carts paused without complaint; it wasn’t bravery, just routine.  

Jhace slid the Lume under the lip. Light spilled back in a slant that showed silver, not black. The seal’s lower edge had lifted half a thumb off the plate; water wrote itself under the corner and spit tiny bubbles. “One finger lift,” he said.  

“Chain,” Tiffani called.  

The crew took weight till the seam cleared water line. The exposed metal gleamed like a small wound.  

“Heat?” she asked.  

He pressed the back of his glove to the plate. Warm from friction, not rot. “Safe,” he said.  

“Cut the lifted edge.”  

He took the short knife from his belt, slid it under the loose flat, and drew one clean stroke to free the failed corner. The old Veil skin peeled back, grain even, failure along glue not Shade—honest decay. He set the scrap aside for Brighlee’s pouch.  

“Sand the seat,” Tiffani said.  

He ran the fine grit stone along the seat until it wore dull and even. He wiped dust into the river with one motion. “Bare.”  

She opened the Veil case, selected two Shade 2 flats, and warmed them over the brazier spoon until they softened. Steam rose and caught the smell of old resin. She knelt, one knee on the plate, and set the first flat against the seat.  

“Hold pressure,” she said.  

Jhace pressed his palm over the flat, slow and steady. The warmth carried through his glove. The flat conformed, surface tension doing the quiet work of law.  

“Tap run,” she said.  

He tapped the edge with the hammer heel, seven count, pause, seven. Resin darkened and cooled. The seal took. She placed the second flat overlapping the first by a finger, warmed the joint with breath, and nodded.  

“Slake.”  

He thumbed the bladder cap, wet the joint, pressure from shoulder only, two fingers’ worth. Steam lifted in a fine sheet, gone before it found light. The smell changed—heat and metal and faint oil—good smell, not warning.  

“Test,” she said.  

Brighlee signaled. Chain bled slow. Water pressed up under the new flats and met resistance. The first bead formed, held, didn’t bubble. Jhace touched the edge; it was cool, not false cool, the real kind that meant the bond was done working.  

“Veil line holds,” he said.  

“Edge guard,” Tiffani said.  

He brushed the outer lip with copper wash, thin as ink, to keep air from chewing it. The copper took immediately, changing color from bright to coin dull.  

Mel Quarr’s boots struck the stair like punctuation. “Seal work?”  

“Mid-span, inner face,” Tiffani said. “Old Veil lifted. Seat sanded. Two Shade 2 flats Slaked and coppered. No HALT.”  

Mel crouched and set her finger to the edge of the fresh seal. “Overlap even. Bead tight.” She pressed harder, testing for lift. None. “Witness. Note joint length.”  

“Eight hands from post,” Jhace said.  

Brighlee wrote it square. “Mid-span seal inner face, eight-hand run, two Shade 2 flats Slaked, copper wash. Witness Quarr. Bell 15.”  

Tiffani checked the float. “Line steady. Plumb?”  

Jhace tapped the bob. Centered on tick. “On.”  

“Full lift to test compression,” she said.  

They took the chain up. The leaf climbed until the water line dropped off the plate’s edge. The new seal shone dull under Lume. He watched the joint’s surface; no lift, no sweat.  

“Drop half,” she said.  

The leaf lowered. Water returned, pressed, and found no purchase. The bubbles stopped. He pressed his palm flat on the metal until he could feel nothing but the pulse of the river beyond. The world steadied.  

“Mark it,” she said.  

He cut a small double line at the upstream corner and pressed his die: BG-MS-15. He rubbed the burr flat.  

“Cost?” she asked.  

“Heat bite on palm. Forearm steady. No ring,” he said.  

She held her thumb over the spot till the heat eased. “Clinic if it blisters.”  

He nodded.  

Chloen had the seal box open already. Tiffani pressed the blue circle onto the slip, counted to three, checked edges, pressed once more to catch a dry corner. She clipped it under the droplet. The new circle dried while the river kept breathing.  

Mel Quarr stood without dusting her knees. “Gate’s sound. You’ll hear the difference in the next lift.”  

Tiffani nodded. “We’ll log the pitch.”  

Mel signed the docket and went back up.  

“Open to half for sound,” Tiffani said.  

The winch bar turned. Chain came live. The gate lifted. Water found the new seam and stayed silent. He could feel the difference through the soles of his boots: the gate’s hum had dropped half a note.  

Brighlee wrote that down too; she kept every sound.  

The first cart in line had started its horses before the rope even lifted; they knew the rhythm. The wheel passed the mid-span joint with no jolt. The driver glanced down out of habit, then away—no squeal meant no story to tell later.  

Jhace cleaned the tools, stacking them by touch and weight. Tiffani sealed the Veil case. Chloen wiped the copper wash from her glove before it could harden. Brighlee folded the ledger closed, thumb between pages to keep it breathing until dry.  

“Season mark?” she asked.  

Tiffani looked at the seal. “Dry season two, blackstone arc.”  

Brighlee wrote it at the bottom of the page.  

They stood a count while river light moved over the new metal. The seam sat even; the copper wash threw back dull reflection; the line held.  

Work complete, not fixed—fixed meant it could fail again. This one was joined.  

---

Arc and pressure tested to half and full lift; float and plumb confirm.
