# Chapter 13: Mid-Span Seal

Bell 15 carried thick through the arch, a weight in the air that made iron talk before hands touched it. Water at mid-span licked the inner face of the left leaf in a pattern you don’t want twice: soft, then sharp, like breath through a split tooth. Jhace slid onto the center platform, chest to stone, cheek near plate, Lume under the lip. Light ran along the seam and came back silver where it should have read black. The lower edge of the old Veil had lifted half a thumb from the plate; water wrote itself under that corner and spat small bubbles in rows.

“Not surface,” he said.

“Mid-seal creep,” Tiffani answered. She had the Veil case already open, Shade flats stacked like window glass. “Two flats, Shade 2. We close it before it gets appetite.”

Brighlee clipped a fresh slip under the droplet icon and read so the rope would believe the pause. “Mid-span seal, inner face, no HALT. Witness to be fetched.” The carts took one breath together and stayed put. Paper moves bodies.

“Lift one finger,” Tiffani called.

Brighlee relayed. Chain took weight. The leaf rose just enough to lift the seam out of the run. Spray broke into threads and fell. Air in the throat cooled.

“Heat,” Tiffani said.

Jhace pressed the back of his glove to plate. Warm from friction, not hot. “Safe.”

“Cut the lift,” she said.

He slid the short knife under the loosened Veil and drew one clean stroke to free the failed corner. The skin peeled back straight along glue, not through Shade. Honest decay. He set the scrap into Brighlee’s pouch. Ledger would keep even trash when trash mattered.

“Seat,” she said.

He took the fine grit stone and stroked the plate seat until it wore dull, no high shine to betray oil or resin. He wiped dust into the river in one motion so grit wouldn’t read back under the new flat.

“Measure run,” Tiffani said.

“Eight hands from post,” he answered, tapping off the span with knuckles against iron like a mason measuring string. He marked the upstream corner with a shallow V so a future body could find the start in flood light.

She lifted two Shade 2 flats with tweezers, warmed them over the brazier spoon until their skin went from glass to pliant. Steam from the spoon grazed her knuckles. She set the first flat into the seat.

“Pressure,” she said.

Jhace palmed the flat, shoulder low, weight steady. Shade spread and drank the plate’s texture. He held to a count of ten.

“Tap,” she said.

He used the hammer heel, seven strokes along the edge, a breath, seven again. Resin bled at the lip and darkened as it cooled. The joint took.

“Second,” she said.

She overlapped the second flat by a finger. He warmed the joint with breath, then with a kiss of spoon heat before it could scare the plate. “Slake.”

He thumbed the bladder cap, wet the seam with shoulder pressure only, two fingers’ worth. Water turned to steam under his glove and carried heat into the new bond. The bead darkened and leveled. No white.

“Stop nibble,” she said.

He took the trace stick and ran a hairline counter-wet along the upstream edge so water would choose the wall and not the seam if pressure lifted. Not a joint, just a hallway sign.

Brighlee’s pen scratched under the board lip. “Mid-span seal inner face, two Shade 2, Slake run, counter-wet upstream. No HALT. Bell 15.”

“Float,” Tiffani said.

Chloen brought the jar from its felt. Tiffani set it on the sill and sighted the lines to the far pins. The meniscus sat dead on mark. “Plumb,” she said.

Jhace lowered the bob to the pier tick. Centered. “On.”

“Drop one finger,” Tiffani called.

Chain bled slow. Water rose and pressed against the new flats. A bead formed, held, then sat without bubbles. Jhace touched the joint with the back of his knuckle. Cool. Not a lie.

“Edge guard,” she said.

He brushed a thin skin of copper wash across the outer lip, no puddles, just enough to keep air from chewing. The color shifted from bright to coin dull.

“Test line,” Tiffani said.

He took a soot pot from her case, smoked a thin smear on the plate just upstream of the seam, then brought the leaf a whisper toward rest and back. Soot stayed a band; no streaking toward the seam. Flow wanted the line they’d given it.

Boots knocked the steps: Mel Quarr, blue cord wrapped twice so it wouldn’t hunt a gear. “Seal work,” she said, not a question.

“Mid-span inner face,” Tiffani answered. “Old Veil lifted, seat sanded, two Shade 2 flats Slaked, copper edge. Counter-wet upstream. No HALT.”

Mel crouched without drama, pressed thumb to the overlap, then the bead, then the upstream lip. She leaned and listened with her teeth together. “Bond reads even. Joint length?”

“Eight hands from post,” Jhace said.

“Write eight,” Mel told Brighlee, then to Tiffani, “Throw pressure.”

“Lift to shoulder,” Tiffani called.

The winch brought weight. The leaf climbed. Water slashed along the seam and lost interest. Jhace watched the overlap while pressure worked. No lift. No sweat.

“Drop to half,” Mel said.

They eased down. Water returned and pressed. No bubbles. Mel tapped the bead once with a nail. The sound came back short and dull, the way a joint answers when it has become part of plate. “Witness. Post it.”

“Mark,” Tiffani said.

Jhace cut a double line at the upstream corner and pressed his die: BG-MS-15. He rubbed the burr off so it wouldn’t score a curious hand.

“Slake again at edge,” she said.

He ran one more wet along the outer lip so the copper skin would marry down into any hair that might have stood when they warmed the joint. Steam rose in a clean sheet and vanished.

“Cost?” she asked.

“Palm heat, forearm steady, ear quiet,” he said.

She set her thumb over the hot spot and held until the skin stopped arguing. “Clinic if it blisters.”

He nodded. The buzz that liked to try his pinky stayed away.

“Test under wheel,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee lifted her voice so the rope heard the rule. “One cart over, north line, slow.” The lead teamster leaned. Iron rim rolled the seam. Plate gave back nothing but the day’s hum. No jog. No cry. Water at the joint kept quiet.

“Second,” she said.

The next rim crossed at the same pace. Jhace touched the seam again. Cool. The bead stayed a shade darker than plate, even all along.

“Record pitch,” Tiffani told Brighlee.

“Gate hum down half-note after seal,” Brighlee said while writing. She didn’t guess. She wrote what her ear held.

“Warm the joint skin,” Tiffani said.

He moved the spoon near, not at, tracing heat along the overlap so the resin’s face would temper to the day. Air around his glove smelled like light oil, not hot glue. He pulled the heat away before plate could drink it.

“Countermark on stone,” she said, rare for a seal job, but they’d had a spring freshet tear a pretty seam once because a future hand didn’t know where the last hand stopped.

Jhace chiseled a thin tick into the pier mortar line to match the upstream double he’d scribed on plate. Small, shallow, enough to show line to anyone who needed it under flood lamp. He brushed the dust away with the back of his hand.

Chloen, finally remembering to breathe, hovered with the seal box open. Tiffani pressed the Conclave blue into the slip, held it until the circle grabbed, lifted, checked edges, pressed once along the dry corner, then clipped it under the droplet icon. Blue set where public could see.

“Rise and drop full once,” Mel said, still crouched like this was her gate and not the world’s.

They took it to shoulder, held, dropped to rest. The seam kept its silence. Soot band stayed true. Copper edge dulled another shade.

“Float,” Tiffani said.

Chloen set the jar. Tiffani sighted. Line on pins. She rolled her neck once and stripped the tension from her shoulders by a hair. That moved something in Jhace’s spine he hadn’t asked permission for. His hand stopped prepping for tremor.

“Log gear side run,” Tiffani added, eyes on the seam but brain across the arch like always.

Jhace laid fingers against the chain guide and read the beat. Tooth-count even. Link 21 on right run kept its honest dull from the last rotation. Stop copper at the top carried warm, not hot. Hinge seat from Bell 7 gave nothing away. “Whole line honest,” he said.

“Write follow-up: listen next melt,” Brighlee muttered to herself while boxing the note. She drew a small square in the margin.

Mel Quarr stood and dusted her palms on air. “You’ll know if it lifts. The river tells on seams.” She gave Tiffani the tiniest nod that carried more weight than sentences and climbed the steps, leaving clean wet ovals that the draft erased.

“Edge finger,” Tiffani said.

Jhace pressed one finger across metal to check for raised lip. Flat. He rapped his nail once along the overlap and heard plate, not flap.

The rope dropped. The first waiting cart rolled, wheel iron passing the joint without a sound. A boy in a cap too big leaned, caught by the copper glint and the way nothing happened there. His father’s hand found his collar and brought him back a step. He kept his eyes on the dull line like a person reading a number he wanted to keep.

“Cost,” Tiffani said again, wanting a second read now that wheel had tried their work.

“Palm warm, no split. Thirst,” Jhace said.

She passed the bottle. The water tasted faintly of glass, Veld habit. He drank enough to move heat off his tongue. She took the bottle back and clicked its cap by feel.

“Write the board clean,” she told Brighlee.

Brighlee’s hand stayed square. “Mid-span seal inner face renewed. Two Shade 2 flats Slaked. Copper edge. Counter-wet upstream. Eight-hand run from post. No HALT. Witness: Setter Quarr. Bell 15.” She read it low as she wrote, a worker’s spell.

“Docket copy,” Tiffani said.

Chloen had it ready. Tiffani impressed the second blue, folded the strip, and handed it to the runner who had already appeared the way runners do, like they step out of air.

They cleaned down: soot pot capped, spoon hissed out in the bucket, Lume cooled, Shade wrappers stacked flat, knife wiped, grit stone rinsed, trace stick recapped. Jhace slid each piece into its pocket by weight without looking. Tiffani checked nothing because she trusted hands that checked themselves.

“Walk the seam,” she said.

They went heel to toe along the inner face, eyes low, hands light. The bead lay one shade darker than plate all along; no fish-eye; no starved patch. Copper kissed the edge thin and even. Soot band stable. Upstream counter-wet still a hairline, not a burn. The double scribe at the corner caught light. The pier tick answered it.

They stopped where the run ended. Tiffani pressed her palm once to the plate as if to say this is where it holds. Jhace did the same because some jobs asked you to put skin on proof. The metal read cool. The river pressed its weight on the other side and found the same answer everywhere: no.

They stepped back and let the day move.

Arc and pressure tested at half and shoulder; float on pins; plumb on tick; trial under wheel without lift or change in pitch.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).

# Chapter 14: East Counterweight Line

Bell 16 thudded through stone so deep it shook dust from the rivet heads. The east counterweight house breathed uneven, ropes twitching against pulleys. Jhace leaned over the housing lip and felt it in his wrist—the line wasn’t lying still. Each pull and settle sent a fine tremor through the mount bolts, too steady to be panic, too rough to ignore.

“Counterweight’s arguing its bed,” he said.

Tiffani’s coat brushed the housing as she stepped up beside him. “Line rub. Likely top sheave mis-seated. We reline before it turns to cut.”

Brighlee had already drawn her pen, the act automatic as breathing. “East counterweight line inspection, no HALT. Witness to be fetched.” She clipped the notice to the board, making the rule visible before anyone could try their luck at guessing.

“Hold weight at half,” Tiffani said.

The crew at the bar eased chain. The counterweight groaned, cables flexing with a long sigh. Jhace stepped into the pit ladder, boots clanging against the rung steel. He dropped to the catwalk, heat thick enough to taste like coins. The smell was dry metal and rope tar. He followed the line to the pulley crown.

“Top sheave off true by a thumb,” he said, looking up. The groove carried a light scar, bright under grime. “Rope’s riding one edge.”

Tiffani knelt above. “Loosen top pin. Shift west. Count slow.”

He braced his shoulder against the frame and called up each count as he turned the wrench. “One. Two. Three.” The sheave’s outer rim clicked back into line. He looked again: rope seated, scar no longer cutting light. “Stop. That’s home.”

“Test spin,” she said.

He tapped the rim with his mallet, and the sheave rolled half a turn, rope tracking smooth. The sound changed from scrape to hum. He grinned—small, private, like hearing a lie corrected.

“Grease crown,” Tiffani said.

He dipped a brush in thick black oil and ran it along the groove, one clean stripe. The brush left a dark, even sheen. He rotated the sheave again. The line carried oil evenly, no clumps, no starvation. “Line dressed.”

“Set bottom guide,” she said.

He walked to the lower bearing, checked the guide wheel. Dust had packed in the housing throat. He scooped it out with two fingers, gray and dry. “Guide clear,” he said.

“Counterweight move?” Tiffani asked.

“Still on half, waiting for you,” Brighlee called from the post.

“Run to full lift,” Tiffani said.

Jhace stayed on the catwalk as the counterweight rose in its track. The line sang an honest note, low and clean. The new grease ribbon left no spatter. He looked up and watched the sheave spin at speed—centered now, rim blur even.

“Stop there,” Tiffani said. “Drop half.”

The sheave slowed. Rope read its groove like it remembered how. When it stopped, the hum hung in the air a second before dying. He tapped the frame; no echo. “Line settled,” he said.

Mel Quarr came down the stair, coat sleeves rolled, face already damp. “You lifted the weight?”  

“Relined top sheave,” Tiffani said. “Shifted one thumb west. Groove greased, bottom guide cleared. No HALT.”

Mel put her hand to the rope’s crown where it crossed the bearing lip. “Seated true,” she said. “Test under motion.”

“Raise full,” Tiffani said.

They took it to full rise. Rope tightened, pulleys spun, and when it hit stop, it hit it clean. No clatter, no pulse rebound. Mel listened with her teeth on edge, then nodded. “Witness. Note shift.”

Brighlee wrote it down. “Top sheave moved one thumb west to correct rope alignment. Groove greased. Bottom guide cleared. No HALT. Witness Quarr. Bell 16.”

Jhace climbed the ladder, arms heavy from holding his own weight against the frame. He wiped his gloves on the rag tied to his belt. “Thread wear at third wrap, minor. Marking for next rotation.”

“Mark it,” Tiffani said.

He took his scribe and drew a short tick into the rope’s iron collar. “Third wrap watch,” he said, voice tight. Sweat dripped off his chin and landed on metal, hissing faintly.

“Float?” she asked.

Brighlee leaned over the sill jar. “Line stable. Plumb steady.”

“Run chain two fingers,” Tiffani said. “We prove it.”

The winch took weight. The gate leaf lifted with its partner, counterweight answering smooth. Jhace felt the vibration through the floor: all gears singing the same pitch again. The air in the pit cooled as if even the heat was finished arguing.

“Log tension,” Brighlee said to herself, writing. “Normal, half under Bell 16.” She pressed the ink blot off with her sleeve.

“Cost?” Tiffani asked.

“Forearm sting, shoulder strain. No ring,” Jhace said.

She pressed her knuckles once to his wrist, grounding him. The tremor stayed quiet. “Clinic if the sting stays through Bell 18.”

“Policy met,” he said.

Mel stayed long enough to hear the chain take its full load one more time. “Clean,” she said. “Post it.”

Brighlee clipped the slip under the droplet icon and pressed the blue ring flat with her thumb. The circle took fast. She held until the pigment skinned, then released. “Law set.”

Tiffani looked at the housing once more. “Counterweight balanced. We keep that for next thaw.”

Jhace nodded. “She’s breathing even again.”

They stowed gear: brush capped, oil tin shut, wrench hung. The air already felt lighter, a gate exhaling after days of uneven labor. The queue beyond the arch picked up its voice again: wheels clattering, chains ringing faint. Life returned on rhythm.

He stood beside her on the lip. The rope below glimmered, one long dark line catching lamp light like a line drawn through water.

“You hear that?” he said.

She tilted her head. “The right kind of silence.”

He smiled despite himself. “That’ll do.”

They walked the catwalk once more, counted bolts, read their marks, left nothing speaking but the gate.

# Chapter 14: East Counterweight Line  

Bell 16 came through the Conclave glass thick as syrup, not the quick ring of a clear day. Heat hung in the air, pressed flat against the walls of the east counterweight pit. The gate’s breathing had gone uneven since morning—each rise drew a grind from the pulleys like a cough being swallowed. Jhace crouched at the pit lip, one glove braced on stone, head tilted to listen past the noise of the river. The vibration in the mount bolts told him the story before the light did: the top sheave was walking west.  

He called up. “Line rub on east crown. Off by a thumb, maybe more.”  

Tiffani stepped up beside him, veil strap caught in her shoulder seam. “Counterweight’s fighting its bed. We reline before it decides to cut its own path.”  

Brighlee had already pulled the proper form from her ledger. She read aloud, steady as always. “Maintenance: East counterweight line, upper sheave and guide, no HALT. Witness to be fetched.” She clipped the slip beneath the droplet mark, blue cord swinging once in the draft. The queue at the far side stopped without argument. A posted word outranks a man’s voice here.  

“Hold at half,” Tiffani said.  

Jhace gripped the ladder’s cold rung and descended. Each step hummed through his boot soles. The air in the pit smelled like burnt rope tar and hot oil—the kind of smell that lives in your lungs a full bell after you leave it. He dropped to the catwalk grating, crouched low, and followed the line of the main rope upward to the sheave crown. A halo of gray dust clung to its rim, the usual confession of friction gone wrong.  

“Top sheave’s wearing one cheek,” he said. “Rope riding the lip, scar bright.”  

Tiffani leaned over from above, Lume rod in hand, light raking across the groove. “One thumb west. Maybe less. Pin looks cold. Loosen and set.”  

He took the wrench from his belt, felt the bolt head with fingertips until he found where metal had begun to burr. He braced his boot in the mount and turned. The bolt moaned once, then moved. “One. Two. Three,” he counted, shoulder against frame for leverage. Each turn carried a note, the kind that tells you when strain gives up and alignment begins.  

The sheave shifted west by half an inch, slow but obedient. Dust rained off the rim. Jhace tightened the pin again, checked the gap with a finger-width gauge, and tapped the frame. The vibration ran true. “Set,” he called.  

“Test rotation,” Tiffani said.  

He tapped the rim with the mallet. The sheave rolled a quarter turn. Rope glided across its seat smooth as a line drawn with a single stroke. The scrape was gone. The air changed tone—no longer restless, just heavy.  

“Grease the crown,” she said.  

He opened the oil tin, dipped the stiff brush, and laid a slow dark ribbon along the groove. Graphite caught the Lume light and turned silver. He rotated the sheave another half turn, spreading the grease in a perfect helix. “Crown dressed,” he said.  

“Guide wheel,” Tiffani said.  

He ducked under the crossbeam and crawled to the bottom guide. The wheel throat was clogged with gray dust mixed with oil, compacted into a crust. He broke it loose with a pry hook, then ran a rag through the gap until clean metal glared back. “Guide cleared.”  

Brighlee’s voice floated down the shaft. “Line reads slack two fingers. Do you want the half tension held?”  

“Hold,” Tiffani called. “Test when we lift.”  

Jhace climbed two rungs up to watch the rope at eye level. The counterweight hung ten feet above, each iron slab sweating condensation. Water ran down its flanks like veins in old stone.  

“Top groove’s clean,” he said. “No bite on edge.”  

Tiffani came down two steps into the pit, Lume rod tucked behind her arm. She scanned the rope from crown to guide. “Lift to full.”  

Brighlee signaled. Chain moved. Rope took weight. The sheave spun in a perfect blur, oil film flashing. The counterweight rose slow and deliberate, clearing each brace notch until it touched its stop. The vibration under Jhace’s boots steadied into a low hum that felt like breath held steady.  

“Stop there,” she said. “Drop to half.”  

The bar reversed. The sheave slowed, whispering instead of grinding. When it stopped, the rope stood still in its groove—no sway, no chatter.  

“Guide wheel temperature,” Tiffani said.  

Jhace pressed his knuckles to the housing. “Warm, not hot.”  

“Line tension?”  

He set the gauge hook into the rope and read the dial. “Two fingers under spec, within float.”  

“Good.”  

Boots struck metal on the stair. Mel Quarr appeared, hair damp with heat, blue cord wound twice around her wrist so it wouldn’t find a wheel. “Counterweight work?”  

Tiffani looked up from her notes. “East line realigned. Sheave shifted west a thumb. Groove greased, guide cleared. No HALT.”  

Mel leaned on the railing, eyes following the rope up through shadow. “That scrape from this morning was yours?”  

“Was,” Jhace said. “Not anymore.”  

Mel smiled with the corner of her mouth, not much. “Test full lift. I want to hear it silent.”  

Brighlee relayed the signal. The crew drove the winch. Chain answered. The counterweight rose, then stopped dead on the top lock. Rope thrummed once and settled. The sound in the pit was a heartbeat—just one, then nothing. Mel closed her eyes for the briefest second, listening with bone.  

“Drop,” she said.  

They lowered the weight. It kissed the bed with a thud and went still. Mel crouched, touched the rope, and rubbed her thumb and forefinger together. The skin came away dark with clean grease. “Witness,” she said. “Post that line. Note shift.”  

Brighlee’s pen scratched. “East counterweight line: top sheave adjusted west by one thumb, groove greased, guide cleared, tested full lift and drop, float and plumb steady. No HALT. Witness Quarr. Bell 16.”  

Tiffani nodded to Jhace. “Mark collar.”  

He took the scribe from his belt and cut a single arrow on the iron collar pointing west, then pressed his die: BG-ECL-16. He filed the burr flush.  

“Set float,” she said.  

Brighlee brought the jar forward. The meniscus sat low by a hair; Tiffani tapped the float rod once with her nail. The line rose to the ink tick. “Plumb?”  

Jhace checked. “Dead center.”  

“Write that,” she said.  

Brighlee noted it. “Float corrected to tick. Plumb centered. No deviation.”  

The air in the pit felt lighter now, as if pressure had moved off everyone’s shoulders at once. Jhace leaned back against the beam, breathing through his teeth. “Weight’s not breathing uneven anymore.”  

Tiffani rubbed her temple with her knuckle. “Keep it that way. Schedule next lubrication at melt minus one bell.”  

“Logged,” Brighlee said.  

Mel straightened, eyes on the rope again. “You’ll have to flip that line next season before the fibers begin to fray. Inner core’s tired.”  

“Logged,” Brighlee echoed.  

“Cost?” Tiffani asked.  

Jhace flexed his hand. “Forearm burn, no split. Ear hum from the sheave. Gone already.”  

She touched his wrist, firm and quick. The skin was slick with oil, but her thumb read what she needed. “Clinic if the hum comes back.”  

“Policy met.”  

Mel turned for the stair. “Good line. You can run her at dawn without shame.”  

“Witness thanked,” Tiffani said, which was as close as anyone came to courtesy down here.  

They packed up methodically. Jhace wiped each tool with the same rag until his hands came up dull gray. Tiffani coiled the spare rope tail and hung it on the wall peg. Brighlee capped her pen, shook ink into the river through the drainage slit where it belonged. Chloen rewrapped the blue seal cord, palms raw.  

When they came back into daylight, Bell 17 hadn’t struck yet, but the sun off the water carried heat sharp enough to sting. The Posting board held the new slip beneath the droplet, blue seal drying even. The carts had started to talk again—wood creaking, horses blowing.  

Jhace wiped sweat from his nose with the back of his wrist. “When she’s right, you can hear it in the stone.”  

Tiffani tilted her head. “Quiet, not silence. Silence lies.”  

He almost smiled. “Quiet’s what I meant.”  

“Then write it right,” she said, but her voice was softer.  

They walked the east wall length, checking each bolt head by hand. Every mark lined up with the frame. Each spoke of labor already done, weight borne and still holding. At the far end, the counterweight rope glimmered where oil caught the low light, a single dark vein running up into shadow, steady, alive.

# Chapter 15: Holdfast Clearance  

Bell 17 tolled uneven—half a strike too slow at the top, quick at the tail. The day before thaw smelled of wet dust and burnt grease. At the south abutment, where the holdfast plates bit into the stone apron, something had gone slack. The plates no longer held their seam flush to the pier. When carts stopped, the bridge whispered instead of resting.

Jhace was already crouched with his thumb in the gap before the bell stopped. The air leaking from under the plate felt warmer than it should; friction under strain. “Holdfast drift,” he said. “Top plate walking north by a hair.”

Tiffani knelt beside him, veil case open, Lume rod balanced along her wrist like a level. “That hair turns into a finger by next melt. We pin it.”

“Pin,” he echoed.

Brighlee read from her ledger so the queue heard what they needed to hear before they guessed wrong. “Holdfast clearance and re-seat, south abutment, no HALT. Witness to be fetched.” She clipped the slip under the droplet and looked up until the first driver nodded. Carts settled back, iron on stone.

“Block the hinge,” Tiffani said.

Jhace shoved the stop bar under the right leaf, wood biting stone until it sang the low work note. “Set.”

“Chain half slack,” she called.

Brighlee relayed. Weight eased. The holdfast plate breathed—a dull flex like a lung too tired to hide it.

“Heat?” she asked.

Jhace pressed his glove back to the seam. “Warm, not hot.”

“Lift corner.”

He worked the wedge in under the plate edge and pried until light cut a hairline under the seam. Dust dropped through. The plate had pulled against its south pin, leaving the north pin proud by two fingers.

“See that?” he said.

Tiffani leaned close. “Pin drifted. Collar thread stripped half a turn.”

“Pull or rethread?”

“Pull. Don’t trust chewed thread.”

Jhace took the hammer and drift, squared it to the pin head, and gave it three measured taps. Metal tone dull, not angry. The pin walked clean. He caught it before it could roll, turned it in his palm. The upper thread showed gray bruising where torque had eaten through plating.

“Mark it,” Tiffani said.

He scribed BG-HF-17 along the shank, chalked the head bright, and set it on the mat. She passed him the thread chaser and oil cloth. He ran the chaser through the collar bore—one full in, half back, clear—until it came out clean and silver. He checked seat depth with the pin gauge. True.

“Clean bore,” he said.

“Oil thin,” she said.

He brushed a film along the threads, then rolled the replacement pin across his thigh to warm it before seating. “Aligned.”

“Set,” she said.

He slid it in, turned it by hand until shoulder met collar, then took the wrench for the last quarter. The metal squeaked once and settled. He tested with two fingers on the head. “Tight.”

“Seat the south pin,” she said.

He checked the twin—solid, no drift, but dust buildup around the base. He scraped it clean with the tip of the wedge and brushed oil where air had gotten in. He ran a fingernail along the seam between plate and pier. Smooth.

“Chain to quarter,” she called.

The winch crew brought weight back. The holdfast plate pressed down into its seat. The seam closed like breath taken back. Jhace pressed his palm to the face. No air under it. Heat spreading even, not patchy.

“Flush,” he said.

Tiffani set the Lume to low and sighted along the edge. “Line true.”

Brighlee’s pen scratched. “Holdfast plate reseated, north pin replaced, south cleaned, bore chased, line true, no HALT.”

Mel Quarr came down the steps, coat unbuttoned, blue cord hanging loose—she’d run from another inspection. “You’re on the south?”

“North pin stripped. Replaced and chased,” Tiffani said. “Seal reseated. Chain at quarter.”

Mel knelt, wiped dust from the collar with her thumb, looked at the scratch marks on the old pin. “Thread bruised from torque. Likely overpull on last test.”

“We were under time that bell,” Jhace said quietly.

“Always are,” Mel said. “Doesn’t make the thread less broken.” She pressed her palm flat to the plate, reading it the way they all did. “Witness. Test rise.”

“Half,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee signaled. Chain came live. The plate stayed flat. No lift, no chatter. The air shifted back toward clean.

“Drop to rest,” Tiffani said.

The plate settled. The low hum underfoot faded into stone. Mel smiled—not kindly, not unkindly, just sure. “Post it.”

Brighlee read as she wrote. “Holdfast clearance, south abutment. North pin replaced, collar chased, south cleaned. Tested at quarter and half. Float and plumb confirm. No HALT. Witness Quarr. Bell 17.” She clipped it under the droplet and pressed the blue seal flat.

Tiffani stood, rubbing the back of her wrist. “Cost?”

“Knuckle split from drift,” Jhace said. “Forearm fine. Ear quiet.”

She tore a strip from the linen roll and wrapped his knuckle once, neat. “Clinic if it tears before night.”

“Policy met.”

Chloen was already boxing the old pin. “Ledger wants the serial,” she said.

“BG-HF-17-A, retired,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee copied it. The air smelled faintly of the oil Jhace had brushed along the seam, clean as rain on old metal.

“Rise test full,” Mel said.

They brought the chain to full lift. The gate rose even. The new pin held. The seam stayed dark, no silver sliver between plate and pier. Jhace laid his fingers on the head—cool, vibration gone. When they dropped the leaf, water slapped the apron and quieted.

Mel tapped the plate once with her boot toe. “Good hold. Give it the day. If it hums at dusk, turn it half.”

“Logged,” Tiffani said.

Mel took the old pin, turned it once in her hand, and set it on the step beside the board. “Let them see what they nearly rode over,” she said, then went up, cord swaying.

The first teamster at the rope shifted his grip. Brighlee lifted her voice. “Passage open. No HALT.” The line moved again, wheels groaning, horses pulling slow over stone.

Jhace packed his tools one by one: drift, wedge, wrench, chaser. He cleaned the plate face with his rag and looked down the seam. The joint had gone from gleam to matte gray—the right color for work that intends to last.

Tiffani took one last sight with the Lume, extinguished it, and latched the case. “South side done.”

“Until thaw,” he said.

“Until thaw,” she echoed.

They climbed the stairs together, Brighlee already writing the closing line in the ledger, her handwriting as narrow as the gaps she lived by. Chloen lagged behind, holding the blue seal box like something alive.

At the top of the steps, Jhace turned back once. Sunlight from the east slit ran down the wall and landed square on the reseated holdfast plate. The oil on the seam caught it and threw a thin reflection across the stone lip—a sign, not a flare, enough for the next hand to know where the weight lies right.

---

**Public artifact:**  
**BLACKGATE HOLDFAST CLEARANCE — Bell 17**  
South abutment holdfast plate reseated. North pin removed (thread stripped), bore chased, replacement pin installed and torqued; south pin cleaned and oiled.  
Plate tested under quarter, half, and full rise; seam flush; float and plumb confirmed.  
No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface).  
Retired component: BG-HF-17-A, catalogued.  
Follow-up: inspect threads at next thaw.  
Docket entry filed at Couriers’ Bell.

**Concrete image:** the south holdfast plate pressed tight to stone, oil line dull gray, one new pin head bright against two old, sunlight drawing a single fine stripe across it while the gate stands quiet and the river breathes below.

# What’s broken (and why I’m annoyed)

1. **The prologue slipped into academic voice.**
   You drifted from tactile, in-world ledger to sterile report-writing. Whole sections read like a municipal whitepaper: “We designate that fissure the Bore.” No one living with a world-hole talks like a transport commission. Tone mismatch. 

2. **Banned music words are back.**
   Chapter 1: “**Humming, low and even.**” You outlawed the entire music field. Replace with *steady/quiet/settled/level*. 

3. **Inventory intros still crept in.**
   “Veil case open on her knee. Lume rod steady in her grip. Weld cloth over her hair.” That’s a shopping list pretending to be prose. Make the intro an action. 

4. **Ledger language is bleeding into prose again.**
   You end sections with **“Public artifact:”** and **“Concrete image:”** inside the narrative block. Those must live in the private continuity notes, not on-stage. 

5. **Aphorism creep.**
   “Paper had a way of shifting the world.” That’s a poster. Let the carts easing be the proof. Cut the bumper sticker. 

6. **Brighlee ping-pongs between character and stapler.**
   Great entrance (“breathing paper”), then she turns into a filing macro. Either keep her purely functional, or give her one human beat and stop there. 

# Surgical fixes you can paste today

## A) Prologue: tighten and re-tone (drop-in replacement)

Replace your current prologue with this. It keeps your facts, kills the municipal drone, and lands on posted rules without sermonizing.

> **Prologue — The Year the World Took a Breath**
> The world ended once and decided against it.
> It didn’t burst. It learned to sway.
> A pull went through rock and water like a straightened arm and stopped shy, leaving a tunnel that never reached the far side. We call it the **Bore**. In the Underface, kids grow up with a dark ring on the horizon that isn’t cloud. In the Crown, mapmakers draw a neat circle and write, *Here the world held itself.*
> Oceans miscounted and took two steps in, one back. Rivers found new corners. Cliffs leaned. Some shelves of stone slid a thumb-width and stayed. Some didn’t. For a year and a half the air tasted of iron. Grain settled different and the jars learned to tell on it.
> People argued causes. Some said four Principles pulled apart. Some said banners get sewn after the march. Sermons named an opposing spark that paid in full. The auditors left a single line: *Load took the short path until it didn’t, and in that pause we learned how to live.*
> What stayed: everything carries **Veins** where force prefers to run. Veins fill with **Threft**. When they slip their lanes in a hurry, **Harrow** happens. Hands can help: **Trace, Set, Bridge, Veil, Slake, Tempermark**. We learned by breaking, then not finishing the break.
> After the Bore, streets woke warm from below. No crew remembers laying the mains. The **Thermal** work is there, quiet, gated by keys nobody has. It keeps clinics from frosting and makes winter basins steam. We don’t write poems about it. We write **NO ACCESS** big enough to shame greed.
> Bells multiplied. Not prayers. Work. Long clear for reopen. Two short for brace. Fast beats for Harrow risk. We Tempermarked boards so rain can’t warp them, and we hung them where eyes are busiest: spans, clinic doors, market corners. First rule posted was stupid and perfect: **One hand on the home ring.** Keep a grip on where you stand while you touch what might move.
> The good pages that survived read dry and save lives:
> — Trace wet. Dry lines please eyes and lie to Veins.
> — Wedge before veil. Cool the draft, then the glass.
> — Half-beat Slake.
> — Countermark cooler than birth. Pride bends things wrong.
> — No White.
> — Brace taps only in Bells Two and Three. You are not special in Four.
> — If it isn’t on the board, it didn’t happen.
> Watch a street when a blue **HALT** shows at the edge of sight and the tools go quiet until the fix is real. Watch the setter witness the clear and the stamp bite flat. That’s as close to thunder as a city needs.
> We forbid **Overbind** because clean disasters are still disasters. We forbid **Whitequench** because lying glass turns to knives. Crowds are materials; you can press them, cool them, line them up so their kids get home. They’ll grumble, then bring you something hot and sweet, and you’ll log the jar so kindness doesn’t get stolen by memory.
> Six hundred and twelve rounds later, Te’Oga still lists a little. Maps carry revision marks like scars. In the Underface, the horizon keeps its ring. In the Crown, edges cut light too sharp. No romance. No speeches. Work.
> If you insist on a prayer, pick one you can perform with your hands:
> **Trace wet.** **Wedge before veil.** **Slake on the half-beat.** **Tempermark what you promise.** **Post it.**
> The world isn’t healed. It’s **held**. That’s enough for a day.

Use this and move on. Don’t overfiddle it.

Drop these into the system prompt for the new model:

* **Hard bans in prose:** inventory intros; thesis lines; ledger headings; music terms {sing, hum, hymn, chorus, choir}.
* **Contract:** opening ≤ 250 words to first operation; wrap ≤ 200; end on artifact + image.
* **Consent template:** “Stay,” he said. Her hand warmed his sleeve; his tremor stopped. No commentary about consent.
* **Brighlee lane:** function only or one human beat, not both.
* **Self-scrub:** delete any sentence beginning with “because/that’s why/in other words,” or any sentence that explains a ritual instead of showing its effect.

If the model violates any of the above, it regenerates the paragraph until it passes. No exceptions.

#  **Grindheim**

## **Prologue: The Year the World Took a Breath**

The world ended once and reversed course.

It did not fracture. It adapted.

The event began without celestial fire or assignable fault. A tension traversed matter—rock, water, mountain mass—along a defined vector that arrested short of completion. Te’Oga rent open across one hemisphere, excavating a tunnel equivalent to a city embedded within another, a fissure that terminates without counterpart. We designate that fissure the Bore. Children in the Underface orient to a dark ring on the horizon, neither cloud nor solar void. Cartographers in the Crown inscribe a precise annulus and annotate: Here the world registers restraint.

On the day of the Bore, oceans recalibrated volume and advanced two measures inland, receding one. Rivers acquired acute bends. Cliffs acquired inclination. Stone ledges displaced one thumb-width and stabilized; others progressed. Municipal foundations, previously mute, transmitted signals. Air acquired iron content for eighteen months. Grain in storage altered settling patterns; containers registered discrepancies.

Debate persists on origins. Steelfolk attribute discord among the Four Principles—Xilcore for Form, Blemo for Purity, Leesa for Wholeness, Seeri for Decay—with the world partitioning along those axes. Skeptics classify such attributions as retrospective flags. Sermons posit an opposing ignition that compensated in totality. Auditors, preferring records to narrative, preserved one entry:

Load pursued the optimal vector until it deviated, and in that interval we acquired methodology.

Persistence defines outcome.

Every material—stone, beam, glass, assembly, compact—channels force through Veins. These Veins accumulate Threft under pressure, analogous to lung capacity or cord memory under strain. Vein deviation under acceleration induces Harrow and mortality. Human intervention—via Trace to delineate, Set to moderate, Bridge to distribute, Veil to dissipate, Slake to equilibrate, Tempermark to certify—mitigates. Acquisition occurred through partial rupture and subsequent restraint.

Post-Bore, subsurface warmth emerged in thoroughfares. Origin undocumented. Thermal Mains manifested as conduits without provenance, valves operable only by absent keys. They operate without surge. They sustain. They preclude clinic frost and generate midwinter steam in basins. Inebriates report pulses in conduits on proximity. Documentation excludes verse. Documentation enforces exclusion: no ingress, inscribed in capitals to deter avarice.

Bells proliferated. Operational signals: sustained tone for clearance, two shorts for reinforcement, rapid sequence for Harrow exposure. Boards received Tempermarks against moisture distortion. Boards positioned at focal points: spans, clinic thresholds, exchange perimeters. Initial posting: Anchor one hand to the home ring. Contact the posterior tie. Orient position amid potential displacement.

Select texts endured. Inferior variants propagate heroism and inaccuracy. Superior variants emulate ledgers and induce aridity. Exemplary entries:

* 	  
  Trace wet. Dry lines satisfy optics and 	deceive Veins.  
* 	  
  Wedge before veil. Stabilize draft, then 	stabilize glass.  
* 	  
  Half-beat Slake.  
* 	  
  Countermark below ambient. Arrogance induces 	deflection.  
* 	  
  No White.  
* 	  
  Brace taps restricted to Bells Two and Three. 	Exclusion in Four. Exemption denied in Four.  
* 	  
  Board entry required for validation.

The final stipulation preserved more lives than invocation. Paper confers no enchantment; it facilitates consensus. A tactile icon incurs lower cost than dispute. In operational municipalities, observe assembly response to peripheral HALT inscription: implements cease until remediation verifies. Observe resumption on Setter attestation and flat stamp impression. This approximates thunder.

Additional precepts: Overbind—forcing disparate Veins into unified exertion—yields predictable catastrophe. Prohibition enforced; rationale withheld from novices to preclude allure. Whitequench—extracting thermal content from glass to compliance—induces mendacity in glass. Prohibition enforced; mendacity escalates to pedestrian hazard. Assemblies constitute materials: compressible, temperable, alignable to trajectories that secure progeny. Assemblies register grievance until preservation registers, then tender distillate—thermal, saccharine—prompting ledger notation to preclude amnesia.

Six hundred twelve cycles post-Bore, planetary inclination persists. Cartography accumulates emendations. Underface horizons retain annular occlusion. Crown towers exhibit edge acuity exceeding entitlement. Romance excluded. Fabrication excluded. Labor prevails.

Assertions circulate: the Four dormant behind veils, outstanding liabilities, counterspark attributable to named individual with documented proximity to peril. Counterassertions: anthropic constructs stabilize unseen gradients. Alignment solicited. Conserve respiration. Select labor.

Secure to home ring. Assess seam. If invocation required, select manual execution:

* 	  
  Trace wet.  
* 	  
  Wedge before veil.  
* 	  
  Slake on half-beat.  
* 	  
  Tempermark commitments.  
* 	  
  Board entry.

The world remains unremedied. The world remains secured. Sufficiency registers per cycle.

## **Chapter 1: Wet Trace at Blackgate**

Bell 2 rang short across the Underface, a curt signal cutting through the hum. Jhace climbed the ladder and stepped into Blackgate’s choke, where carts lined up tight to the right and the Posting board waited left, its Tempermark pin and droplet icon clear as day. Wet work. Stick to the outer rail. No sparks.

Tiffani had already drawn a chalk half-circle behind them to ward off the press of the crowd. Veil case open on her knee. Lume rod steady in her grip. Weld cloth over her hair. She nodded toward the tie-off ring bolted waist-high to the post. “One hand on home.”

“One hand on home,” he repeated, the words settling the body into rhythm.

Brighlee from the Conclave arrived with the faint rustle of paper. “Work order nine-three-two. Wet Trace, Blackgate. Setter: Jhace brast. Glass: Tiffani veld. No HALT.” She Tempermarked the droplet card and clipped it to the board. Icon, Bell number, names. Witnessed. The carts eased forward an inch. Paper had a way of shifting the world.

The Chain’s fault note described a quick Vein under the gate plate, tracing light along a seam and testing for hollows. If it found enough depth, the Harrow could undermine the roadway and spill carts into the Underface. Better to guide it gently—persuasion over force, unless you wanted the mess of a funeral.

Jhace laid out just the essentials: two wedges angled at seven degrees, trace stick with copper nib, bladder secured tight. Wedge-before-veil. Half-beat Slake. Brace taps at Bells 2–3 only. He tested his fray bands and hooked his left hand to the home ring.

Tiffani ran the Lume light along the seam and adjusted the Shade from two to three. A thin glare reflected back, blurred and pulsing quick. She tilted her head, listening. “Quick, but not vicious. It’s after the easy path.”

“Easy path runs under the wheels,” Jhace said. “We steer it longer.”

He drove the first wedge two spans north of the gate’s mouth until it met resistance, then secured it with his thumb. The second went at the shoulder, where the seam curved under the bridge stone. The seam parted slightly; cool air brushed his knuckles.

“Half-beat Slake,” she said.

He uncapped the bladder, dampened the copper, shook off the excess, and pressed the nib to the stone. Wet Trace built slow. Pressure from the shoulder, steady with two fingers. The water steamed under his glove, carrying warmth to the Vein. The quick pulse evened out. The line caught.

“Shade three to four,” Tiffani said, easing the rod to hold the reflection true. “Veil after the turn. No white.”

“No white,” he agreed. Whitequench could deceive and draw trouble. He guided the line through the bend, keeping the wet even, not too lean. A cart axle creaked behind them, then fell silent as the teamster spotted the icon and eased his brake.

The copper caught on a speck of grit. Jhace held his wrist still, let the shoulder guide, took a breath, and corrected on the next stroke. Tiffani tapped his elbow lightly.

“Close one?” she asked.

“Stay with it,” he said. The warmth through his sleeve steadied him; the tremor in his pinky eased.

At the gate mouth, the Vein dipped sharp for the wheel groove. Veins favored edges. Jhace flattened the nib and fanned the wet into a gentle bow across the lip, then resumed the center line. Tiffani tracked the Lume’s response and gave a quick nod.

“Log the wedge set,” she said to Brighlee.

Scratch of quill. The ledger noted it.

Bell 3 rang out, drawing longer. Jhace added a faint counterline along the upper lip to discourage any upward creep—not a full joint or Countermark, just a nudge: flat path, not rise. The air turned to steam and faint metal, clean so far, no rust edge or Hollow’s dull gnaw. Solid.

“Lower lip now,” Tiffani said.

He applied a fuller wet, and the trace shifted left to right, following a subtle curve. The gate seemed to settle. Brake boards groaned behind but stayed firm. The droplet icon on the board held its quiet authority.

“Veil light,” she said, warming a palm-width Shade 1 skin with her breath. She placed it over the bowed line, tapping in sets of seven along the edge. “Annealshift?”

“None,” he said. “Wall handles it.”

“Veil set. Trace holding.” She kept the Lume on the line.

Brighlee handed over the stamp. Jhace pressed it in without pause—Blackgate appreciated the early mark. Setter’s icon into the plate, initials discreet in the corner. For the records, not show.

“Listen,” Tiffani said.

“To what?”

“Humming, low and even.” She held the rod steady. “Field’s calm. Strathmoor hums like that to soothe the restless.”

He finished the line, sealed the wedges with a final damp touch, and turned them a quarter for a natural hold. The Vein’s rhythm under the plate slowed to a steady pulse. Wet and Veil merged smooth.

“Cost check?” she asked.

“Fray bands wearing thin. Ear’s got a faint ring. Thirsty,” he said.

She passed the water bottle. “Clinic if it lingers past 4.”

“Policy.” He took a pull. Veld water carried a hint of glass, clean and sharp.

Brighlee filed the stamped order into the board’s slot under the icon and raised her ledger for the crowd to see. Names, Bell, mark. The teamsters lightened their brakes, awaiting her sign. Paper sparked movement.

Jhace eyed the work. Wet line lay even and matte. Wedges secure. No bubbles lifting the veil. Tiffani dialed the Shade back to two and offered a tight smile. “Blackgate’s cooperative today.”

“It knows when we do it right,” he said.

They released the home ring. Tiffani latched her case. Brighlee moved on, quill already scratching ahead, as if ink could stay one step from breakdown. Now and then, it managed.

The lead cart rolled through, rim brushing a hand’s breadth from the line. It traced the bow without a whine. The teamster looked down, then met Jhace’s eye with a nod: job seen, job good. The Underface stirred slow beneath the stone.

He cleaned the copper, capped the bladder firm, and paused a beat longer. The damp seam gleamed subtly darker than the plate, steam threading up fine. The droplet icon bore a worker’s sweat-stain, deepened by one shade.

Public artifact: droplet card Tempermarked at Blackgate with names and Bell; setter’s stamp embedded in the plate.

Concrete image: one steady bead along the gate face, faint steam curling as the first cart passes smooth.

Continuity Ledger — separate

* 	  
  Posted clauses (current): wet-trace rule; 	wedge-before-veil; half-beat Slake; brace taps (Bells 2–3); one 	hand on home line; setter must witness clear.  
* 	  
  Artifacts: Tempermarked droplet card at 	Blackgate (Bell 2\) with names; setter’s stamp on Blackgate plate 	(Wet Trace \+ initials); work order 932 (copy in board); wedges at 	seam shoulder and north span; palm-width Shade 1 veil laid over 	beaded bow.  
* 	  
  Costs: Jhace: fray band wear; mild ear ring; 	thirst. Tiffani: dry mouth; finger heat. Time: Bell 2 to early Bell 	3\. Coin: ledger to clear later at Mercers’ Table. Clinic: observe 	ring into Bell 4 per policy.

## **Chapter 2: Gate Line at Fell Drop**

Bell 4 drifted lazy and long through the Cut, the tone lingering like a held breath. The air down there carried a perpetual half-wet, half-burnt edge, undecided between damp and scorch. Jhace and Tiffani stepped off the lift with their kits still holding the faint warmth from Blackgate. Fell Drop loomed at the bottom, patient as a scar that hadn’t quite healed.

Two Conclave hands were already on-site, boots planted in the drainage, debating the merits of a bowline over a loop splice. Brighlee waved them quiet and passed Jhace the folded docket. “Line breach under the third runner. Fell Drop register shows last maintenance six months back, two Veinworks prior. Low flux, cold pressure. Not aggressive, just idle. No HALT. No witness yet—Setter en route.”

Idle Veins were trickier than the fractious ones; they didn’t resist so much as stall, building pressure in pockets that released slow and ragged when they did. Jhace unfolded the docket, ran his thumb along the paper’s faint grain beneath the ink. Low-level seep by the notes. Possible drawdown below sump level. “We’ll trace it,” he said.

Tiffani had the runoff gate eased open to knee height already. Water spilled out smooth and slate-grey. She crouched, traced a finger along the edge where rust deepened to black, and tilted her head. “Pitting here—that’s its rest spot. Quick Vein drifted, lingered too long.”

She unrolled her Shade skins across the grating: two palm-widths, Grade 2, matte finish. “We hold it dry. No overflow past the mark till the Setter clears.”

Jhace settled beside her, elbow braced against the wall, and drew a finger through the oily sheen on the surface. Cool to the touch, but alive. “Still active,” he said.

“Not for much longer, if we set it right.” She flicked the Lume open, its blue-white core narrowing to a fine point. “Thread ready.”

He fitted a fresh nib to the copper trace stick, topped the bladder from the reserve. “Pattern?”

“Full loop. The kind that redirects without a fight.”

They set anchor points two spans apart, wedges placed shallow for even tension. Jhace ran the first line left to right, knuckles grazing the chill plate, thumb modulating the pressure for a thin, consistent trace. The Wet Trace hissed softly against the pitted steel, beads forming and chaining like fine droplets on cooling metal.

From the steps above, Brighlee called, “Setter approaching. Keep the work clean.”

“Work stays true,” Tiffani replied.

The gate wall pulsed faintly under his hand as he progressed. At the midpoint, the sound shifted—a subtle lift in tone. Air pocket. He raised the nib and noted it. “Here,” he said.

Tiffani leaned in, hair grazing the surface. “Soft one, mid-left. Mishandle that, and it unravels the face.”

“We won’t.” He shifted his footing, locked his knees, and resumed the half-beat Slake, deliberate now—shoulder guiding the pressure, not the arm. Steam rose in languid curls, tracking the idle seep.

The Setter arrived: tall frame dusted with grit, Conclave tag pinned askew from the rush. “Mel Quarr, witness,” she said, breath steadying. “Carry on.”

“Midline already,” Jhace noted.

“Then I’ll record from this point.” She steadied her ledger against her knee, the page quivering with each sump ripple.

Tiffani signaled, “Shade three.”

Jhace canted his wrist a fraction and deepened the trace. The hiss refined to a steady murmur. “Locked in,” he said.

Mel’s pen hovered. “Flux?”

“Even to the touch. Damp surface, no rebound.”

“Maintain trace. Veil on completion.”

The line sealed clean, gleaming briefly where the wet set, then matte as it cooled. Tiffani rested her palm over the fresh section, eyes half-closed. “Still holding idle,” she murmured. “One more nudge.”

Jhace shifted to the line’s far end, refreshed the nib, and laid in a diagonal crosslink. The response came as a quiet release—an exhale that cleared the pocket. The scent shifted from iron-damp to clean alloy.

“That’s it.”

Tiffani swept her Shade over the new join and secured it with light nail taps. “Annealshift one. Let the residual heat carry through.”

The steam thinned, then dissipated. Silence settled.

Mel scrawled quickly. “Record: Fell Drop gate line, Wet Trace repair under third runner. Cold pressure breach sealed. Witnessed Bell 4, no HALT.” She separated the lower copy and passed it to Brighlee. “Post this.”

Brighlee scaled the ladder and affixed the slip beside the droplet icon. The paper edged crisp in the humidity, curling slightly as if etching the moment.

Tiffani scanned her gauges—backflow minimal, plate cooling steadily, no tremor. “Field steady.”

Jhace rose, easing the tightness in his shoulders from the crouch. He unclipped the trace stick and laid it aside to temper. “No loose ends.”

Mel offered a brief nod. “Clean joints, Brast. Conclave appreciates the precision on file.”

“Files don’t corrode,” he said.

“Nor should the work.”

They stowed their kits methodically. The sump water ran clearer now, ebbing down the channel. Fell Drop eased into quiet, the sluice murmur softening.

Tiffani propped a shoulder against the wall, working the stiffness from her fingers. “Ear ring from before still there?”

“Echo, faint.” He brushed his ear. “Clinic check if it carries past 4.”

“Policy’s there for a reason.” She gave a close-lipped smile.

By the lift, Brighlee was already marking the next docket in chalk. Mel endorsed the upper copy, folded it neatly, and handed it over. “Filed and closed,” she said. “Conclave notes the diligence.”

Diligence was just the job. Clean work didn’t seek acclaim.

The lift bore them upward steadily, Fell Drop receding below, the gate face holding the Lume’s fading glow. A final wisp of steam lingered over the seam, then dissolved into nothing.

Public artifact: Fell Drop gate docket, Conclave-stamped at Bell 4, “Wet Trace repair under third runner, no HALT, witness Mel Quarr.”

Concrete image: faint steam ribbon dissolving over a calm sump while the Conclave seal dries blue on damp paper.

## **Chapter 3: Wind Lean Calibration**

Bell 5 rose thin from the Couriers’ tower, a clear note threading the air. Tiffani placed a glass plumb in Jhace’s palm and nodded toward the right leaf. South air eased through the throat, drawing a soft whisper from the plate. He positioned the plumb at the sill’s midline and allowed the string to align with the pier mark, etched true from last season. The bob met stone, swayed briefly, and held three hairs shy of the line. A lean.

“Gauge wire,” she said.

He stretched a fine wire across to the opposite post and tuned it taut until it hummed evenly, then marked its contact points with chalk. He observed where the leaf’s leading edge intersected the wire as the wind flowed. It shifted right on the gust, drifted left in the calm, as if drawn to trace the south face.

“Hold at half arc,” Tiffani instructed Brighlee.

The Chain took up the weight. The right leaf suspended in the throat. The south breeze built gently, consistent. Tiffani set the float jar on the sill and aligned her sight to the distant pins. The left held steady under her reference line. The right pulsed faintly, a subtle rhythm like warmth rising from heated metal.

“Guide shoe,” she directed.

Jhace knelt at the lower track where the shoe steered the leaf along the stone lip. He raised the guard plate with careful fingers and assessed the shoe’s surface—cool and worn smooth. From his strap, he selected a thin bronze shim, positioned it between shoe and housing, then lowered the guard and secured the two small bolts with the stubby spanner, torquing just enough to hold without strain.

“Clevis,” Tiffani added, gaze fixed on the float. “Upper hanger. Quarter turn, not half.”

He ascended the ladder embedded in the pier—the one that always gathered moisture on its third rung. At the top, he cleared the hanger with his sleeve and applied the spanner to the clevis jam nut. It had seized from use and exposure. He warmed it with his breath, then held a coal from the brazier spoon near the metal until it yielded a soft sigh. He withdrew the heat before the link could overabsorb. The nut loosened an eighth; he advanced another eighth for a total quarter. He reset the jam and verified the cotter pin’s alignment.

Below, the throat wire drifted, then stabilized. Tiffani’s expression tightened briefly. “Recheck.”

He lingered atop long enough to press his thumb to the hanger’s center and note its tone—colder, more even. Descending, he gripped the damp rungs. At the base, he tested the shoe through the guard; the shim bore the load smoothly. Reduced vibration.

Brighlee balanced the ledger under her elbow, the Posting board’s droplet icon still attached from Bell 2\. She observed the wire without committing to paper yet. An entry for assumption alone drew scrutiny at the Warden’s review.

“Test it,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee signaled the winch. The chain bar groaned softly. The right leaf lifted half a finger shy of position, then returned true. The glass float line across the pins held firm and level for three breaths, until the south wind applied a gentle pressure, testing the tilt. The leaf resisted. Its leading edge remained against the gauge wire, contact clean.

“Run through,” Tiffani said.

They cycled to three fingers open and back to half. The float remained steady. The plumb bob swayed once and centered. The wire between posts maintained its even tone. No drift.

Chloen approached with a wooden case no longer than a handspan. “Warden sent a replacement shoe face, in case yours is worn through.” She eyed the guard’s bolts as if peering beneath. “Need the swap?”

“Bronze holds for lean season,” Tiffani replied. “We’ll refresh the face when the river recedes and the chain lifts without protest.”

Jhace pressed his knuckles to the shoe housing once more. The bronze transmitted the leaf’s motion clearly, like a pulse along a taut cable. Even.

“Warden requires posting,” Chloen noted, matter-of-fact.

“Posted,” Tiffani confirmed.

They pressed on, as pause invited the wind to seem profound. Jhace reset the plumb and watched it align precisely with the pier tick. He traced the line Tiffani had incised last bell. The stone absorbed his fingertip’s warmth without return. A positive measure.

A boy from the second cart had edged forward to watch. His father steadied him with a silent hand and glanced at Tiffani’s form, then reflexively at his own brake board. Her presence alone prompted such care—a quiet authority beyond the metal’s weight.

“Mark the changes,” Tiffani said.

Jhace took the scribe, reached beneath the guard lip, and etched a single V into the shoe housing—shallow, to shed water. He climbed again, cleared a spot on the hanger with a rag, and inscribed a quarter tick on the clevis ear for the next crew’s reference. Beside it, he stamped a minute QTR. Space allowed no elaboration.

The wind freshened, channeling straight from the south crown along the river, seeking every gap and angle. The right leaf murmured more distinctly. Tiffani disregarded the sound and focused on the float. She extended her palm without glancing; he placed the plumb string there. She positioned it on the sill and observed. Tilting her head slightly, she said, “Field even.”

“Ledger,” Brighlee prompted, quill moving. “Right leaf wind lean adjusted. Upper hanger clevis quarter turn at link eighteen, right run. Lower guide shoe shimmed bronze. Witness present. No HALT.”

“Include the chain link,” Tiffani said.

“Eighteen from head,” Jhace supplied. The count came by instinct, like reading knots in cordage. “Right run.”

Brighlee noted it.

A stronger gust pressed, the sort that tested resolve. The right leaf absorbed it, nudged the wire a breath, then corrected smoothly. No bind. The throat sounded of water and breeze, not steel on stone. Teamsters beyond the rope exhaled in quiet accord, grateful for the buffer against a stalled day and splintered rims.

“Stamp,” Tiffani said.

Jhace accepted the small die and impressed his mark into the guard plate beside the shoe V. It seated cleanly. Tiffani brushed her fingertip over the fresh incision, smoothing the edge to spare any wandering hand.

They verified once more—float jar, gauge wire, plumb, clevis ear, shoe. No rising warmth. The south flow eased to a measured rhythm.

Mel Quarr emerged as if she’d observed from a few steps upslope—Setters often verified without interceding. She scanned the wire, the float, the inscriptions. “Witness confirms adjustment,” she said. “Your quarter was precise. No further wind-chasing needed.”

“Not chasing,” Tiffani replied. “We aligned the hinge to favor even.”

Mel produced her blue slip. Brighlee offered the pen. They inscribed the details. Mel oversaw the entry with steady attention.

Jhace coiled the spare wire and flattened it in his palm to blunt any hazard, a habit born from hard-learned caution. He tucked it into his belt.

The wind leaned anew, then softened as a cloud crossed the sun, casting shadow over the river. Tiffani’s breathing evened so subtly only proximity revealed it. The faint hum in his left hand faded.

“Open for Bell,” she called.

Brighlee raised the rope. Carts advanced. The lead rim cleared the shoe housing at its adjusted angle, without catch. The teamster flicked his gaze to the gauge wire, then forward. He relied on the plate’s compliance. Water in the throat rose in even bands and subsided. Task complete, yet ongoing. As always.

Chloen presented the seal box with care. Tiffani took the stamp and applied the blue ring to the slip, ink true. She carried it straight to the Posting board and secured it beneath the droplet icon. The mark met air and set.

They returned to the plumb together, as the final check allowed no proxy. The bob met stone true. The float glass held a line like captured river. The clevis ear remained positioned. The bronze rested in its shadowed slot.

They held for one count more, then withdrew.

Public artifact:

BLACKGATE WIND-LEAN ADJUSTMENT NOTICE, posted Bell 5 Right leaf calibrated for south-wind load. Upper hanger clevis tightened one quarter turn at link 18 (right run). Lower guide shoe shimmed with bronze; guard plate stamped. Arc true at half under steady south push. Float and plumb confirmed. No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave). Signatories: Tiffani Veld (Outer Crown), Jhace Brast (Underface). Docket entry made at Couriers’ Bell.

Concrete image: a glass plumb bob touching the pier tick dead-on while the right leaf hangs at half in a south wind, gauge wire drawn taut across the throat, bronze shim hidden under a guard that carries a fresh scribed V.

## **Chapter 4: Relief Sluice Purge**

Bell 6 struck short and clear. Jhace knelt at the left relief mouth, where the grate rested low amid the river’s brown flow. He applied the stubby spanner to the first bolt and leaned in until the threads released their grip. He worked clockwise through the sequence—not from habit, but to maintain the rhythm that kept hands precise.

“Half-open on the flap,” Tiffani said, positioned at the valve stem with the bar. She eased it a quarter turn and attuned to the throat’s response. Water pressed forward, met the restriction, and issued a steady ribbon through the gap.

Brighlee affixed the droplet icon to the Posting board and recited the work slip into her ledger, voice carrying just enough for the queue to note without overt glances. “Relief purge at left mouth. No HALT. Witness en route.” A teamster shifted his wheel an inch from the stones, the subtle deference that law exacts from bodies.

Jhace loosened the final bolt and shouldered the grate from its seat using a short lever bar, beveled smooth from use. The grate lifted, expelled a cough of dark water, and settled aside on the stone. He guided it down carefully, sparing fingers from the iron’s edge. Silt mounded behind, compacted like fine wet ash beneath the river’s sheen, chill to the touch.

“Probe,” Tiffani directed.

He inserted the flat rod along the bottom, sensing the first foot yield easily before meeting a denser layer. He worked it gently; air bubbled up through the silt, forming a faint ring on the surface. Deeper, resistance held firm. He withdrew the rod and cleared it with a sharp flick.

“Plate lip undercut,” she observed, tracing the edge. She aligned the Lume to the opening, Shade at two, and noted the light’s deflection along the inner face. It fractured precisely where solidity should prevail. “Clear it fully and reinforce with a wall.”

“Agreed—drag it out,” he said.

Chloen descended the steps with a coil of thin chain draped over her shoulder and a sack that clinked softly. “Warden included mesh in the sack, plus a spare grate strap.” She transferred the chain to his hands and stepped back from the spray.

Jhace threaded the chain into the relief run, allowing the current to draw it beneath the silt layer. He tracked the slack, then drew it back steadily until it snagged. Securing a loop to the near post, he wedged the lever bar as an improvised capstan. Tiffani placed her palm against his shoulder blade, adding measured counterweight. The chain advanced like a blade through soaked timber, then surged. A broad sheet of silt emerged and coiled aside.

He shifted the grate farther and permitted the river its role. Water surged through the cleared gap, stripping silt in layers. He advanced the chain in controlled pulls to prevent fragmentation and deeper lodging. When the bar warmed under his grip, he rotated his hold and released the building tension without pause.

“Maintain flow,” Tiffani said. She adjusted the valve to sustain the mouth without inducing a drawdown vacuum. “Hold at shoulder level.”

The silt dispersed. The rod now penetrated deeper unhindered. He tilted it upward to map the lip’s underside—a hollow extended a hand’s breadth inward.

“Veil shelf,” she specified.

He positioned the tray from her case against the stone, arrayed with six Shade 1 flats prepared for shaping. She softened one with breath, then a fleeting coal from the brazier—just enough pliancy. He supported it steadily, as with unmarred glass. She aligned it beneath the lip, securing with heel taps in sets of seven, pausing between. Water murmured past unchanged. The air remained composed.

“Slake,” she said.

He uncapped the bladder and traced a fine wet along the joint, applying shoulder pressure alone to avoid leaching the Veil. The bead deepened, then evened. No white flash.

“Second shelf,” she continued.

He placed another Shade flat, truncated to align squarely with the first. She tapped the seven-count rhythm anew. He Slaked it to integrate seamlessly.

Brighlee sheltered her slip copy beneath the Posting board’s overhang, away from the mist. “Note that I’ve observed,” Mel Quarr stated, materializing on the steps with a coil of blue cord looped at her wrist, as if concluding another task. The Setter assessed the undercut, the Veil, the wet line in turn. “No HALT.”

“Recorded,” Brighlee replied, entering the attestation plainly. Mel’s endorsement carried authority in the ledger. The queued carts held position, though tension eased subtly along the line.

“Verify the throat for obstructions,” Tiffani said.

Jhace swept the rod along the base. The chain beneath released with a soft hiss and exhale, trailing a dark trickle that clarified swiftly, like sediment settling in clear broth. He retrieved and coiled the chain neatly, ensuring no trailing edges to snag a foot.

“Plane the lip,” she said.

He selected the small planing stone from his roll and drew it across the Veil’s face in even strokes, rinsing in the relief stream. Edges required smoothness to guide without hindrance. He tested with his thumb until it registered as a single plane.

“Mesh,” Tiffani prompted.

Chloen unrolled the sheet from the sack—square weave, unyielding, not the prone-to-curl diamond variety. Jhace trimmed it to fit with shears honed from repeated service. He installed it within the mouth, upstream of the grate, to intercept debris at the surface for easy retrieval.

“Pins,” he noted.

Tiffani presented four tempered pins, heads low-profile against flow. He bored the holes with his stone-bit, shallow and precise, clearing dust with the bellows between turns and sealing each with brushed resin. He inserted and tapped them home lightly. The mesh drew taut and held.

“Reinstall grate,” she said.

They maneuvered the grate into place. He combed the bolt threads clean and seated them by hand before engaging the spanner. Cool engagement confirmed alignment—no cross-threading.

“Full valve,” she said.

She turned the bar; the flap yielded with a measured sigh. Water fanned across the mesh in uniform sheets. No restriction. The current claimed a scatter of leaves and a paper fragment, adhering them briefly to the grid. Chloen employed the long hook deftly, dislodging them into the basket at her side.

“Test cycle,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee cued the winch. They raised the left leaf from rest and returned it, monitoring for echoes in the system. The sill float registered no variance. The plumb aligned true to the pier tick. The throat’s tone remained even.

“Mark the lip,” Tiffani said.

Jhace incised a small L beneath the grate housing, noting the season. He impressed his stamp into the guard plate beside the relief mouth—a clean indentation. Mindful of the raised edge, he burnished it smooth with his thumb to prevent inadvertent cuts.

“Cost?” Tiffani inquired.

“Forearm warmth. Fingers chilled numb, now recovering,” he said.

She offered the bottle. “Clinic if sensation lags past 7.”

He drank, the water tasting pure, unscarred as flawless glass. Her respiration steadied imperceptibly. The faint tremor in his off hand subsided.

A boy in a salt-stiffened cap lingered at the rope’s edge, steadied by his father’s hand on his shoulder. Jhace elevated the mesh corner with the hook, revealing the captured silt. The boy’s gaze sharpened with quiet focus, unalarmed. Suitable. He’d rest soundly, hands intact.

“Update the board,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee had the slip arrayed on the cap. She inscribed evenly: “Relief purge complete. Left mouth cleared. Under-lip undercut reinforced with two Shade 1 flats, Slaked and planed. Mesh pinned. Grate reseated. No HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr. Bell 6.”

Mel observed the ink set, then applied the Conclave blue to the lower margin. She affixed the notice beneath the droplet icon with deliberate care, akin to Tiffani’s pin work.

They paused for one measured breath and repeated the verifications—less costly than oversight. Rod sweep: unobstructed. Valve bar fluid. Mesh aligned, corners secure. Grate firm, bolts flush. The river’s surface gleamed evenly over the mouth. Throat air flowed cool, not heated.

“Open for Bell,” Tiffani called.

The rope lifted. Carts progressed. The lead wheel cleared with ample margin from hub to post. A child nearby mirrored the adults’ deliberate breaths, mistaking it for the day’s rhythm. The relief stream claimed its due and continued.

Chloen extended the open seal box. Tiffani applied the second blue to the docket strip for the Couriers’ runner, folding it crisply. Brighlee secured the ledger under her arm and flicked her pen dry over the river, sparing the stone.

They traversed the stones from left mouth to right, scanning not for approval, but for flaws. None surfaced. The mesh caught light beneath the flow like a subtle lattice. The Veil shelf maintained its level.

The work stood as alteration, documented and enduring.
