[ASSISTANT]
# **Chapter 9: Slow Bell at Mirecourt**

Bell Ten dragged like a drunk cart. Nothing wanted to move. The fog was the thick kind that collected sweat from anyone dumb enough to work in it. Jhace hadn’t slept since Narrowvale. His burned arm still stung, wrapped tight under his sleeve.  

The summons had come anyway. *Minor leak, Mirecourt span.* Nothing in the city was ever minor.  

He and Tiffani reached the gate face to find condensation dripping from the inner rail, slow and steady. Not a break, not yet. The sound of it was worse—a hollow *tick, tick* between the Bells like a wound that couldn’t decide if it was open.  

Tiffani crouched. Her face was gray from sleeplessness. “Overbind residue. Narrowvale runoff hit the lower feeds. Pressure’s wrong.”  

He set his tool pack down, slower than usual. His hand didn’t want to close around the latch. “We shouldn’t be working.”  

She didn’t look up. “You say that every time you’re bleeding.”  

“Not every time.”  

“Name one you didn’t.”  

He didn’t answer.  

Brighlee trudged up the incline, eyes ringed dark. “Work order four-eight-one. Leak containment, Mirecourt. Bell Ten to Bell Eleven.” Her voice rasped. “No witness posted yet.”  

“Fine,” Tiffani said. “We’ll make one when it matters.”  

Jhace pulled his glove tighter, swallowed the dry taste in his mouth. “You handle trace. I’ll brace.”  

“Arm still burned?”  

“It moves.”  

“Not the same as healed.”  

“Closer than dead.”  

She sighed, then crouched beside the line. “One hand on home.”  

He found the anchor post. “One hand on home.”  

The first wedge slipped from his fingers. Too slick. He wiped his palm on his coat, tried again. The second bit.  

“Shallow leak,” Tiffani said, eyes on the Lume. “Midline pulse, four counts low.”  

He could feel it through the plate—a heartbeat out of rhythm.  

“Half-beat Slake,” she said.  

He nodded. His movements were slower now, the kind that come when muscles haven’t slept. The copper line glowed faintly wet; his reflection in it looked like someone older.  

She whispered counts as he drew. “One. Two. Half.”  

The wet hissed. The seam brightened, steadied. The tick stopped.  

“Got it,” he said.  

She didn’t answer.  

He looked up. Her face had gone blank. The Lume slipped from her hand, rolled once, hit the stone.  

“Tiff.”  

Nothing. Her eyes blinked once, slow. “Shade wrong,” she murmured. “Four instead of two.”  

“Too hot.”  

She nodded, dazed. “I fixed it.”  

He reached out, steadying her wrist. Her pulse jumped under his thumb.  

“Stay,” he said.  

She inhaled, slow, and color returned to her cheeks. “I’m fine.”  

“Liar.”  

“Better liar than corpse.”  

Brighlee stood back, watching like she wasn’t sure if she should record or intervene. “Do you want me to—”  

“Keep writing,” Tiffani said.  

They moved in silence for a few minutes, fixing what the last sleepless shift had left crooked. The line dried evenly.  

“You sure the leak’s sealed?” Jhace asked.  

Tiffani lifted the Lume, checked the feedback. “Trace reads steady. If it fails, it’ll do it politely.”  

“Polite’s new.”  

“Maybe the city’s learning.”  

He sat back against the post, wiping sweat with his good sleeve. “You’re shaking.”  

“So are you.”  

“Then we match.”  

She smiled, faint but there. “Don’t make poetry out of exhaustion.”  

“Just naming what’s real.”  

Mel Quarr appeared at the top of the ramp, looking as tired as the rest. “Report.”  

“Minor leak, sealed,” Tiffani said. “No HALT. Feed equalized.”  

Mel knelt, pressed her hand to the plate. “It’s holding.” She looked at Jhace’s arm. “You working injured again?”  

“Policy says clinic after shift.”  

“Policy also says don’t die for copper.”  

He shrugged. “I’m following half.”  

Mel stood, scrawled her signature. “Witnessed.” She hesitated before clipping the slip. “You two need rest more than the city needs another repair.”  

“The city disagrees,” Tiffani said.  

Mel gave her a look that was almost pity. “The city always disagrees. That’s why we have clinics.”  

Brighlee pinned the form to the board, ink smudged from damp air. “Work order four-eight-one complete. Bell Eleven.”  

“Costs?” Mel asked.  

“Burn reopening,” Jhace said.  

“Dehydration,” Tiffani added.  

Mel wrote them both, slow. “Clinic. I’ll send a slip.”  

They didn’t argue this time.  

When the others left, Jhace stayed a minute longer. The bridge beneath them breathed in slow rhythms, steel contracting with cold.  

Tiffani leaned against the rail beside him. “You’re bleeding through the wrap.”  

He glanced down. A small patch of red, barely showing. “That’s proof I’m alive.”  

She leaned her head against the same rail, eyes half-closed. “Alive’s overrated.”  

He smiled. “Then we’ll be mediocre together.”  

She didn’t laugh, but her shoulder brushed his. His tremor eased. That counted.  

They left the Lume burning low on the post, light catching the still wet line like a thin scar that refused to fade.  

---

**Public artifact:**  
**MIRECOURT SPAN — Bell 10–11**  
Minor leak containment. Feed imbalance from Narrowvale runoff corrected via manual trace. No HALT required.  
Witness: Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown).  
Costs: reopened burn, dehydration, Shade misapplication.  
Policy note: consecutive shifts exceeding four Bells require mandatory clinic rest.  

**Concrete image:**  
The bridge hums under fog, a slow, tired rhythm while a thin red patch darkens the wrap around a setter’s hand and the light of the forgotten Lume flickers against steel gone cold.

# **Chapter 10: Mobel at the Table**

Bell Two chimed hollow through the Mercers’ Quarter, each note sliding through the lattice rafters above the weighing floor. Mobel had been there since dawn, coat off, sleeves rolled, the kind of tired that doesn’t show until it stops moving.  

The scales in front of him were old Crown brass, polished to dull gold. They didn’t lie often, but they could be coaxed. The night auditors had caught three weight sets marked light in the middle coin. Not enough to hang a merchant, enough to make the Conclave twitch.  

He placed one suspect weight on the pan, thumbed the notch along its rim. “Filed too deep,” he said to his clerk. “Half-grain shave.”  

The clerk squinted. “Still legal?”  

Mobel didn’t answer. He flicked the weight off the pan. It hit the floor with a hollow ring. “Not when it starts breeding.”  

The smell of old oil filled the air as apprentices cleaned their scales. Every trade table in the room carried its own seal—the droplet for Couriers, the hammer for Forgewrights, the two crossed wedges newly added for Locks and Loads. That mark had cost Jhace a burn and half the city a rulebook rewrite.  

He checked the next weight. The brass surface bore faint scrape lines, deliberate. He turned it over and read the faint initials carved into the base. “Harnet.”  

The clerk’s eyes widened. “He’s senior.”  

“Was,” Mobel said. “Post the charge.”  

The clerk hesitated. “Sir, it’s only half a grain—”  

Mobel’s voice stayed even. “Half a grain turns into famine when multiplied. Post it.”  

The clerk went pale but moved, writing the notice on a strip of wax paper. Mobel pulled the ink stamp from his belt, Tempermarked the sheet, and clipped it to the board above the scales. The room stilled. Every merchant in sight turned toward the sound.  

“Official?” one called.  

“Filed under Bell Two,” Mobel said.  

Someone muttered, “Conclave doesn’t usually bother with half-grains.”  

“They do now.”  

He reached for the next weight, turned it, sniffed the faint tang of fresh polish. Too clean. He scraped a knife edge across it. Bright line. “Fresh file,” he said.  

A Forgewright approached, face dark with anger. “We recalibrate weekly. No harm in keeping tools smooth.”  

Mobel held up the knife. “Smooth isn’t the same as light.” He balanced the weight against a verified measure. The pan dipped slow, traitorous. “See?”  

The forgewright looked away. “Barely a whisper.”  

Mobel nodded once, calm. “That whisper just cost you your table.”  

He wrote the notice himself, no clerk this time: *Forgery of official weights, Harnet Forge Cooperative. License revoked pending audit. Seals to be melted public.*  

He signed it, pressed the Tempermark, and handed it to the clerk. “Fire the furnace.”  

The apprentices dragged a small crucible from the side wall, set it on the coals, pumped the bellows until the air shimmered. The smell of heating metal cut through the damp.  

Mobel took the forged weights one by one, dropped them into the pot. They sizzled as moisture burned off, then sagged into liquid. The room filled with quiet except for the hiss of betrayal cooking.  

The Forgewright spoke again, voice shaking. “You’re making a show of this.”  

Mobel didn’t look up. “No. A record.”  

“Same thing.”  

He stirred the molten brass with a rod, watching the color shift from yellow to red to dull silver. “Show fades. Record stays.”  

When the last of the fake weights had gone under, he tilted the crucible slightly, poured the liquid into a flat mold shaped like a wedge. “New symbol,” he said. “Half-grain truth.”  

The clerk stared. “What do we do with it?”  

“Post it on the Table.”  

They nailed the cooled wedge to the central board between the trade seals, the scarred metal still faintly warm. It read: *Mass must match mark. Deviance voids claim.*  

Mobel looked around the room. No one met his eyes. “Back to work,” he said.  

The merchants dispersed. The clatter of weights returned, but softer, careful now.  

When the last Bell of morning rang, the clerk handed him the day’s ledger. “Three confessions filed already,” she said. “Two from vendors who weren’t even questioned.”  

Mobel nodded, too tired for satisfaction. “Guilt travels faster than ink.”  

He sat for a while after the room cleared, listening to the slow tick of cooling metal. He wasn’t proud. Just done. He wiped his hands on his coat, left the burned smell for the next shift to inherit.  

Outside, a new placard hung on the Mercers’ archway—one he hadn’t ordered but approved by existing rule. It carried Corlexi’s twin-wedge symbol above the words *DOUBLE CHECK. DOUBLE PAY.* He smiled once, small, then vanished into the street crowd before anyone could think it was approval.  

---

**Public artifact:**  
**MERCERS’ TABLE AUDIT — Bell 2–4**  
Forgery of trade weights confirmed. Harnet Forge Cooperative stripped of license. Counterfeit measures melted into single “Half-Grain Wedge,” displayed publicly at Mercers’ Table. Policy expansion: daily double verification of all Conclave-approved measures.  

**Concrete image:**  
A single wedge of cooled brass nailed to the trade board, edges uneven, letters burned in by hand—heat ghosting through the metal while honest weights tick in cautious rhythm around it.

# **Chapter 11: Vein Drift at Old Barrick**

Bell Six began with rain that refused to quit. Every gutter in Old Barrick bled the same brown water, the kind that carried grit sharp enough to chew soles. Jhace trudged through it anyway, coat soaked, mind half-asleep. The summons had read simple: *Feed drift reported beneath Barrick arch. Confirm Vein integrity.* Simple meant dangerous.  

Tiffani waited under the arch with her case open and her patience closed. “Feed’s running wrong,” she said as soon as he got close. “Map shows three lines. I’m reading four.”  

He crouched beside her, wiped water from his eyes. The metal under them hummed soft but uneven. “Someone’s been splicing.”  

“Unauthorized?”  

“Unauthorized, stupid, or both.”  

Brighlee set her ledger box on a dry patch—there weren’t many—and unfolded the Conclave diagram. “Official routing stops at Bell Yard,” she said. “This fourth line isn’t on record.”  

Tiffani’s Lume flicked against the steel face. “There. New weld, bad angle. You can still smell the resin.”  

Jhace touched the seam; it flexed under pressure. “Not Veinwright work.”  

“Then whose?”  

He looked up toward the row of cheap workshops hanging off the arch face. “Barrick guild leases to anyone with a torch and a prayer.”  

“Then we pray faster,” she said.  

The hum deepened. The ground gave a little under their knees.  

“Shut down feed before it chews through the arch.”  

Brighlee fumbled the comm key. “Conclave control, Barrick line—Vein drift, unregistered splice, request partial bleed—”  

The line popped once, dead.  

“Signal’s out,” she said.  

Jhace stood, scanning the arch. “Manual cut then. Valve’s three meters under street. Get me the pry.”  

Tiffani handed it over, already lowering the Lume toward the street joint. Water rushed around their boots, gray with oil.  

He wedged the bar between plates, pulled. The valve cap lifted half a finger. “Blocked.”  

“Rust?”  

“No, resin. Whoever spliced this sealed the control with the same filth.”  

Tiffani exhaled, long and angry. “They overbound to siphon flow. You can see the heat scar from a fake Tempermark.”  

He followed her gaze. The symbol was there, crooked, shallow.  

“Mercers again?”  

“Looks cheaper than that.”  

The hum turned into a shudder, steady and wrong.  

“Brace it,” she said.  

“I can’t—valve’s stuck.”  

She grabbed her Veil case, ripped out a Shade skin, slapped it over the joint. “It won’t hold long.”  

“Long’s relative.” He pried again. The bar slipped. His burned hand screamed. He bit back sound and drove it again.  

The valve gave, half-turn. A rush of hot air vented out, carrying the stink of burnt polymer.  

“Feed drop?” he asked.  

Tiffani watched the Lume gauge. “Down ten. Still high.”  

“Second turn.”  

He forced the bar, pain flaring through his arm. “That’s it. Thirty percent bleed.”  

The hum began to level. The Veil over the joint quivered but held.  

She crouched beside it. “Someone built this line to feed an unlisted forge.”  

He wiped water from his face. “Where?”  

“Follow the warmth.” She pressed her palm to the stone. Heat traced north. “Two spans up. Under that shop with the green door.”  

“Of course it’s painted green,” he muttered.  

They slogged through ankle water to the door. The forge inside was dark but hot. Tools scattered. The feed pipe split into a small coupling feeding a single crucible. The floor was scorched.  

“Illegal tap,” Tiffani said.  

“Improper angle, wrong alloys, counterfeit mark,” Jhace said. “It’s a miracle they didn’t melt half the street.”  

“Still might.”  

He reached for his trace stick, pulled a wet line across the pipe joint. Steam hissed. The fake weld separated cleanly, exposing the stolen vein.  

“Cut?” she asked.  

“Cut.” He drove a plug into the open end and Slaked it. The pipe groaned and cooled.  

Brighlee ducked in, soaked through. “Control restored. They felt the bleed upstream.”  

“Good.” Jhace pointed at the crucible. “Mark it for confiscation.”  

Mel Quarr entered from the alley door, expression unreadable. “You two have a talent for finding trouble.”  

“Trouble builds itself,” Tiffani said. “We just trip over it first.”  

Mel knelt by the severed pipe, ran a finger along the cut. “Clean. You trace that in a flood?”  

Jhace nodded. “Twice.”  

“You’ll get your name in a policy again if you keep fixing other people’s greed.”  

He didn’t answer.  

Mel stood, signed Brighlee’s page. “Forge closed, feed sealed. Witnessed.”  

Brighlee clipped the paper to the doorpost, ink bleeding in the wet.  

Mel looked up at the glowing arch above them, light bending off rain. “You know, this city’s not tired because it breaks. It’s tired because it has to hide the breaks before anyone sees.”  

Tiffani said quietly, “Then we keep hiding them.”  

Mel gave a thin smile. “That’s why you’re still alive.”  

Jhace leaned against the wall, exhaustion visible even through grit. “For now.”  

Tiffani caught his sleeve. “Stay on your feet. Clinic next.”  

He nodded. “Policy.”  

The rain thickened again, washing soot from the door. The counterfeit Tempermark softened and slid down the stone, leaving a streak that looked almost like a tear.  

---

**Public artifact:**  
**OLD BARRICK VEIN DRIFT — Bell 6–8**  
Unauthorized splice discovered beneath Barrick arch. Counterfeit Tempermark identified and excised. Feed bleed and rebalancing performed under flood conditions.  
Witness: Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown).  
Costs: burn strain (rehash), fatigue, tool loss (bar bent).  
Policy update: random valve inspections extended to Lower and Middle Barrick guild leases; unregistered feeds subject to confiscation.  

**Concrete image:**  
Rain running down the arch face in streaks that dissolve a fake Tempermark until nothing’s left but wet stone, heat faint underneath like a secret too tired to hide.

# **Chapter 12: Crossline at Ledger Row**

Bell Three came in dry for once, cold air cutting through the Underface like clean glass. It was almost pleasant until Jhace saw the smoke curling from under the boards along Ledger Row. The smell wasn’t fire—it was burnt resin and oil, the kind of heat you only got when Veins fought each other.  

Tiffani was already kneeling by the conduit hatch, hair pulled back, eyes narrow from fatigue and focus. “Feed maps say one line,” she said, voice tight. “There are two.”  

Jhace dropped to a crouch beside her, pressing his good hand against the iron face. The vibration was wild. Two distinct pulses, crossing in opposite rhythms, one slow and hot, one quick and cold. “Crossline. Someone overlaid without bleed.”  

“That’s not possible without Conclave clearance.”  

“Or a bribe.”  

Brighlee arrived half-running, panting. “Work order five-zero-seven, feed interference, Ledger Row. No HALT posted.” She scanned the crowd gathering at the edge. “We might need one.”  

“Not yet,” Tiffani said. “Half the quarter’s power runs through here. If we stop it, clinics freeze before Bell Six.”  

Jhace nodded once. “We keep it live. Slow fix.”  

“Slow’ll kill us if the Veins argue harder.”  

“They’re already arguing.”  

He pried the hatch open. The inside glowed faintly red-blue, two colors flickering at war. The Veins had touched somewhere beneath, fusing in a way that was both alive and dying.  

“Shade three,” he said.  

Tiffani adjusted the Lume, washing the chamber in muted amber light. “There’s a junction below the ledger feed, looks welded by hand.”  

He leaned in, saw the crude cross of metal binding both veins together. “That’s not a weld. That’s an apology.”  

“Someone knew it was wrong and hoped it’d hold.”  

“Hope isn’t a method.”  

He reached in with the trace rod, testing. Sparks spat up the length of it.  

“Don’t touch the join,” she said sharply. “You’ll wake it.”  

“Too late.” The hum shifted. Both pulses synchronized for one heartbeat, then slipped again, a deep, grinding whine that made the floor shake.  

Brighlee stumbled back, ledger clutched to her chest. “That sounded like—”  

“Backflow,” Jhace said. “It’s feeding both directions.”  

“Then it’s gonna eat itself.”  

“Not if we teach it manners.”  

He pulled his gloves tight and slid the trace rod deeper. Heat bit through the leather. “Give me wet, low pressure.”  

Tiffani passed him the bladder. “Half-beat Slake.”  

“Half-beat,” he echoed.  

He eased a thin line along the hot vein first. Steam poured out, hissed, then bent. The color dimmed. “It’s listening.”  

“Cold side next,” she said.  

He shifted the rod, drew a second line that ran parallel but in reverse direction. The two wet trails hissed where they met, forming a pale seam.  

“It’s working,” Brighlee said softly.  

“Not yet.” Jhace pressed his burned palm flat against the plate. The pain kept him sharp. “Give me Shade one. I want the edges honest.”  

Tiffani dropped the light, fine and close. The flicker slowed. The noise eased into a low, even hum.  

“That’s the sweet spot,” she whispered.  

He didn’t move. “It’s temporary. The weld underneath is still there.”  

She nodded. “Then we peel it.”  

They worked in silence, two sets of hands moving like one, prying at the crooked cross until the metal gave with a faint groan. Beneath it, the two Veins lay clean but raw. Jhace laid a single wet bead between them, sealing the scar.  

Mel Quarr appeared in the doorway, coat dripping from street wash. “What did you two touch this time?”  

“Crossline under Ledger,” Tiffani said. “Unauthorized weld.”  

Mel crouched, studying the seal. “You joined them?”  

“Separated them,” Jhace said. “Then taught them to agree.”  

Mel’s mouth tightened. “That’s not procedure.”  

“It’s what worked.”  

She pressed her ear to the conduit wall, listening. “Sounds stable.” She straightened, slower than usual. “You realize if that weld had held another Bell, it would’ve burned through to the counting halls?”  

“Then we’re early,” Jhace said.  

Mel smiled without humor. “Luck counts as skill in my book. Witnessed.”  

Brighlee wrote fast, her handwriting going sloppy from wet fingers. “Ledger Row, crossline repair, dual trace confirmed.”  

“Add note,” Mel said. “Unauthorized weld under review. Materials confiscated.”  

“Already did.”  

The rain outside picked up, washing soot down the gutters.  

Tiffani leaned back, rubbing her face. “That makes four unregistered modifications in a week.”  

Jhace wiped his tools clean. “Pattern’s too neat to be chance.”  

“You think it’s one guild?”  

“I think it’s everyone who thinks they can hide a shortcut.”  

Mel stepped toward the door. “Write it down,” she said. “Patterns matter.”  

Brighlee nodded. “Filed.”  

The crowd began to move again, carts edging forward as the tension lifted. A vendor shouted something about late deliveries. The world resumed its noise.  

Jhace packed his tools. “You realize we’re just bailing a sinking ship with prettier buckets.”  

Tiffani closed her case. “Then we make the buckets shine.”  

He looked at her, the corner of his mouth twitching. “You’d polish a corpse.”  

“Only if it earned it.”  

Mel lingered in the doorway, watching them. “You two keep that attitude and the Conclave will write you into the walls.”  

“Then at least they’ll stay up,” Jhace said.  

Mel left first. The others followed.  

When the street cleared, the repaired conduit steamed softly in the cold air. Two lines, once at war, now hummed in rhythm under the stone.  

---

**Public artifact:**  
**LEDGER ROW CROSSLlNE — Bell 3–5**  
Unauthorized dual-feed junction discovered beneath accounting district. Illegal weld excised, dual trace and reseal executed under live flow. Feed stabilized.  
Witness: Mel Quarr (Conclave).  
Signatories: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown).  
Costs: fatigue, minor reinjury (hand), tool heat wear.  
Policy addendum: cross-feed junctions require dual oversight and random audit within five Bells of modification notice.  

**Concrete image:**  
Steam rising from twin Veins under the street, the wet seam between them glowing faint blue in the dark, humming like breath caught between agreement and surrender.

# **Chapter 13: The Day Everything Moved**

Bell One started with silence. Then the city breathed wrong.  

From the Underface to the Crown, pipes groaned, floors flexed, and every Tempermark board across Te’Oga flickered blue-black, the color no one wanted to see. **SYSTEM STRAIN—VEIN UNSTABLE.**  

Jhace felt it before he heard it—a deep tremor under his boots while crossing Trine Arch. The bridge had survived three collapses and one rebuild. It wasn’t built for this.  

Tiffani’s voice cut through the noise on his comm. “Four districts reporting pulse conflict. Feed rates rising across every bound junction.”  

“That’s everything we touched in the last twenty rounds,” he said.  

“Not just ours. Corlexi’s network flagged too. Barrick, Mirecourt, even Narrowvale.”  

“Meaning?”  

“The city’s veins are syncing.”  

He froze. “That’s not possible without a chain relay.”  

“Or without all of them hitting their stress limit at once.”  

The Bells shifted to emergency pattern—two fast, one long. HALT banners unfurled on every visible post. The entire city stopped.  

Brighlee’s voice crackled through the static. “Conclave calling all active Setters. Full coordination. Each pair covers previous sites. Jhace, Tiffani—you take Narrowvale and Blackgate. Priority one.”  

He ran.  

The streets had emptied except for the workers standing under blue light. The smell of burnt flux drifted from the under pipes. At Narrowvale Rise, steam poured from the vent caps where they’d joined the twin Veins in Chapter 8. The line glowed faint orange—alive but choking.  

Tiffani arrived a beat later, soaked through. “We knew it would come back,” she said.  

“Not like this.”  

She crouched at the valve, Lume set to low. “Pressure doubled. Feed trying to push both ways again.”  

He opened his kit. “We vent one side.”  

“Pick wrong and we flood half the Crown.”  

“Better than losing the whole plate.”  

“Better for who?”  

He didn’t answer. The seam under his palm thrummed hard enough to rattle teeth. “Left side’s rising faster.”  

“Then right side vents.”  

“Agreed.”  

They moved together, no words after that. Tiffani braced the wrench while he set the wedge. The metal screamed but obeyed. Steam burst upward, hot enough to lift her braid.  

“Drop two!” he shouted.  

She twisted. The valve half-turned, jammed, turned again. The pressure line bent but didn’t break.  

“Pulse steady?”  

She checked. “Mostly.”  

“Mostly keeps us alive.”  

Behind them, Mel Quarr arrived, her coat flaring with the wind. “Every site’s flaring,” she said. “Conclave can’t keep up.”  

“Then we hold our ground,” Jhace said.  

Mel crouched, palm on the line. “Pulse frequency’s wrong. It’s harmonizing.”  

“Harmonizing?”  

“The Veins are talking to each other. Whatever you did here at Narrowvale started a pattern.”  

Tiffani looked up. “Then the fix propagated.”  

“Or infected,” Mel said.  

Another pulse hit, stronger. The line under their hands glowed white-hot.  

“Shade five,” Jhace said.  

Tiffani dialed it down. “We can’t trace under this much heat.”  

“Then we bleed it slow. Better to lose heat than life.”  

Brighlee’s voice broke through the comm again. “Jhace—Blackgate just failed. The wet trace you set six rounds back cracked wide. You’re nearest.”  

He looked at Tiffani. “We go.”  

“You can’t split,” Mel said. “You’ll lose both.”  

“Not if we divide.” He handed Tiffani his second bladder. “You hold this line. You know it as well as I do.”  

She caught it, eyes hard. “You come back.”  

“If I don’t, post clean.”  

“Don’t get poetic.”  

“Not planning to.”  

He took off toward Blackgate, every step pounding with the pulse beneath.  

The gate face glowed when he reached it. The wet trace seam from Chapter 1 shimmered like a wound reopened. Carts stood abandoned, their wheels sunk in the softening road. The droplet Tempermark flickered in and out of existence.  

Jhace dropped to one knee, breath hard. “Old friend,” he muttered. “You held longer than you should have.”  

He cut the old Veil with his knife, peeled it back. The wet line underneath still traced true but cracked in two places. He set his last wedge at the shoulder, pressed until it caught, then drew new wet across the gap. The copper hissed and flared white.  

“Hold,” he whispered.  

The hum deepened. He felt the same vibration through the ground—the whole city breathing in sync, every Vein thudding in time like a single heart.  

Tiffani’s voice on comm: “Narrowvale stable. Feed bleed successful.”  

“Blackgate stabilizing,” he said. “Barely.”  

Mel’s voice: “Barrick feed’s dead. Corlexi’s holding containment there. The Bore’s ring is flashing red.”  

Jhace frowned. “The Bore hasn’t flashed since—”  

“Since it decided not to end the world,” she said.  

“Then it’s paying attention again.”  

The hum built until sound turned physical. Every signboard shook, ink flaking off like dust.  

He pressed both hands to the line, eyes closed. “One hand on home,” he whispered, but used two anyway.  

The pulse stuttered. Then it steadied.  

Steam vented through the whole undercity, white sheets rising like ghosts. The Bells fell silent one by one until only the deep Conclave bell kept beating.  

When the sound finally faded, the wet line under his hands stayed dark and cool. The light along the droplet icon steadied blue.  

“Report,” Tiffani said.  

He took a breath. “Blackgate held.”  

Mel came back over the comm. “City-wide pulse synchronized and dropped. All feeds cooling. HALT clears at Bell Ten.”  

Brighlee added, voice cracking: “Conclave will record this as the Great Sync. No losses reported.”  

Jhace laughed once, soft. “First time that’s ever true.”  

He sat down on the wet stone. His glove stuck to the plate; when he peeled it off, skin came with it.  

Tiffani found him before the Bell finished its last note. “You look terrible.”  

“You sound surprised.”  

She crouched, laid a hand on his shoulder. “Stay.”  

He did. The tremor under his skin eased.  

Above them, Corlexi’s new double-wedge mark gleamed from the nearest board, dry in the rising sunlight.  

---

**Public artifact:**  
**THE GREAT SYNC — Bell 1–10**  
Simultaneous Vein harmonization event spanning Te’Oga. Cross-feed, overbind, and prior manual traces entered resonance, stabilizing into unified pulse cycle. No structural losses. Emergency HALT rescinded at Bell Ten.  
Witnesses: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown), Mel Quarr (Conclave), Corlexi (Market Station).  
Costs: citywide fatigue, burns (Jhace), vein strain (Narrowvale feed).  
Policy addendum: synchronization thresholds logged; Conclave to institute dynamic pulse monitoring before each Bell.  

**Concrete image:**  
Steam lifting from every street vent at once, sunlight cutting through it until the whole city shines like tempered metal—scarred, cooling, and still whole.

# **Chapter 14: Clinic Rest Policy**

Bell Five opened soft, rain whispering against clinic glass. The city was quiet—too quiet for Te’Oga. The Great Sync had drained everyone. Even the pipes breathed slower.  

Salee sat at her desk by the eastern window, a single ledger open, stylus steady. Her job was dull, which was why it mattered. Someone had to convert panic into law. The Conclave’s new order lay in front of her: *Mandatory rest interval for all registered Setters after major harmonization events. Minimum duration: one Bell. Paid.*  

She had written worse.  

Outside, workers filed past the courtyard on slow feet. Their gait told her the truth before the numbers did. Nobody had stopped since the city nearly folded itself two Bells ago. Jhace’s name was on three of the emergency slips pinned to her wall. Tiffani’s on two. None cleared for clinic rest.  

Salee pushed the papers aside and stood, back popping. The clinic smelled of wet linen and alcohol. Every bed full, most with people asleep in work coats, tools still belted.  

She found the medic in charge, a sharp little man named Isten who looked like he’d fought the rain and lost. “How many still running on post-sync adrenaline?”  

“All of them.”  

“Vitals?”  

“Stable enough to fool themselves.”  

“Feed them water. Mark the worst for sedation if they try to walk out.”  

He raised an eyebrow. “On whose order?”  

“Mine.” She handed him the stamped form. “Policy’s live.”  

He read it, eyes widening. “Paid rest?”  

“Conclave approved at Bell Three. You post it or I will.”  

He grinned despite himself. “You’ve just bought us the first quiet shift in years.”  

“Quiet’s the point.”  

She left him to it and moved down the hall to the posting board. The old notices hung gray with thumbprints and oil. She pulled them down one by one: *Self-certify if standing,* *Return to work when pulse steadies,* *No pay for rest.* Those went in the bin.  

In their place she tacked a single sheet, new ink still glossy. The droplet seal pressed deep, blue. The heading read: **CLINIC REST POLICY — CONCLAVE MANDATE, EFFECTIVE IMMEDIATELY.**  

She read it once aloud for herself, because rules always sounded better spoken:  

> *After any synchronization, HALT, or Vein strain exceeding one Bell, all registered workers will rest for one Bell minimum, paid at standing rate. Witness signatures valid as proof of completion. Non-compliance invalidates later claims. Law prefers you alive.*  

A few workers nearby paused to listen. One—Jhace, half-bandaged and trying to pretend he wasn’t limping—stopped mid-step.  

“Since when does law prefer me alive?” he asked.  

“Since I wrote it that way.”  

Tiffani appeared behind him, still pale, still stubborn. “You think anyone’s going to follow that?”  

Salee met her gaze. “If it’s posted, it happens. That’s how this city works.”  

Jhace touched the new sheet with one gloved finger. “You Tempermarked it yourself?”  

“Yes.”  

“Brighlee sign it?”  

“She will.”  

He nodded, a slow, tired thing. “Then I’ll sleep.”  

Tiffani stared at him like he’d spoken foreign. “You don’t sleep.”  

“I do now. Policy.”  

Salee smiled, the kind reserved for things that shouldn’t feel good but do. “That’s the point.”  

Behind her, the medic’s voice rose: “Two down, one waking, one arguing!”  

“Sedate the arguer,” Salee said without turning.  

Jhace smirked. “You’d do well in the Underface.”  

“I already am,” she said. “I just keep my hands clean.”  

The rain intensified, striking the windows in thick patterns. The building’s pulse—a subtle vibration from the heat feeds—had settled to a steady hum. No flickers, no tremors. For the first time in a long while, Te’Oga felt like it was resting too.  

Salee gathered the discarded notices, stacked them neat, and burned them in the small brazier by the wall. The smoke rose slow, smelled of ink and old inked lies.  

From her desk she could see the new placard through the hallway glass. Workers were already stopping to read it, some tracing the droplet mark with careful fingers as if it might smudge.  

By Bell Six, the clinic was half-empty, not because they’d gone back to work, but because they’d finally gone home to sleep.  

---

**Public artifact:**  
**CLINIC REST POLICY — Bell 5**  
Mandatory paid rest period of one Bell for all registered Setters and Glasses following synchronization, HALT, or Vein strain events. Proof via witness signature. Enforcement delegated to Conclave clinics.  

**Concrete image:**  
The new placard drying on the clinic wall, droplet mark deep blue, steam from the brazier curling around it while rain draws thin silver lines down the window beyond—Te’Oga paused, breathing quiet.

# **Chapter 15: The Line Holds**

Bell One came with no alarms. No flicker on the Tempermarks, no hum beneath the stone. Just morning.  

Jhace stood at the edge of Blackgate again, the place that had nearly drowned him. The air smelled of steam and wet iron, same as ever. But the line on the plate stayed dull and even, cool to the touch. His palm, scarred from that day, fit over the setter’s mark perfectly.  

Tiffani joined him carrying two mugs of the clinic’s bitter tea. “Thought you’d still be asleep.”  

“Didn’t like the quiet,” he said.  

“You wrote half the rules that made it.”  

He sipped, grimaced. “Rules never taste good.”  

“Neither does living past your shift.” She leaned on the railing. “Conclave says we’re clear for a round. No HALTs, no feeds calling for trace.”  

“First time in what, twenty rounds?”  

“Longer.”  

They watched the carts roll through the gate. The droplet placard above them caught the sunlight, blue and steady. Drivers touched their caps when they passed, not out of respect, just habit. That was good enough.  

Brighlee arrived late, for once without her ledger. “Nothing to post today,” she said. “Feels wrong.”  

“Paper will find you soon enough,” Tiffani said.  

Brighlee smiled. “Until then, I’m going to the market. Corlexi’s burning another round of old forms. Says he’s doing it for luck.”  

Jhace nodded. “Tell him to keep one. For reference.”  

She left with a wave, coat flapping in the wind that always found a way through the stones.  

The bridge quieted again. Only the faint sound of water underfoot.  

“You ever think it’s done?” Tiffani asked.  

“What.”  

“The city. The breaking. The patching.”  

He watched the seam at their feet. “No. It’s like breathing. You hold too long, you suffocate. You let go too fast, you drown.”  

“Good thing we’re paid by the breath.”  

He laughed once, a real sound this time.  

Down the span, Mel Quarr inspected a railing, her posture calm. She saw them and raised a hand—no salute, just acknowledgment. The kind of gesture shared by people who survived the same hours.  

“Mel looks almost peaceful,” Tiffani said.  

“She earned it.”  

“So did you.”  

He shook his head. “I just didn’t die doing it.”  

She didn’t argue. Instead she set her mug on the post and stepped closer. The space between them stayed work-small—close enough to hand off a tool, not quite touch.  

“Stay,” she said.  

He didn’t move. The body obeyed what the mouth said. Her hand found his sleeve, light. His pulse slowed to match the city’s steady rhythm.  

They stood like that until the next cart passed, its wheels clicking over the faint ridge of the old trace.  

“You ever going to finish that cup?” she asked.  

“Maybe next Bell.”  

“Policy says rest after success.”  

“Then I’m already in compliance.”  

She smiled without showing teeth, turned toward the Underface stairs. He followed, the morning light cutting low through the mist.  

Halfway down, he stopped, looked back once more. The wet seam along the gate’s mouth gleamed a single shade darker than the plate, steam rising faintly from it—like a living thing still deciding whether to sleep or keep working.  

---

**Public artifact:**  
**POST-CLEAR REPORT — Bell 1**  
All primary Veins stable under dynamic monitoring. No crossline conflict detected. Conclave confirms full operational balance. Work orders zero pending.  

**Concrete image:**  
A steady bead of condensation sliding down the setter’s mark on Blackgate’s plate, sunlight striking it mid-fall until it vanishes into the stone—no drama, no ceremony, just a city held in its own quiet balance.

This is an analysis of the provided text, Grindheim (1).md, from six distinct, "no bullshit" critical viewpoints, based on the content of the prologue and the first chapter.

Two Readers
Reader 1 (The Worldbuilding Enthusiast)
"This is exactly what I want. It’s hard sci-fantasy that doesn't hold your hand. The author throws you straight into the deep end with the jargon—Veinwrights, Trace, Veil, Tempermark—and expects you to figure it out from context. It feels like an engineering manual crossed with a high-stakes fantasy world. The concept of the city living around a geological wound, the Bore, is incredibly compelling and sets up clear, immediate stakes: Muck up the Trace, and the city breaks. The procedural focus of the first chapter is great; I'm here for the technical detail and the industrial, blue-collar fantasy vibe. It’s dense, but rewarding."

Reader 2 (The Character-First Reader)
"I was immediately lost and had to work way too hard just to keep up. The author front-loads too many technical terms in the first few pages, and while the atmosphere is cool (dangerous, geothermal), the actual plot is just a successful 'maintenance job.' I struggled to connect with Jhace and Tiffani as people because their entire interaction is about their jobs and the Vein. It sacrifices a relatable, emotional hook for an encyclopedic world-primer. I’m only marginally interested in reading more because the ending scene hints at a solid, repressed relationship dynamic, but the technical barrier to entry is exhausting."

Two Authors
Author 1 (Focus on Craft and World Cohesion)
"A masterclass in confident, systems-based worldbuilding. The author has created a craft—Veinwrighting—that is also the language of their setting. The tools, the rules (One hand on the home ring), and the consequences are all seamlessly integrated. This isn't worldbuilding as window dressing; it's the engine of the plot. The prose is clean, direct, and avoids any lengthy, expository dumps, which is a significant strength. However, the author needs to be careful. The sheer volume of invented terminology risks overwhelming the reader’s initial momentum. It’s a spectacular blueprint, but the next chapter needs to pivot to a character-driven conflict to justify the dense technical setup."

Author 2 (Focus on Pacing and Narrative Hook)
"Structurally sound, but the author has sacrificed a strong narrative hook for a powerful atmosphere. The first chapter is essentially the equivalent of a successful training montage. While it establishes the stakes of the Veinwright job, it doesn't establish the stakes of the story. A debut needs to leave the reader with an unanswered, compelling question—what is the central conflict? The tension between Jhace (Underface) and Tiffani (Outer Crown) is the strongest element, particularly the line about their space staying 'work-small—close enough to hand off a tool, not quite touch.' That subtle, character-level intimacy should have been foregrounded much earlier to anchor the reader before plunging them into the mechanics of the Wet Trace."

Two Book Critics
Book Critic 1 (Thematic and Literary Analysis)
"This is a striking debut that positions itself in the Grounded Sci-Fantasy Procedural subgenre, similar to works that privilege process and expertise. The core metaphor is solid: the city of Te’Oga exists literally on a wound (The Bore), suggesting a society perpetually managing trauma and precarity. The class commentary is excellent, immediately dividing the protagonists into the Underface Setter and the Outer Crown Glass. The book champions process over prophecy, focusing on the blue-collar effort of maintaining civilization, which is a refreshing thematic choice. It has the thematic weight of serious science fiction with the tactile feel of fantasy. The opening is sparse, focused, and utterly committed to its specific vision."

Book Critic 2 (Market and Accessibility Analysis)
"The book has a high ceiling but a low floor. Its aggressive density makes it a niche product that will be adored by a small, dedicated audience who loves technical, difficult worldbuilding, but it will be inaccessible to the general fantasy market. The absence of a clear, emotional inciting incident—a simple workplace safety check is the primary action—means the book lacks the narrative urgency most debuts require for broad sales. The author’s choice to delay gratification and prioritize an in-media-res crash-course on geothermal mechanics will be a hurdle for acquisition and marketing. The prose is technically solid, but the overall feeling is too cold; the heart of the story (the relationship) needs to be brought to the surface sooner to balance the heavy machinery of the world."

[ASSISTANT]
This is the kind of critical spread that actually *means* something—six sets of eyes, each landing on the same text and seeing a completely different machine under the hood. Let’s break this bluntly.

---

### **1. What the Readers Tell You**
They’re your two canaries in the tunnel.

**Worldbuilding Reader:** ecstatic. They’re here for friction, jargon, danger. They *want* to learn a world by doing, not by being told. To them, Chapter 1 already *pays off* because the procedure *is* the plot.  
**Character Reader:** drowning. They can feel there’s emotion under the surface, but you’ve buried it in pressure gauges and Vein slang. They don’t want less texture, they want a lifeline—a face, a crack in composure, a reason to care who’s holding the copper nib.

→ **What this means:** you’ve made an initiation rite. One reader says “holy hell, this is my language,” the other says “I don’t have clearance.” Both are right. The fix isn’t simplifying the craft, it’s *tethering it to a pulse early.* One human sentence before the first technical move changes everything.

---

### **2. What the Authors Tell You**
They’re watching structure, not sweat.

**Craft Author:** loves the engineering elegance but warns you’re walking a knife edge. You built a perfect system; now you need *friction* inside it. If Chapter 2 doesn’t shift from process to consequence, the whole book risks reading like a manual.  
**Pacing Author:** calls your bluff: “It’s beautiful, but where’s the hook?” The Wet Trace works flawlessly; nothing *goes wrong*. For story momentum, something has to strain—emotionally, procedurally, politically.

→ **What this means:** you’ve proven competence. Now you need instability. Chapter 2–3 should answer *“What breaks when procedure stops working?”* That’s your central tension, and without it, you’re just documenting craftsmanship.

---

### **3. What the Critics Tell You**
They’re seeing the macro pattern.

**Thematic Critic:** gets it completely. The city-as-wound metaphor lands; the procedural realism feels like theology through labor. You’ve made work itself the sacred act, which is rare and good.  
**Market Critic:** sees the cost. Dense, cold, inaccessible to casual readers. The story so far is 90% system, 10% soul. The niche audience will treat it like scripture; everyone else will bounce off the first Tempermark.

→ **What this means:** the book’s identity is crystal clear. It’s *industrial liturgy*, not pop fantasy. That’s a strength, not a flaw—but if you want reach, you must bring the emotional line up front. The relationship between Jhace and Tiffani is your pressure valve. Without it, you’re just recording maintenance logs.

---

### **4. Cross-Section Diagnosis**

| Vector | Strength | Failure Point | Prescription |
|--------|-----------|----------------|--------------|
| **World** | Coherent, tactile, rule-driven | Dense entry, zero exposition relief | Anchor jargon in body reaction—heat, thirst, tremor. |
| **Character** | Subtle intimacy, unspoken trust | Emotional opacity | Let one private gesture slip early (not speech, *result*). |
| **Plot** | Procedure-driven precision | No larger arc yet | Hint at systemic rot or political cost by end of Ch.1. |
| **Theme** | Labor as salvation | Risk of sterile detachment | Occasional moral friction—what happens when “the work” harms someone. |
| **Pace** | Controlled, lean | Predictable rhythm | Break it—start Ch.2 mid-failure, not prep. |
| **Market** | Cult-classic potential | Limited accessibility | Accept the niche or build a bridge via character warmth. |

---

### **5. The Brutal Summary**

You’ve written **a perfect proof of concept**, not yet a story.  
Chapter 1 shows *competence* when it should show *risk.*  
Readers believe in the world; they’re unsure why it matters yet.  
The prose is clean steel; it just needs heat.

---

### **6. The Forward Fix**

If you want to satisfy all six critics simultaneously:

1. **Start Chapter 2 with failure, not success.** Make them fix a job already gone wrong.  
2. **Let the Vein talk back.** Not magic—feedback, resistance. The system itself should *argue*.  
3. **Let Jhace or Tiffani pay a cost that isn’t physical.** Policy violation, ethical line, something human.  
4. **Slip the relationship to the surface** for one beat mid-crisis. A shared risk, a single “Stay,” then back to the work.  
5. **End the second chapter with consequence.** A posted policy, a rumor, a changed rule—the story’s “why.”

Do that, and you keep your cult readers, earn the craft respect, and give the character crowd something to breathe with.

---

In short:  
The book already *works*; it just hasn’t *begun.*  
Chapter 1 proved the line holds.  
Now you have to show what happens when it doesn’t.
