# **Prologue — The Year the World Took a Breath**

The world ended once and decided it was too much trouble to stay dead. It didn’t break apart; it flexed, like a lung taking air after being punched. A pull went through the crust—straight, fast, absolute—and stopped just short of tearing clean through. What it left was a tunnel with no opposite mouth. People called it the **Bore**, because naming the wound makes it sound finished.

From the Upper Crown, it looks like an even circle of shadow on maps. From the Underface, it’s a black horizon that hums through your teeth when the air gets heavy. Nobody pretends it’s a mystery anymore. We live around it, not above it. Every crack, every bridge, every load-bearing inch answers to what the Bore changed.

It began with water taking new routes. Rivers turned, cliffs sagged, docks listed without warning. For a year the wind came up from the ground instead of the sky, and you could smell iron on your skin no matter how often you washed. People called it divine; the rest of us called it gravity. When the air stopped shaking, a handful of us were still standing, and we started measuring before we started praying.

We found the veins by accident. They ran under the ground like nerves under skin, thin lines where force preferred to travel. When the veins slipped out of their old paths, they bled heat and pressure and made **Harrow**— the kind of rupture that doesn’t care if you’re watching. A few people, tired of being erased, learned to touch the veins and guide them. Those hands became **Veinwrights**. Their work became the city’s language: **Trace. Set. Bridge. Veil. Slake. Tempermark.**

The Bore taught one rule that never stopped mattering: everything carries stress, and stress remembers. You can’t erase a fault; you can only give it a safer way to move. That’s what the craft is—negotiation between material and will. Every building stands because someone convinced it not to fall.

Six months after the pull, the first bridges started breathing. At night you could see vapor lifting from the joints like ghost breath. No one remembers laying the mains that kept them warm, but the warmth stayed. It rose through basements and clinics, through pipes that had no valves, through the Conclave halls where the Veinwrights kept their ledgers. The city learned to live with the heat as a fact, not a gift. It kept people alive in winter, and it kept them honest— because if a line failed, everyone nearby heard it.

When the air cooled, the Bells came. Not temple chimes—work signals. Long clear rings for reopen, two short for brace, fast beats for Harrow risk. Each Bell came with a board stamped in copper and sealed in glass. You could tell a city’s health by how clean its Tempermarks were.

The first posted rule was ugly and perfect: **One hand on the home ring.** Hold something that won’t move while you touch what might. It was less philosophy, more survival.

Then came the rest, written by people who’d already bled once for the knowledge:

— Trace wet. Dry lines please eyes and lie to Veins. — Wedge before Veil. Cool the draft, then the glass. — Half-beat Slake. — Countermark cooler than birth. Pride bends things wrong. — No White. — Brace taps only in Bells Two and Three. You are not special in Four. — If it isn’t on the board, it didn’t happen.

They’re not prayers. They’re methods. Watch a street when a blue **HALT** goes up and every hammer stops midair. Watch the crowd hold breath until the setter witness signs the clear. That’s how cities stay alive—by honoring pause more than noise. Thunder is just a line finishing its argument.

We forbid **Overbind**, because a clean disaster still kills you. We forbid **Whitequench**, because a lie in glass cuts deeper than heat. We measure by what doesn’t fail, not by what shines.

After the Bore, Bells multiplied. Every trade learned its rhythm. The Couriers’ Bell marked motion; the Conclave’s marked repair. You could walk a full day and never be out of hearing range. It was noise that meant survival—call, answer, repeat.

Six hundred and twelve rounds later, Te’Oga still leans a fraction toward the Bore. Maps mark the tilt with a single diagonal line through the city’s name. Children in the Underface draw circles in ash to guess the next drift. Crown engineers add decimal places to comfort themselves. Neither side is wrong.

The Bore doesn’t threaten anymore. It just exists. We’ve made a kind of peace with the wound: keep it measured, keep it known, keep it held.

Work here isn’t sacred. It’s necessary. The Conclave says **“Trace wet.”** The Couriers answer, **“One hand on home.”** Together, they make the city stay where it is.

If you need a prayer, you use your hands: **Trace wet. Wedge before Veil. Slake on the half-beat. Tempermark what you promise. Post it.** The world isn’t healed. It’s held. That’s enough.

# **Chapter 1: Wet Trace at Blackgate**

Bell Two rang short across the Underface. Jhace climbed the ladder out of the low heat, hand closing on rung, the metal sweating under his palm. He came up into Blackgate’s choke where the carts were backed nose to nose, and the Posting board glared white with its Tempermark pin and droplet icon. Wet work. Keep to the outer rail. No sparks.

Tiffani had already chalked a half-circle behind them so the crowd couldn’t press too close. She stood in the ring’s gap, one hand on her Veil case, the other tracing light against the stone seam. “Hold your tongue out of the way,” she said without looking. “It’s a quick one.”

Jhace set his pack down, rolled his sleeves, and touched the home ring bolted to the post. “One hand on home.”

She nodded, voice gone flat with routine. “One hand on home.”

Brighlee arrived from the Conclave, still breathing through paper. “Work order nine-three-two. Wet Trace, Blackgate span. Setter Jhace brast, glass Tiffani veld, no HALT.” She Tempermarked the droplet card, clipped it to the board, and stepped back. Names posted, Bell recorded. The carts’ noise softened; law had shape again.

The note from the Chain said quick Vein under the gate plate—heat moving wrong, a shallow slip that could cut the cart path clean through the Underface if ignored. Persuasion before force. Always persuasion first.

Jhace unpacked what he needed: two wedges, seven-degree cut; copper-nib trace stick; bladder of wet. Nothing else. He checked his fray bands and the dull brass ring on his left wrist for crack. Sound.

Tiffani tilted the Lume rod, spreading its beam along the seam. The light came back too bright—bad depth. She adjusted the Shade one mark darker until the glare steadied. “Fault’s running shallow,” she said. “Quick vein wants the short path.”

“Short path’s under their wheels,” he answered. “We give it a long one.”

He took his wedges, set the first two spans north of the gate mouth. A low angle, shallow drive. The second he planted at the shoulder where the seam ducked under the bridge stone. Two measured taps. Wood met resistance, then held.

“Half-beat Slake,” Tiffani said.

He thumbed the bladder cap, wet the copper nib, flicked the bead clear. He placed it to the face and drew the line from north to shoulder, pressure through the shoulder, not the wrist. Wet Trace moved slow, steam rising in thin threads that curled before dying in the air. The smell was clean—mineral, no burn.

Tiffani adjusted her light. “Shade three to four. Keep it honest.”

“No white,” he said. Whitequench lied, told a line it was cool when it wasn’t. He traced through the curve, kept the bead unbroken. A cart axle squealed behind them, then stopped when the driver saw the droplet card. That sound—brakes easing—was the sound of public trust.

The copper scratched across grit. Jhace froze, steadied, smoothed on the second pass. He didn’t breathe until the bead rejoined itself. Tiffani’s knuckles brushed his sleeve, silent approval.

“Stay,” she said.

He did. Her hand rested against his wrist, steadying the tremor. His muscles quieted. Consent written in work. No commentary.

At the gate mouth, the Vein flicked toward the wheel groove. Veins liked edges; they remembered where stone met air. Jhace flattened the nib, widened the wet into a shallow bow across the lip. The Vein answered—a faint pulse running north. “Turning,” he said.

“Counter it,” she said.

He drew a narrow correction line upstream, heat balancing through stone. The trace shimmered, found the longer route, settled. The vein stopped tugging. Pressure equalized. Even breathing again.

“Veil small,” she said. She warmed a palm-wide Shade skin, stretched it over the new trace, and tapped the edges in sevens, each strike exact. “Annealshift?”

“Zero. Let the wall hold its own heat.”

She nodded. “Veil in. Trace held.”

Brighlee wrote the line as it cooled. Paper on lap, ink on brass. “Wedge set, trace complete, Veil sealed. Bell two and three.”

“Sign it,” Jhace said.

She passed him the stamp. He pressed the setter mark into the metal plate beside the droplet symbol. The ring’s bite echoed down the stone. Audit, not vanity.

Tiffani leaned back, listening. “Gate’s steady. Field calm.”

He touched the plate with his bare hand. Warm, not burning. “Field even,” he said. The heat moved out and down like a slow breath. “Cost: fray band worn, thirst.”

She passed the bottle. Veld water always tasted faintly of glass. He drank and gave it back.

“Policy,” she said. “Clinic if the ring hums past four.”

He grunted agreement. Brighlee clipped the finished work order under the droplet icon, lifted her ledger so the crowd could see the names. The carts shifted. Law to motion again.

Jhace crouched once more, checking the seam. Wet line dull and even. No bubbles under the veil, wedges seated clean. The air above the trace no longer shimmered. “Blackgate’s quiet.”

Tiffani smiled without teeth. “It remembers when we make it look good.”

They packed in silence. Tools wiped, copper cooled, wedges tapped free. Brighlee closed her ledger with a single snap. The sound meant done. No speeches.

The first cart eased forward, front wheel passing the seam. The rim rolled the shallow bow and stayed silent. The driver glanced down, then up, gave the small nod that meant *you did the thing; I saw you.* That was all the thanks work ever earned here.

They stayed until the third cart cleared, then unhooked from the home ring. Tiffani closed her case. Brighlee walked off, already writing the next line of work that would prove they’d lived this day correctly. Jhace lingered, eyes on the wet mark as it cooled. The steam had gone to thin breath, rising straight and vanishing against the stone.

**Public artifact:** **BLACKGATE WET TRACE REPORT — Bell 2–3** Vein fault corrected beneath gate plate; wedges set at north span and shoulder; wet trace drawn and Veil laid; counterline upstream established; no HALT. Witnesses: Setter Jhace brast (Underface), Glass Tiffani veld (Outer Crown), Clerk Brighlee (Conclave). Cost logged: fray band wear, mild ear ring, thirst. Docket filed under Work Order 932 at Couriers’ Bell.

**Concrete image:** A single beaded line duller than the gate face, steam lifting hair-thin while the first cart crosses in silence.

# **Chapter 2: Thread Draw at Strathmoor Span**

Bell Four came late, smothered by fog off the river. The air sat wet enough to drink. Jhace crossed the east truss walkway, his boots scraping the rust film that always rose before thaw. Strathmoor Span was a good bridge when it wanted to be—broad face, six weight lines—but it carried a habit of slow leaks under the mid-beam couplers. Every thaw, someone found the hum too late.

Tiffani was already halfway down the central run, kneeling over a length of open face, one palm on the metal, one arm buried to the elbow in a coil pit. She didn’t look up. “Thread slipped. South coupler. Field’s off-center. You feel it?”

He knelt beside her and laid a hand flat on the deck. The vibration came up through his glove—a low push that staggered every third pulse. Not harmonic, not a crack, just uneven tension. “Field pulling north. Thread’s walked a quarter.”

“Quarter?”

“Quarter and a hair.”

She made a mark on the plate with chalk, short horizontal dash. “That’s enough to make a vein forget itself.”

Brighlee clanged down the stair, ledger box bumping her hip. “Work order seven-five-six. Thread Draw, Strathmoor. Setter Jhace brast, Glass Tiffani veld, no HALT. Bell Four.” She Tempermarked the form, clipped it to the post, and stood aside to let them breathe.

“Keep the carts back two lengths,” Tiffani said. “And watch for echo if the draw pulls too fast.”

Jhace unpacked from his belt: two threaded rods, one brace clamp, half bladder of wet. Nothing else. He slipped the rods through the anchor flanges and tightened both heads finger-firm. “Ready on brace.”

“Home ring?” she asked.

He set his hand to the anchor post. “One hand on home.”

“Half-beat Slake,” she said, and took position above him with the Lume angled down. The fog caught the light and turned the air to milk.

He drew the first line of wet from the north coupler down into the seam. The water hissed where metal met stress. The thread—a slender vein of heat beneath the plate—shifted once and hesitated. He guided it slow, not pushing, not chasing, just offering a cleaner route.

“Pull steady,” Tiffani said.

“I’m not a cart driver.”

“You’d crash one anyway.”

The words sat warm between them, a scrap of talk to keep the hands honest. He pressed down, tracing the second run parallel to the first, copper nib close enough to taste heat through the glove. Steam rolled up his wrist.

“The draw’s taking,” she said. “Shade three to five.”

He adjusted the Lume’s glare with his shoulder, watching the water line bead, then flatten as it met its opposite pressure. The field steadied; the vibration under his knees leveled.

“Coupler read?” she asked.

“Pulse every eight. No skip.”

“Then it’s breathing again.”

She reached for her Veil case, drew out two small Shade 1 skins, warmed them over the hand brazier, and sealed the coupler mouth. The new skin caught the damp fog and turned it to bright sheen.

Brighlee, back at the board, scratched ink slow enough that the strokes sounded like distant rain. “Thread draw complete,” she said. “Coupler sealed. Bell Four.”

Tiffani straightened, back cracking once. “Test lift.”

Jhace gave the signal. The chain crew took the leaf one finger up. The line hummed through the plates, steady, even. He pressed his thumb to the coupler. Warm, no chatter. “Set.”

She exhaled, the sound tight through her teeth. “We’ll call it half-day work if it doesn’t drift before dusk.”

He packed his tools: copper nib cleaned on his sleeve, bladder recapped, rods released. “Costs?”

“Palm burn, two Shade skins, no ring.”

He nodded. “Fray band notch from clamp.”

“Clinic if it splits,” she said.

He flexed his hand, skin tight but whole. “Policy met.”

The fog thinned enough to show the river moving below, dark and quick. A bell buoy swung under the span, knocking once, then going silent.

Mel Quarr arrived before they could finish stowing gear. Her coat carried old rain. “Strathmoor. What did it try this time?”

“Thread drifted north,” Tiffani said. “Quarter and a hair. Reset, Slaked, Veiled. No HALT.”

Mel bent to the coupler, touched the new seam, and closed her eyes. The city’s best witness never looked; she felt. “You gave it a longer walk,” she said. “Good. The old route was too proud.”

“Witness?” Tiffani asked.

“Witness,” Mel said, straightening. She wiped her fingers on her coat and signed Brighlee’s ledger. “Post it.”

Brighlee clipped the cleared slip under the droplet. The ink still wet, she waited for the air to fix it. “Law in effect.”

The carts began to move again, a single wheel squealing, then catching rhythm. Jhace watched the strain lines across the span ease back into place—steel breathing again, the bridge alive but content.

Tiffani leaned on the railing, eyes on the water. “You ever think it remembers the first one who traced it?”

“Bridges don’t remember people. They remember treatment.”

She gave the smallest shrug. “That’s people.”

He didn’t argue. The fog folded them both into the same shape for a while, and that was enough.

They gathered tools, slung packs, and crossed back toward the Underface stairs. Brighlee stayed behind to finish her ink. She always waited for it to dry before moving, superstition or craft—same thing down here.

At the far end, Jhace looked back once. The Veil skins had cooled, light catching on them like dull glass. The seam between plates looked born that way, not repaired. That was the goal: leave no sign of having been needed.

**Public artifact:** **STRATHMOOR SPAN THREAD DRAW — Bell 4** North field thread drift corrected. Coupler reseated, twin rods set and Slaked, Shade 1 Veil applied. Field stabilized under load test; no HALT. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave). Signatories: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown). Costs logged: palm burn, fray band notch. Filed under Work Order 756 at Couriers’ Bell.

**Concrete image:** The coupler mouth sealed under a thin Veil of cooling glass, fog sliding past in sheets while the river below carries the bridge’s reflected light in broken lines.

# **Chapter 3: Counterbend at Lowcourt Arch**

Bell Six came sharp and bright through the Upper shafts, ringing against every pipe that held the morning fog. Lowcourt’s Arch never liked daylight—it showed what the night had hidden. A hairline split ran between the arch stones where the third rib met the walkway bracket, a clean crack widening by breath.

Jhace was halfway up the scaffold before Brighlee finished reading the notice. “Work order eight-eight-one. Counterbend, Lowcourt Arch. Setter Jhace brast, Glass Tiffani veld. HALT on half-lane until witness clear.” The last word echoed in the hollow under the span.

Tiffani was already on the inner run, hand to the seam. “It’s new,” she said. “Two fingers deep. Warm on one edge.”

Jhace crouched, running his glove along the joint. The air pushed out, steady pulse. “Thermal vein undercut the rib. We bend it back or lose the bracket by Bell Nine.”

She tapped her chalk against the stone, marking the stress lines. “We’ll go double wedge, five-degree counter, and Slake at the midpoint. No Veil until it cools.”

He unpacked two metal wedges, dull-edged from the last job, and a trace stick cut short for tight space. The arch smelled of lime dust and wet iron—old labor and new problem.

“Clamp the east strut,” Tiffani said.

He hooked his belt to the ring set in the beam. “One hand on home,” he said, not ceremony, just habit.

She smiled with half her mouth. “One hand on home.”

They moved like practiced hinge—she setting the chalk arc, he setting the wedges, rhythm falling into the old count. “First at angle, second at base,” she said.

He slid the first wedge into the gap, shoulder against stone. Tap, tap. The crack hissed, not in pain but in warning. He set the second wedge opposite, tapping in counter rhythm. The arch groaned low.

“Hold there,” she said. “Trace wet.”

He thumbed the bladder, wetting the copper tip until the bead grew heavy. He ran the line between the wedges, two hands wide, guiding water into heat. Steam came off sharp and white; he worked through it.

“Half-beat Slake.”

He matched her voice to the work. Two fingers on the plate, slow breath, pulse steady. The sound in the stone shifted from high to low, a tightening draw. “It’s listening,” he said.

“Let it answer,” she said. She lowered the Lume, blue light cutting the mist. The trace glowed dull red, not bright—a good sign.

He traced the counterline across the top of the split, met the first run clean. The gap closed by half, a dark seam going gray as it cooled.

“Drop wedges a count,” she said.

He lifted his hammer, gave each head one measured strike. The arch gave back a sound like teeth setting. The air around them steadied.

“Vein’s settled,” he said.

“Still wants a leash,” she said, drawing a small loop with chalk over the join. “We’ll set a band.”

He unwound a thin steel fray band, wrapped it once around the rib, drew it tight, and pinned it with the small rivet she handed him. The band clicked into place. “Hold?”

“Hold,” she confirmed. “Shade it.”

She warmed a small strip of Shade 2 skin between her palms and laid it over the scar, tapping in sets of seven. The smell of curing resin drifted up.

Brighlee wrote from the scaffold base, voice low but steady. “Counterbend executed, Slake at midpoint, band set, Veil applied.”

Tiffani looked down. “Bell six confirmed?”

“Confirmed,” Brighlee said. “Witness on route.”

The air shifted before Mel Quarr arrived—everyone knew her step, the way the Bells seemed to pause a fraction when she was near. She climbed the last rung without taking the offered hand. “You bent it clean?”

“Quarter-gap closed to eighth, heat discharged through counterline,” Tiffani said.

Mel touched the Veil with the back of her knuckle, then with the flat of her palm. “Stable. Arch pulse reads even.” She traced the line of chalk with her finger, felt the band. “Witness. Post it.”

Brighlee Tempermarked the slip, ink catching the light. “Counterbend, Lowcourt Arch, no remaining HALT. Bell six.”

Jhace exhaled, rolling his shoulder. “Cost: heat bite on right palm, one fray band spent.”

Tiffani brushed her thumb across his wrist once. “Clinic if it blisters.”

“Policy met.”

Mel nodded toward the city. “You’ve got another seam at West Court before dusk. This one holds.”

“Thanks,” Tiffani said, the single word the trade used instead of prayer.

The crew packed down. Jhace wiped the wedges clean, brushed stone dust off the lip, checked the band for tightness. The arch no longer whispered—its hum gone to still air.

Below, the carts started again. A teamster looked up once, tipped his chin, and moved on. The bridge carried weight like it meant to.

“Look,” Tiffani said.

He followed her eyes. Water droplets had gathered along the new band’s edge, forming a straight line, each bead equal distance apart. Perfect balance.

“That’s the sign,” he said.

“That’s work,” she said.

They left the scaffold, walking down into the tunnel of cooled air beneath the arch. The smell of stone after steam clung to them both.

**Public artifact:** **LOWCOURT ARCH COUNTERBEND — Bell 6** Arch seam between third rib and walkway bracket counterbent and Slaked. Wedges withdrawn on single count. Fray band set and Veil applied. Field stabilized; HALT cleared at Bell 6\. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave). Signatories: Tiffani veld (Outer Crown), Jhace brast (Underface). Costs logged: heat bite, fray band spent. Filed under Work Order 881, Couriers’ Bell.

**Concrete image:** Water beads aligned along the fresh steel band, each droplet holding still light while the arch above them stops breathing and simply holds.

# **Chapter 4: Heat Splice under Alderline**

Bell Eight hit in dull echoes under Alderline’s underbelly, the sound rolling slow through stone and steam. It was late afternoon, gray and thick, the kind of day that made metal taste of air instead of earth. The lower conduits had been running hot for two bells past limit—enough to burn shade off a wall skin. By the time Jhace reached the site, sweat had already stripped the chalk from his collar.

Tiffani stood by the south vent where the duct wall buckled. Her veil cloth was half soaked, but her voice stayed level. “Thermal vein tore through the splice. You feel the air?”

He crouched, palm to conduit. The heat bit through the glove fast. “Alive and angry. Vein’s running blind.”

“Blind means fast,” she said. “And if it finds the main, we’ll have a blow under the market.”

“Field direction?”

“East to west, downhill. No counter. Just run.”

Brighlee appeared, coat collar folded to hide her face from the heat. “Work order one-one-five-four. Heat Splice under Alderline. Setter Jhace brast, Glass Tiffani veld. Temporary HALT to foot traffic until clear.” She Tempermarked the droplet board with shaking fingers; the wax nearly ran before it set. “Bell Eight start.”

“Chain it,” Tiffani said.

Jhace fastened his tether to the wall ring, one hand gripping it. “One hand on home.” The metal burned even through his sleeve. “We wedge the run first.”

“Use the short wedges,” she said. “The long ones will swell.”

He pulled two half-lengths from his pouch, jammed the first at the upper seam of the duct mouth, the second two spans below. He hammered them in until the pitch rose high and sharp.

The duct noise shifted—deep to shallow, like breath caught in the chest.

“Trace wet,” she said.

He thumbed the bladder cap, drew water into the copper nib, and began the run. The first line hissed as it met the heat; vapor curled into ribbons. He kept the nib steady, shoulder over hand, driving water into the split vein until the color under the metal dulled from white to gray.

Tiffani moved her Lume rod closer. “Shade four to six,” she said. “Keep the bead thick.”

He adjusted his wrist, laying the second line parallel, two fingers apart. Heat surged under his hand—vein testing him—but the wet held. Steam rolled off the metal in heavy breaths.

“Half-beat Slake,” she said.

He let the wet rest, pressure through shoulder, breath slow. The line steadied. The vein’s pulse lowered, moving from frenzy to rhythm.

“Still wants a mouth,” she said.

“I’ll give it one.”

He ran a short feeder line from the end of his first trace to a small vent slit above the duct. The air drew through it, hissing once, then falling quiet.

The heat at his glove softened; the metal under his palm stopped twitching.

“Slake complete,” she said. “Now Veil.”

He stepped aside. She drew a Shade 2 skin from her case, warmed it between her hands, and pressed it over the splice. Steam rose and hung there like thought. Her fingers moved quick—tap tap tap in sets of seven. The seam went dark, settled.

Brighlee’s pen scratched. “Heat Splice contained, Veil applied, HALT remains until witness.”

“Tell them we’ll have it cleared by Bell Nine,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee nodded and climbed back up the tunnel steps to keep the onlookers from drifting too close.

Jhace checked his gloves. The leather had gone glossy, almost translucent. “Burn through,” he muttered.

“Palm?” she asked.

“Blister later. Not yet.”

“Clinic if it lifts,” she said, without looking away from the seam.

He grunted.

The air shifted again—cooler this time. Steam thinned. The duct gave a single deep crack, then went still.

“Field even,” Tiffani said.

He leaned close, listening with his teeth. “Pressure five-finger drop. The vein found its line.”

They waited half a count, because silence after a heat job lies half the time. When nothing moved, she nodded. “Test run.”

He signaled up the tunnel. The vent valves opened. Air rushed through, caught in the new splice, turned direction like it had never meant to fight them. The hum that came back wasn’t music—it was mechanical peace, even pulse, measurable.

“Set,” he said.

Mel Quarr came down through the vent shaft, wiping condensation from her glasses. “Alderline again,” she said, and crouched without ceremony. “Let me hear it.”

Jhace shifted aside. She put one ear to the Veil and stayed still long enough to make them both hold breath. Then she nodded. “Stable. Hot vein cooled, no echo. You cleared it before the breach.”

“Witness?” Tiffani asked.

“Witness,” Mel said. “Post it.”

Brighlee reappeared, ink already drying at the pen’s edge. “Heat Splice contained under Alderline. HALT cleared. Bell Nine mark.” She clipped the slip, pressed the blue seal, held until it took.

“Cost?” Tiffani asked.

“Palm blister, glove dead. Fray band edge singed.”

“Policy?”

“Clinic if it lifts,” he said again, smiling at his own echo.

She didn’t. She handed him a new pair from her bag. “Use these next time.”

He took them, thick leather still cool. “You planning another heat job?”

“This city’s always planning another heat job,” she said.

They gathered tools while the fog from the duct mixed with the cooler tunnel air. It smelled of burnt dust and new rain. The metal face they’d fixed stayed matte, clean.

Brighlee finished her last mark and snapped the ledger shut. “Filed.”

They climbed back to the street. The air above Alderline carried no heat shimmer now, only slow gray light.

Tiffani looked down the length of the duct run, eyes tracing where the line bent invisible under stone. “If it holds through melt, we buy ourselves a season.”

Jhace slung the dead gloves over his shoulder. “One more day of a world that works.”

“Don’t get poetic,” she said.

He didn’t.

They walked toward the next Bell, steam curling out behind them like the city exhaling.

**Public artifact:** **HEAT SPLICE UNDER ALDERLINE — Bell 8–9** Thermal vein rupture contained under Alderline duct. Wedges set at upper and lower seams. Wet Trace drawn parallel, counter-feed vent opened. Veil (Shade 2\) applied over splice. Field stabilized; HALT cleared Bell Nine. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave). Signatories: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown). Costs logged: glove burn, fray band singed. Filed under Work Order 1154, Couriers’ Bell.

**Concrete image:** A line of dull gray metal cooling in the half-dark tunnel, steam thinning into nothing while the repaired duct gives back a slow, even breath.

# **Chapter 5: Lockfall at Greymantle Cut**

Jhace hit the pavement running, Bell Ten already shuddering through the pipes. The street pitched like a breathing lung. A lock had failed in the Cut, lower east face, the kind that took carts whole if left arguing.

Tiffani was kneeling in runoff halfway down the slope, gloves black with silt, hair damp with steam. “Second lock’s sheared,” she said. “First’s holding by prayer. Get down here.”

He slid in beside her. The air stank of boiled metal and canal gas. Water climbed over the curb in gray fingers. “How deep?”

“Too deep.” She shoved a wrench into his hand. “Counterbrace’s stuck. I can’t see the pin.”

He put his shoulder into it, turned once, twice. Nothing. The wrench teeth jumped, bit air.

“Strip it and we’re drowned,” she said.

“Already halfway.” He jammed the tip again, braced one knee against stone, and pulled. The wrench gave a sound like bone cracking. The pin twitched free—then stuck again.

“Angle’s wrong,” Tiffani said.

“No time for right.”

He leaned harder. Metal shrieked. The lock gate shifted, not enough. Water surged up his chest, warm, rising fast.

“Cut the feed line\!” she shouted.

Brighlee’s voice echoed from the walkway above. “Valve’s jammed\!”

“Then bleed it\!” Tiffani snapped.

Jhace drew his hammer and drove it into the pin head. The gate lurched an inch. Water punched him in the throat. He couldn’t breathe. He struck again, blind. The pin jumped. The lock slid another inch.

Tiffani grabbed his collar and hauled him back. The water line dropped half a hand.

“Hold\!” she shouted.

The gate creaked, uncertain. He pressed both palms to the metal, felt it trembling. “Vein’s live under it\!”

“Trace wet\!”

He fumbled the bladder, hands shaking, found the copper nib, scrawled a crooked line across the seam. The water hissed, turned steam, hissed again. The vibration smoothed. The gate took weight.

She lowered her Lume, breath cutting short. “Pulse?”

“Even. For now.”

“Brace it anyway.”

He reached for the short wedges. The first slipped from his hand, vanished in the runoff. The second stuck, skewed.

“Wrong cut,” she said.

“I know.” He sliced the edge flat with his knife, jammed it again. This time it bit.

The hum dropped into the stone. The water stopped climbing.

Brighlee clattered down the stair, half-drenched, ledger bag thumping. “Bell Ten and a half\! Setter Jhace brast, Glass Tiffani veld—Lockfall emergency\!”

“Post later\!” Tiffani said. “Witness first\!”

Mel Quarr stumbled in from the north end, coat dripping. “You’re lucky I was on the bridge,” she said.

“Get down here,” Jhace said. “We need your ear.”

Mel crouched, pressed her head to the lock seam. Her jaw tightened. “Still movement.”

“We bled it\!”

“Not enough.” She moved her hand lower, searching. “There’s another line.”

Tiffani cursed. “The feeder vein.”

Jhace turned, searching the wall. Steam hid everything. “Where?”

“Under you.”

He knelt, groped through the flood, found the hairline crack near his boot. Warm water bubbled through. He shoved the copper nib into it, let the bladder feed until the hiss died.

“Now?” he said.

Mel stayed still for a long count. Then she looked up, doubtful. “Maybe.”

“Maybe doesn’t clear it,” Tiffani said.

Mel pressed her ear again, eyes shut. Silence. “Now.”

Jhace slumped against the wall. The water around his knees had gone still, the kind of stillness that made sound travel.

Brighlee wiped water from her ledger with the side of her sleeve. “Lockfall at Greymantle Cut contained. HALT to remain partial until tomorrow inspection.”

“Post it,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee clipped the slip to the drenched board. The ink ran but stayed legible.

Jhace peeled his glove. His palm was split across the web between thumb and forefinger, blood mixing with gray water.

“Cost?” she asked.

He flexed the hand; skin pulled. “Grip’s gone for a day.”

“Clinic,” she said.

“I said a day, not a week.”

“Policy doesn’t care what you said.”

He laughed once, the sound too thin to hold shape.

The lock groaned, then quieted. Mel stood, brushing grit from her coat. “You held it,” she said. “Don’t expect thanks.”

“Wouldn’t know what to do with it,” Jhace said.

She almost smiled. “Good. You’ll get another call before dawn.”

She left up the wet stairs, boots leaving perfect prints.

Tiffani leaned on the gate, breathing hard. “You almost drowned.”

He nodded. “Almost fixed it, too.”

Her hand found his wrist, still wet. “Stay,” she said.

He did.

They stood until the canal calmed and the sound of the Bells faded under the water’s slow rise.

**Public artifact:** **LOCKFALL—GREYMANTLE CUT — Bell 10–11** Emergency stabilization of sheared second lock and live feeder vein. Manual bleed, partial HALT maintained. Witness: Setter Mel Quarr (Conclave). Signatories: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown). Cost logged: palm laceration, lost wedge. Behavioral change: additional braces mandated on Cut locks within next ten rounds. Filed Couriers’ Bell, Work Order 2043\.

**Concrete image:** A line of calm gray water lapping at the bent iron gate, steam twisting upward while a red handprint dries against the stone beside the home ring.

# **Chapter 6: Corlexi’s Fire**

Bell Twelve rolled flat over the roofs, muffled by mist and smoke. Corlexi stood in the market square with both hands in the ash bin, pulling placards out one by one. Each card bore the old single-brace symbol: two wedges facing inward, out of date by one Bell. Every lock failure that week had carried that mark. Policy changed this morning. Someone had to burn the proof.

He held the first card by its corner. The wax Tempermark bled blue when the heat touched it, curling into itself like muscle recoiling. The stamp read *Greymantle Cut—Work Order 2043*. He remembered the red handprint rumor before he saw the stain burned into the ink edge. “Lucky he didn’t drown,” he muttered, and dropped it into the flame.

A group of apprentices watched from the steps, carrying fresh boards. They whispered to each other, eyes on the burn box. “Double brace rule?” one asked.

Corlexi didn’t look up. “Double brace rule. Within ten rounds.”

The fire spat once. Wax smell thickened. The second placard carried a name he knew—Maren hilt, a good setter who’d cracked a knee when a brace failed two months ago. He held it longer before letting it fall.

On the far side of the square, the Couriers’ Bell office was already laying out the new boards. Ink crews worked off a folding table, stamping wedge glyphs two-by-two, copper heads hot enough to hiss when they touched wood. The pattern had changed overnight: old inward braces replaced by paired diagonals, more metal, more safety.

“Quicker this way?” an apprentice asked.

“No,” Corlexi said. “Slower, safer.”

He fed the third placard into the bin. The crowd near the food stalls had gone quiet, watching the smoke. It wasn’t ceremony; it was relief disguised as boredom.

A Conclave clerk crossed the square with a basket of new seals. “Posting priority corners first,” he said, voice careful. “The Conclave wants public compliance before dusk.”

Corlexi wiped sweat from his cheek with the back of his arm. “They always do.”

The clerk hesitated, holding a placard halfway. “You were there for Lowcourt, right?”

“Doesn’t matter where I was.” He took the placard and set it into the fire. “Now I’m here.”

The last of the old signs turned to smoke. He raked the ashes with a long iron rod until the glyphs broke apart. Behind him, the apprentices lifted the new boards, carried them toward the market posts, the stairwells, the corners by the canal. Every new mark would show two wedges instead of one.

A teamster paused his cart to watch. “Another rule already?”

Corlexi turned, voice even. “One that keeps your wheels on the ground.”

The man tipped his cap and moved on.

Corlexi knelt by the ashes, scooped a handful into a small cloth bag, tied it tight. “Witness residue,” he said to no one, writing the phrase on a slip. Burned rules needed proof they’d died.

By the time the Bell rang half, the square had changed color. The old blue of single braces had gone gray in the soot, replaced by fresh copper and paint that hadn’t yet dulled. Children traced the new glyphs with their fingers as they passed.

From the east alley came a gust of steam—pipe vent clearing from Alderline’s repair. Corlexi looked up through it, saw the faint shadow of the Conclave tower against the fog. The world was always patching itself faster than people could keep up.

An apprentice returned from the posting run, breath quick. “Every corner’s marked. Last one’s drying by the pump road.”

Corlexi nodded. “Good. When it rains, the wax will tell us which ones hold.”

“What if one doesn’t?”

“Then we trace it again,” he said, and stood. His knees popped. “That’s what work is. Do it right until right becomes habit.”

The clerk from the Conclave sealed the record page with the last of his ink. “Policy change filed: all Cut locks to carry double brace before Bell Twenty-two. Witness Corlexi, Market Station.”

He signed his name small, a single curl at the end. “Filed,” he said.

The clerk hesitated. “You burn them clean.”

“Everything burns clean if you give it enough air,” he said. “Even bad policy.”

The clerk left with the ledger. The apprentices lingered, unsure if they were dismissed. Corlexi pointed at the fresh boards stacked by the rail. “Post two more at the bridge ends before Bell Thirteen. People forget fast.”

They moved, boots thudding.

When the square cleared, Corlexi sat on the edge of the ash bin. The fire had gone to coals, orange and low. He held one unburned corner of an old placard, tracing the single-wedge mark with his thumb. It wasn’t wrong when they made it; it was just small. The city had outgrown small.

He tossed it into the coals and waited for the wax to hiss.

**Public artifact:** **CONCLAVE POLICY UPDATE — Bell 12** Effective immediately: all lock braces within Te’Oga to be doubled, twin-wedge configuration mandatory. Previous single-brace seals void. Compliance verification by Couriers’ Bell inspectors within ten rounds.

**Concrete image:** A square gone gray with ash, new copper placards catching steam-light, and the faint smell of burned wax rising into the fog while the city pretends it was always built this way.

# **Chapter 7: Double Fault at Rindle Yard**

The Thread was already broken when they got there, hanging like split rope from the upper seam. Bell Two had barely cleared; the air smelled of burnt iron and shame.

Jhace dropped to one knee beside the fault. The brace plates were wrong—the old single set, bent inward, paint flaked. Someone had patched over the crack with tar and hope.

“Double rule went out last Bell,” Tiffani said, standing over him. “This is still single.”

“Work Order’s dated yesterday.” He peeled a tag from the post. The Tempermark was smudged. “Filed before the change.”

“Doesn’t make it safe.”

He ran a finger along the line. “Who set it?”

“Rindle yard foreman signed it. Kesh Hurn.”

“Figures.” He tapped the seam; dull echo. “He braced shallow, didn’t backfill the groove.”

She knelt beside him, Lume low. “You want to condemn it?”

He looked at the carts queued behind them—grain wagons, two bells late already. “Condemn and they riot.”

“Leave it and it collapses.”

He didn’t answer. Instead he unpacked a wet bladder and copper nib. “We do what we can.”

Tiffani hissed through her teeth. “Against policy.”

He met her eyes. “Against dying, too.”

The first wedge refused the slot. He turned it, shaved it down with his knife, pressed again. The metal gave half a sound, neither yes nor no.

“Angle’s off,” she said.

“Off by one degree, maybe.”

“Enough.”

He pulled it free, cut it flatter. This time it bit. The seam breathed once.

“Trace it,” she said.

He set the wet line, slow and deliberate. The copper hissed. Steam lifted, thin and bright. Half-beat Slake. His hand trembled.

“You’re bleeding through the wrap,” she said.

“Not enough to matter.”

“You always say that.”

The Thread shuddered under the deck. A deep hum rolled through the plates—wrong tone, heavy. Jhace froze.

“Too hot,” she said. “You’re feeding it too fast.”

He lifted the nib. The hum eased but didn’t stop. “Backflow from the lower vein.”

She angled the Lume light along the seam, found a hairline split running off left. “That’s not ours. That’s old metal.”

“Then we’re chasing ghosts.”

“Ghosts kill slower, but they still kill.”

Behind them, Brighlee jogged down the path, hair damp with fog, ledger in hand. “Double-brace inspection,” she said. “Conclave sent it early.”

“Tell them they’re late,” Tiffani said.

Brighlee saw the fault and went pale. “This isn’t regulation.”

“No kidding.” Jhace drove another wedge. “Give me one Bell before you post.”

“Policy says immediate.”

“Policy wasn’t knee-deep in this mess.”

Mel Quarr arrived half-running, coat half-buttoned. “What’s wrong?”

“Old work,” Tiffani said. “Single brace, cracked thread, wrong feed.”

Mel crouched, laid her palm to the plate. The hum pulsed through her arm. “That’s unstable.”

Jhace didn’t look up. “We know.”

“Then step back.”

“No time.”

She reached for the trace line. “You’ll burn it.”

He caught her wrist. “Then I’ll burn it steady.”

Mel held his stare a beat too long, then pulled free. “You think you can fix this?”

“I think it needs a witness.”

She almost smiled. “You’ll either save it or make it worse.”

“That’s most of my work.”

He adjusted the line, traced again, slower. The wet beaded along the crack, steam rising. The hum dropped half a tone.

Tiffani exhaled. “That’s better.”

“Not done.” He pressed a second wedge. The first shifted. “Damn it—”

The seam snapped open a hair. Water hissed.

Mel shouted, “Step back\!”

He didn’t. He shoved his shoulder against the plate, jammed the wedge deeper. The hiss stopped. The Thread steadied, faint and even.

“Now?” Tiffani asked.

Mel pressed her hand to the seam again. “Still live, but not rising.”

“Enough to clear?”

“Maybe.”

“Maybe?” Jhace’s jaw clenched.

Mel waited. One more heartbeat. “Now.”

He pulled back, soaked through. Tiffani moved fast, laying two Shade 1 skins across the seam, sealing the new double brace. The water cooled; the air turned metallic and sweet.

Brighlee scribbled furiously. “Posting conditional repair pending Conclave audit.”

“Don’t call it conditional,” Jhace said.

“Then call it brave,” she muttered.

He almost laughed.

Mel stood, brushing grit off her knees. “You saved it,” she said.

“Temporarily.”

“Nothing’s permanent.” She looked toward the queue. “Tell the carts they can pass, slow.”

Tiffani lifted her Lume toward the post. “Witness?”

Mel hesitated, hand above the ledger. “The policy’s new. This fix breaks it.”

“Then mark it as precedent,” Jhace said.

Mel studied the line another long moment, then nodded. “Witness.” She signed, slow and deliberate.

The carts started rolling again, wheels creaking, drivers staring down at the fresh seal as they passed. Nobody spoke.

Jhace cleaned his tools, blood streaking the wet cloth. His glove was torn; skin raw at the palm. “Cost?” Tiffani asked.

“Grip’s gone again.”

“Clinic.”

“Policy.”

He managed a smile that didn’t last. “They’ll quote it on my grave.”

She met his eyes. “You keep fixing things that should be replaced.”

“Habit.”

Mel turned away, already writing. “Habit saves cities,” she said over her shoulder. “Until it doesn’t.”

They packed up. The light shifted as fog lifted, revealing the new double-brace gleaming dull under water sheen.

**Public artifact:** **RINDLE YARD—DOUBLE FAULT — Bell 2–3** Unauthorized repair conducted under prior single-brace configuration. Emergency double-wedge applied and Slaked. Thread stabilized under witness. Temporary variance logged pending Conclave review. Witness: Mel Quarr. Signatories: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown). Cost: torn palm, material loss one wedge. Precedent established: immediate correction permitted under superseded policy when delay risks collapse.

**Concrete image:** Steam thinned to reveal the new brace shining wet, two wedges crossing like a scar that chose to hold.

# **Chapter 8: The HALT at Narrowvale Rise**

Bell Five hit mid-echo across the Crown. The air changed pitch. Blue banners flared at every post from Bridgegate to the Vale, the signal that stopped carts and silenced every forge at once. **HALT ORDER: STRUCTURAL RISK.**

Jhace reached the rise at a run, lungs raw, coat half-soaked from the night before. The flag snapped above the street, light cutting through fog. A ten-span arch in the rise had cracked. Half the Upper Market’s weight ran through that bridge.

Tiffani stood with both hands braced on the guard rail, eyes on the fissure widening between plates. “It’s pulling apart along the seam. Two Veins, opposite vectors.”

He knelt, tracing with his glove. The vibration hit his bones. “Feels like a mis-thread in the Crown feed. Someone over-bound it.”

“Conclave techs said the same. They bailed.”

“They left mid-HALT?”

“They said *consultation complete.*”

He almost laughed, short and humorless. “We’re it, then.”

Brighlee’s voice carried from the post above. “Bell Five to Bell Nine window. Full HALT. No reopen without witness clearance. Work order three-three-eight.” She clipped the paper to the board; the crowd pulled back two meters on instinct.

“Half the market’s watching,” Tiffani said.

“Good.” Jhace pulled the cover from his kit. “They can remember what this costs.”

The crack ran three spans, straight and deep, the kind that invited collapse quietly before it screamed. Steam leaked from the lower seam—heat from the feed lines bleeding out.

Tiffani lowered her Lume, adjusted the Shade until the light bounced back dull orange. “The left vein’s dry. No Threft movement. Right one’s alive.”

“Dead and live next to each other,” he said. “It’ll shear itself trying to breathe.”

“Then we trace them both. Marry them at midpoint.”

He nodded, set his bladder, wet the copper, touched nib to plate. The left line refused the water; it pooled and ran.

“It’s rejecting,” Tiffani said.

He pressed harder. “Come on.”

Steam hissed. The water sizzled, scattered into droplets. The line stayed cold.

She frowned. “You’re wasting pressure.”

He wiped the plate. “Try heat first. Lume five.”

She rolled the Shade open. Light flared. Metal glowed dull red. Jhace pressed again. This time the wet hissed once, then ran straight.

“Got it.”

“Right side,” she said.

He shifted over. The live vein responded instantly, water sucking inward too fast. The line flared white.

“Kill it\!” she snapped.

He jerked the bladder back, burned his knuckles on the copper. The seam spat steam, a sharp pop following. Metal jumped under his palm.

“Vein bit,” he said, grim.

“It’ll climb if we don’t split the feed.”

“Brighlee\! Drop flow from the mid-valve\!”

“Already flagged\!”

Jhace caught his breath. “Give me the wedge. Two-inch. No, the hard grain.”

Tiffani handed it over, eyes never leaving the line. “Angle eight degrees.”

He drove the wedge into the plate edge, hammering until the sound stopped echoing. The crack trembled, steadied.

Tiffani checked the light. “Pulse even?”

“Barely.”

She leaned close, voice low. “If it jumps again, we run.”

He smiled through grit. “Noted.”

She set a second wedge, lower. The ground under them thudded twice. Distant—pipes reacting.

“Feed shift,” Jhace said. “It’s adjusting upstream.”

“Then we finish before it notices.”

He wet the line again, smaller run bridging the two veins. The bead shimmered, sweat and steam in his eyes.

“Shade three,” she said. “Keep it honest.”

“Honest,” he echoed.

The hum under their knees deepened, unified, as if both veins found each other and decided not to fight.

Tiffani exhaled, relief half-earned. “Married.”

“Seal it.”

She pulled a Shade 2 skin, pressed it over the joint, smoothing edges with her thumb. “Annealshift zero.”

“Do it.”

She breathed heat across it until it dulled, then tapped the corners seven times. The air changed tone again—low, whole.

Mel Quarr appeared at the top of the rise, coat soaked, hair stuck to her face. “Talk to me.”

“Joined feed,” Jhace said. “Trace holding.”

She stepped down, crouched by the seam. “Both lines live?”

“Equalized.”

She pressed her ear to the plate. A pause, long enough for every worker watching to stop moving. “It’s steady,” she said finally. “For now.”

“For now gets carts moving.”

“Not until I sign.” She pulled her pen.

The ground rumbled again, small but rising. Everyone froze. The seam under her hand pulsed once, hard.

Mel’s pen stopped. “That wasn’t feed. That was expansion.”

“Backflow,” Tiffani said. “Pressure building from the Crown line.”

Jhace threw his kit open, grabbed the release valve wrench. “We vent it.”

“You open that without brace, you’ll shear the plate\!” Mel barked.

“Then we brace it\!” He drove another wedge, deeper, metal ringing like struck bells.

Tiffani grabbed the Lume, cut light to zero, then reopened to full to check feedback. “Still rising\!”

Jhace jammed the wrench on the valve. “Three turns\!”

Mel shouted over the hiss, “Half-turn only\!”

“Three\!”

He spun it once, twice. The seam screamed, then sighed—long, hollow. Steam shot six feet into the air, burning everything in reach.

Tiffani flinched back, gloved hands up. Jhace’s left sleeve caught moisture that scalded through the fabric.

Then the sound shifted: the deep, low exhale of weight finding balance.

Mel crouched again, pressed her palm to the plate. “Pressure’s gone. It’s breathing normal.”

Jhace sat back, shaking his burned arm. “We’re clear?”

She nodded. “HALT lifts at Bell Nine.”

Brighlee’s voice cracked across the rise: “Witnessed\!”

Tiffani let out a sound halfway between a laugh and a gasp. “You’re an idiot.”

“Functional idiot,” he said.

“Still counts.”

She took his wrist, rolled the sleeve up. Skin blistered pink. “Clinic, now.”

He nodded once, too tired to argue.

Mel stood, wrote the clearance in her ledger with hands still shaking. “I’m signing this one myself.”

She posted the slip under the blue banner. The paper soaked through but held. “Policy note: overbind feed must split before marriage. Write that down, Brighlee.”

Brighlee scribbled. “Noted.”

Carts began to move again, slow, careful. The crowd dispersed with the reverent silence only near-misses earn.

Jhace stayed seated, staring at the line. “We nearly lost it.”

Tiffani sat beside him, shoulder to shoulder. “We didn’t.”

Her hand brushed his burned one, deliberate, firm. His breathing evened. That was enough.

**Public artifact:** **NARROWVALE RISE HALT — Bells 5–9** Full structural shutdown under emergency order. Dual-vein overbind corrected via manual trace and vent. Witnessed clearance granted by Conclave Setter Mel Quarr. Signatories: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown). Policy update: overbind feeds require split pre-marriage; HALT protocols enforce local brace confirmation before vent. Costs: burn (left forearm, superficial), tool loss (one wrench warped).

**Concrete image:** Blue banners snapping wet in the cooling air while steam curled around a single blackened glove abandoned by the valve, the bridge breathing slow again beneath it.

# **Chapter 9: Slow Bell at Mirecourt**

Bell Ten dragged like a drunk cart. Nothing wanted to move. The fog was the thick kind that collected sweat from anyone dumb enough to work in it. Jhace hadn’t slept since Narrowvale. His burned arm still stung, wrapped tight under his sleeve.

The summons had come anyway. *Minor leak, Mirecourt span.* Nothing in the city was ever minor.

He and Tiffani reached the gate face to find condensation dripping from the inner rail, slow and steady. Not a break, not yet. The sound of it was worse—a hollow *tick, tick* between the Bells like a wound that couldn’t decide if it was open.

Tiffani crouched. Her face was gray from sleeplessness. “Overbind residue. Narrowvale runoff hit the lower feeds. Pressure’s wrong.”

He set his tool pack down, slower than usual. His hand didn’t want to close around the latch. “We shouldn’t be working.”

She didn’t look up. “You say that every time you’re bleeding.”

“Not every time.”

“Name one you didn’t.”

He didn’t answer.

Brighlee trudged up the incline, eyes ringed dark. “Work order four-eight-one. Leak containment, Mirecourt. Bell Ten to Bell Eleven.” Her voice rasped. “No witness posted yet.”

“Fine,” Tiffani said. “We’ll make one when it matters.”

Jhace pulled his glove tighter, swallowed the dry taste in his mouth. “You handle trace. I’ll brace.”

“Arm still burned?”

“It moves.”

“Not the same as healed.”

“Closer than dead.”

She sighed, then crouched beside the line. “One hand on home.”

He found the anchor post. “One hand on home.”

The first wedge slipped from his fingers. Too slick. He wiped his palm on his coat, tried again. The second bit.

“Shallow leak,” Tiffani said, eyes on the Lume. “Midline pulse, four counts low.”

He could feel it through the plate—a heartbeat out of rhythm.

“Half-beat Slake,” she said.

He nodded. His movements were slower now, the kind that come when muscles haven’t slept. The copper line glowed faintly wet; his reflection in it looked like someone older.

She whispered counts as he drew. “One. Two. Half.”

The wet hissed. The seam brightened, steadied. The tick stopped.

“Got it,” he said.

She didn’t answer.

He looked up. Her face had gone blank. The Lume slipped from her hand, rolled once, hit the stone.

“Tiff.”

Nothing. Her eyes blinked once, slow. “Shade wrong,” she murmured. “Four instead of two.”

“Too hot.”

She nodded, dazed. “I fixed it.”

He reached out, steadying her wrist. Her pulse jumped under his thumb.

“Stay,” he said.

She inhaled, slow, and color returned to her cheeks. “I’m fine.”

“Liar.”

“Better liar than corpse.”

Brighlee stood back, watching like she wasn’t sure if she should record or intervene. “Do you want me to—”

“Keep writing,” Tiffani said.

They moved in silence for a few minutes, fixing what the last sleepless shift had left crooked. The line dried evenly.

“You sure the leak’s sealed?” Jhace asked.

Tiffani lifted the Lume, checked the feedback. “Trace reads steady. If it fails, it’ll do it politely.”

“Polite’s new.”

“Maybe the city’s learning.”

He sat back against the post, wiping sweat with his good sleeve. “You’re shaking.”

“So are you.”

“Then we match.”

She smiled, faint but there. “Don’t make poetry out of exhaustion.”

“Just naming what’s real.”

Mel Quarr appeared at the top of the ramp, looking as tired as the rest. “Report.”

“Minor leak, sealed,” Tiffani said. “No HALT. Feed equalized.”

Mel knelt, pressed her hand to the plate. “It’s holding.” She looked at Jhace’s arm. “You working injured again?”

“Policy says clinic after shift.”

“Policy also says don’t die for copper.”

He shrugged. “I’m following half.”

Mel stood, scrawled her signature. “Witnessed.” She hesitated before clipping the slip. “You two need rest more than the city needs another repair.”

“The city disagrees,” Tiffani said.

Mel gave her a look that was almost pity. “The city always disagrees. That’s why we have clinics.”

Brighlee pinned the form to the board, ink smudged from damp air. “Work order four-eight-one complete. Bell Eleven.”

“Costs?” Mel asked.

“Burn reopening,” Jhace said.

“Dehydration,” Tiffani added.

Mel wrote them both, slow. “Clinic. I’ll send a slip.”

They didn’t argue this time.

When the others left, Jhace stayed a minute longer. The bridge beneath them breathed in slow rhythms, steel contracting with cold.

Tiffani leaned against the rail beside him. “You’re bleeding through the wrap.”

He glanced down. A small patch of red, barely showing. “That’s proof I’m alive.”

She leaned her head against the same rail, eyes half-closed. “Alive’s overrated.”

He smiled. “Then we’ll be mediocre together.”

She didn’t laugh, but her shoulder brushed his. His tremor eased. That counted.

They left the Lume burning low on the post, light catching the still wet line like a thin scar that refused to fade.

**Public artifact:** **MIRECOURT SPAN — Bell 10–11** Minor leak containment. Feed imbalance from Narrowvale runoff corrected via manual trace. No HALT required. Witness: Mel Quarr (Conclave). Signatories: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown). Costs: reopened burn, dehydration, Shade misapplication. Policy note: consecutive shifts exceeding four Bells require mandatory clinic rest.

**Concrete image:** The bridge hums under fog, a slow, tired rhythm while a thin red patch darkens the wrap around a setter’s hand and the light of the forgotten Lume flickers against steel gone cold.

# **Chapter 10: Mobel at the Table**

Bell Two chimed hollow through the Mercers’ Quarter, each note sliding through the lattice rafters above the weighing floor. Mobel had been there since dawn, coat off, sleeves rolled, the kind of tired that doesn’t show until it stops moving.

The scales in front of him were old Crown brass, polished to dull gold. They didn’t lie often, but they could be coaxed. The night auditors had caught three weight sets marked light in the middle coin. Not enough to hang a merchant, enough to make the Conclave twitch.

He placed one suspect weight on the pan, thumbed the notch along its rim. “Filed too deep,” he said to his clerk. “Half-grain shave.”

The clerk squinted. “Still legal?”

Mobel didn’t answer. He flicked the weight off the pan. It hit the floor with a hollow ring. “Not when it starts breeding.”

The smell of old oil filled the air as apprentices cleaned their scales. Every trade table in the room carried its own seal—the droplet for Couriers, the hammer for Forgewrights, the two crossed wedges newly added for Locks and Loads. That mark had cost Jhace a burn and half the city a rulebook rewrite.

He checked the next weight. The brass surface bore faint scrape lines, deliberate. He turned it over and read the faint initials carved into the base. “Harnet.”

The clerk’s eyes widened. “He’s senior.”

“Was,” Mobel said. “Post the charge.”

The clerk hesitated. “Sir, it’s only half a grain—”

Mobel’s voice stayed even. “Half a grain turns into famine when multiplied. Post it.”

The clerk went pale but moved, writing the notice on a strip of wax paper. Mobel pulled the ink stamp from his belt, Tempermarked the sheet, and clipped it to the board above the scales. The room stilled. Every merchant in sight turned toward the sound.

“Official?” one called.

“Filed under Bell Two,” Mobel said.

Someone muttered, “Conclave doesn’t usually bother with half-grains.”

“They do now.”

He reached for the next weight, turned it, sniffed the faint tang of fresh polish. Too clean. He scraped a knife edge across it. Bright line. “Fresh file,” he said.

A Forgewright approached, face dark with anger. “We recalibrate weekly. No harm in keeping tools smooth.”

Mobel held up the knife. “Smooth isn’t the same as light.” He balanced the weight against a verified measure. The pan dipped slow, traitorous. “See?”

The forgewright looked away. “Barely a whisper.”

Mobel nodded once, calm. “That whisper just cost you your table.”

He wrote the notice himself, no clerk this time: *Forgery of official weights, Harnet Forge Cooperative. License revoked pending audit. Seals to be melted public.*

He signed it, pressed the Tempermark, and handed it to the clerk. “Fire the furnace.”

The apprentices dragged a small crucible from the side wall, set it on the coals, pumped the bellows until the air shimmered. The smell of heating metal cut through the damp.

Mobel took the forged weights one by one, dropped them into the pot. They sizzled as moisture burned off, then sagged into liquid. The room filled with quiet except for the hiss of betrayal cooking.

The Forgewright spoke again, voice shaking. “You’re making a show of this.”

Mobel didn’t look up. “No. A record.”

“Same thing.”

He stirred the molten brass with a rod, watching the color shift from yellow to red to dull silver. “Show fades. Record stays.”

When the last of the fake weights had gone under, he tilted the crucible slightly, poured the liquid into a flat mold shaped like a wedge. “New symbol,” he said. “Half-grain truth.”

The clerk stared. “What do we do with it?”

“Post it on the Table.”

They nailed the cooled wedge to the central board between the trade seals, the scarred metal still faintly warm. It read: *Mass must match mark. Deviance voids claim.*

Mobel looked around the room. No one met his eyes. “Back to work,” he said.

The merchants dispersed. The clatter of weights returned, but softer, careful now.

When the last Bell of morning rang, the clerk handed him the day’s ledger. “Three confessions filed already,” she said. “Two from vendors who weren’t even questioned.”

Mobel nodded, too tired for satisfaction. “Guilt travels faster than ink.”

He sat for a while after the room cleared, listening to the slow tick of cooling metal. He wasn’t proud. Just done. He wiped his hands on his coat, left the burned smell for the next shift to inherit.

Outside, a new placard hung on the Mercers’ archway—one he hadn’t ordered but approved by existing rule. It carried Corlexi’s twin-wedge symbol above the words *DOUBLE CHECK. DOUBLE PAY.* He smiled once, small, then vanished into the street crowd before anyone could think it was approval.

**Public artifact:** **MERCERS’ TABLE AUDIT — Bell 2–4** Forgery of trade weights confirmed. Harnet Forge Cooperative stripped of license. Counterfeit measures melted into single “Half-Grain Wedge,” displayed publicly at Mercers’ Table. Policy expansion: daily double verification of all Conclave-approved measures.

**Concrete image:** A single wedge of cooled brass nailed to the trade board, edges uneven, letters burned in by hand—heat ghosting through the metal while honest weights tick in cautious rhythm around it.

# **Chapter 11: Vein Drift at Old Barrick**

Bell Six began with rain that refused to quit. Every gutter in Old Barrick bled the same brown water, the kind that carried grit sharp enough to chew soles. Jhace trudged through it anyway, coat soaked, mind half-asleep. The summons had read simple: *Feed drift reported beneath Barrick arch. Confirm Vein integrity.* Simple meant dangerous.

Tiffani waited under the arch with her case open and her patience closed. “Feed’s running wrong,” she said as soon as he got close. “Map shows three lines. I’m reading four.”

He crouched beside her, wiped water from his eyes. The metal under them hummed soft but uneven. “Someone’s been splicing.”

“Unauthorized?”

“Unauthorized, stupid, or both.”

Brighlee set her ledger box on a dry patch—there weren’t many—and unfolded the Conclave diagram. “Official routing stops at Bell Yard,” she said. “This fourth line isn’t on record.”

Tiffani’s Lume flicked against the steel face. “There. New weld, bad angle. You can still smell the resin.”

Jhace touched the seam; it flexed under pressure. “Not Veinwright work.”

“Then whose?”

He looked up toward the row of cheap workshops hanging off the arch face. “Barrick guild leases to anyone with a torch and a prayer.”

“Then we pray faster,” she said.

The hum deepened. The ground gave a little under their knees.

“Shut down feed before it chews through the arch.”

Brighlee fumbled the comm key. “Conclave control, Barrick line—Vein drift, unregistered splice, request partial bleed—”

The line popped once, dead.

“Signal’s out,” she said.

Jhace stood, scanning the arch. “Manual cut then. Valve’s three meters under street. Get me the pry.”

Tiffani handed it over, already lowering the Lume toward the street joint. Water rushed around their boots, gray with oil.

He wedged the bar between plates, pulled. The valve cap lifted half a finger. “Blocked.”

“Rust?”

“No, resin. Whoever spliced this sealed the control with the same filth.”

Tiffani exhaled, long and angry. “They overbound to siphon flow. You can see the heat scar from a fake Tempermark.”

He followed her gaze. The symbol was there, crooked, shallow.

“Mercers again?”

“Looks cheaper than that.”

The hum turned into a shudder, steady and wrong.

“Brace it,” she said.

“I can’t—valve’s stuck.”

She grabbed her Veil case, ripped out a Shade skin, slapped it over the joint. “It won’t hold long.”

“Long’s relative.” He pried again. The bar slipped. His burned hand screamed. He bit back sound and drove it again.

The valve gave, half-turn. A rush of hot air vented out, carrying the stink of burnt polymer.

“Feed drop?” he asked.

Tiffani watched the Lume gauge. “Down ten. Still high.”

“Second turn.”

He forced the bar, pain flaring through his arm. “That’s it. Thirty percent bleed.”

The hum began to level. The Veil over the joint quivered but held.

She crouched beside it. “Someone built this line to feed an unlisted forge.”

He wiped water from his face. “Where?”

“Follow the warmth.” She pressed her palm to the stone. Heat traced north. “Two spans up. Under that shop with the green door.”

“Of course it’s painted green,” he muttered.

They slogged through ankle water to the door. The forge inside was dark but hot. Tools scattered. The feed pipe split into a small coupling feeding a single crucible. The floor was scorched.

“Illegal tap,” Tiffani said.

“Improper angle, wrong alloys, counterfeit mark,” Jhace said. “It’s a miracle they didn’t melt half the street.”

“Still might.”

He reached for his trace stick, pulled a wet line across the pipe joint. Steam hissed. The fake weld separated cleanly, exposing the stolen vein.

“Cut?” she asked.

“Cut.” He drove a plug into the open end and Slaked it. The pipe groaned and cooled.

Brighlee ducked in, soaked through. “Control restored. They felt the bleed upstream.”

“Good.” Jhace pointed at the crucible. “Mark it for confiscation.”

Mel Quarr entered from the alley door, expression unreadable. “You two have a talent for finding trouble.”

“Trouble builds itself,” Tiffani said. “We just trip over it first.”

Mel knelt by the severed pipe, ran a finger along the cut. “Clean. You trace that in a flood?”

Jhace nodded. “Twice.”

“You’ll get your name in a policy again if you keep fixing other people’s greed.”

He didn’t answer.

Mel stood, signed Brighlee’s page. “Forge closed, feed sealed. Witnessed.”

Brighlee clipped the paper to the doorpost, ink bleeding in the wet.

Mel looked up at the glowing arch above them, light bending off rain. “You know, this city’s not tired because it breaks. It’s tired because it has to hide the breaks before anyone sees.”

Tiffani said quietly, “Then we keep hiding them.”

Mel gave a thin smile. “That’s why you’re still alive.”

Jhace leaned against the wall, exhaustion visible even through grit. “For now.”

Tiffani caught his sleeve. “Stay on your feet. Clinic next.”

He nodded. “Policy.”

The rain thickened again, washing soot from the door. The counterfeit Tempermark softened and slid down the stone, leaving a streak that looked almost like a tear.

**Public artifact:** **OLD BARRICK VEIN DRIFT — Bell 6–8** Unauthorized splice discovered beneath Barrick arch. Counterfeit Tempermark identified and excised. Feed bleed and rebalancing performed under flood conditions. Witness: Mel Quarr (Conclave). Signatories: Jhace brast (Underface), Tiffani veld (Outer Crown). Costs: burn strain (rehash), fatigue, tool loss (bar bent). Policy update: random valve inspections extended to Lower and Middle Barrick guild leases; unregistered feeds subject

# Hotfix Patch: Grindheim (1).md

## A) Global find/replace (strict)

1) Music verbs → neutral mechanics  
- Find (case-insensitive, whole word): `hum|hums|hummed|humming|sing|sings|sang|sung|singing|hymn|chorus|choir`  
- Replace with context-appropriate: `steady|even|level|low|quiet|pulse|carry|run`  
  - Use “run even” for lines, “carry a steady pulse” for structures, “low” or “quiet” for ambience.

2) Nuke ledger headings inside prose  
- Find paragraphs starting with: `**Public artifact:**|**Concrete image:**`  
- Action: move them out of the narrative block. Append them after a blank line under a private working heading `Continuity:` (your manuscript only; they do not print in-story).

3) Ritual explanations that begin with Because  
- Find: sentences starting with `Because ` that explain a rule.  
- Action: delete or convert to a physical result (heat, pressure change, weight shift).

### Chapter 9

## C) Brighlee discipline (apply per chapter)

Pick **one** mode per scene and stick to it:

- **Functional mode:** only ops words: `Posted.` `Witnessed.` `Bell nine.` `Filed.` Plus the physical act (clip, seal, lift).  
- **Human-beat mode:** allow exactly **one** aside (mutter, eye-roll, a dry one-liner), then pure function.

If she wobbles, cut back to Functional.

---

## D) HALT hygiene

- Exactly **one** full HALT in N1 (Narrowvale).  
- Any other stoppages are **local traffic holds** or **partial pauses**. Wording: “local stop,” “partial hold,” “brace the lane,” never “HALT.”

- First operation ≤ 250 words  
- Wrap ≤ 200 words  
- 900–1500 words total  
- End every chapter on: posted artifact + single concrete image.  
  - In manuscript: put those under `Continuity:` not in-scene.

## F) Consent template (don’t editorialize)

- Pattern: a clear ask + visible physical result.  
  - Example kept:  
    - `"Stay," she said.`  
    - *Her hand warmed his sleeve; his tremor stopped.*  
- No follow-up commentary about consent. You’ve shown it.

```

- POV: Jhace or Tiffani only; interludes ≤ 120 words; no interiority outside POV.
```

{"name":"Grindheim (1) — Clean Manuscript (Prologue + Ch.1–15, Continuity blocks)","type":"document","content":"# Prologue — The Year the World Took a Breath\n\nThe world ended once and decided it was too much trouble to stay dead.\nIt didn’t break apart; it flexed, like a lung taking air after being punched.\nA pull went through the crust—straight, fast, absolute—and stopped just short of tearing clean through.\nWhat it left was a tunnel with no opposite mouth.\nPeople called it the **Bore**, because naming the wound makes it sound finished.\n\nFrom the Upper Crown, it looks like an even circle of shadow on maps.\nFrom the Underface, it’s a black horizon that presses in your teeth when the air gets heavy.\nNobody pretends it’s a mystery anymore. We live around it, not above it.\nEvery crack, every bridge, every load-bearing inch answers to what the Bore changed.\n\nIt began with water taking new routes.\nRivers turned, cliffs sagged, docks listed without warning.\nFor a year the wind came up from the ground instead of the sky,\nand you could smell iron on your skin no matter how often you washed.\nPeople called it divine; the rest of us called it gravity.\nWhen the air stopped shaking, a handful of us were still standing,\nand we started measuring before we started praying.\n\nWe found the veins by accident.\nThey ran under the ground like nerves under skin,\nthin lines where force preferred to travel.\nWhen the veins slipped out of their old paths,\nthey bled heat and pressure and made **Harrow**—\nthe kind of rupture that doesn’t care if you’re watching.\nA few people, tired of being erased, learned to touch the veins and guide them.\nThose hands became **Veinwrights**.\nTheir work became the city’s language:\n**Trace. Set. Bridge. Veil. Slake. Tempermark.**\n\nThe Bore taught one rule that never stopped mattering:\neverything carries stress, and stress remembers.\nYou can’t erase a fault; you can only give it a safer way to move.\nThat’s what the craft is—negotiation between material and will.\nEvery building stands because someone convinced it not to fall.\n\nSix months after the pull, the first bridges started breathing.\nAt night you could see vapor lifting from the joints like ghost breath.\nNo one remembers laying the mains that kept them warm,\nbut the warmth stayed.\nIt rose through basements and clinics, through pipes that had no valves,\nthrough the Conclave halls where the Veinwrights kept their ledgers.\nThe city learned to live with the heat as a fact, not a gift.\nIt kept people alive in winter, and it kept them honest—\nbecause if a line failed, everyone nearby heard it.\n\nWhen the air cooled, the Bells came.\nNot temple chimes—work signals.\nLong clear rings for reopen,\ntwo short for brace,\nfast beats for Harrow risk.\nEach Bell came with a board stamped in copper and sealed in glass.\nYou could tell a city’s health by how clean its Tempermarks were.\n\nThe first posted rule was ugly and perfect:\n**One hand on the home ring.**\nHold something that won’t move while you touch what might.\nIt was less philosophy, more survival.\n\nThen came the rest,\nwritten by people who’d already bled once for the knowledge:\n\n— Trace wet. Dry lines please eyes and lie to Veins.\n— Wedge before Veil. Cool the draft, then the glass.\n— Half-beat Slake.\n— Countermark cooler than birth. Pride bends things wrong.\n— No White.\n— Brace taps only in Bells Two and Three. You are not special in Four.\n— If it isn’t on the board, it didn’t happen.\n\nThey’re not prayers. They’re methods.\nWatch a street when a blue **HALT** goes up and every hammer stops midair.\nWatch the crowd hold breath until the setter witness signs the clear.\nThat’s how cities stay alive—by honoring pause more than noise.\n\nWe forbid **Overbind**, because a clean disaster still kills you.\nWe forbid **Whitequench**, because a lie in glass cuts deeper than heat.\nWe measure by what doesn’t fail, not by what shines.\n\nAfter the Bore, Bells multiplied.\nEvery trade learned its rhythm.\nThe Couriers’ Bell marked motion; the Conclave’s marked repair.\nYou could walk a full day and never be out of hearing range.\nIt was noise that meant survival—call, answer, repeat.\n\nSix hundred and twelve rounds later,\nTe’Oga still leans a fraction toward the Bore.\nMaps mark the tilt with a single diagonal line through the city’s name.\nChildren in the Underface draw circles in ash to guess the next drift.\nCrown engineers add decimal places to comfort themselves.\nNeither side is wrong.\n\nThe Bore doesn’t threaten anymore. It just exists.\nWe’ve made a kind of peace with the wound:\nkeep it measured, keep it known, keep it held.\n\nWork here isn’t sacred. It’s necessary.\nThe Conclave says **“Trace wet.”**\nThe Couriers answer, **“One hand on home.”**\nTogether, they make the city stay where it is.\n\nIf you need a prayer, you use your hands:\n**Trace wet. Wedge before Veil. Slake on the half-beat. Tempermark what you promise. Post it.**\nThe world isn’t healed. It’s held. That’s enough.\n\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 1: Wet Trace at Blackgate\n\nBell Two rang short across the Underface.\nJhace climbed the ladder out of the low heat, hand closing on rung, the metal sweating under his palm. He came up into Blackgate’s choke where the carts were backed nose to nose, and the Posting board glared white with its Tempermark pin and droplet icon.\n\nTiffani had already chalked a half-circle behind them so the crowd couldn’t press too close. She stood in the ring’s gap, one hand on her Veil case, the other tracing light against the stone seam. “Hold your tongue out of the way,” she said without looking. “It’s a quick one.”\n\nJhace set his pack down, rolled his sleeves, and touched the home ring bolted to the post. “One hand on home.”\n\nShe nodded, voice gone flat with routine. “One hand on home.”\n\nBrighlee arrived from the Conclave, still breathing through paper. “Work order nine-three-two. Wet Trace, Blackgate span. Setter Jhace brast, glass Tiffani veld, no hold.” She Tempermarked the droplet card, clipped it to the board, and stepped back. Names posted, Bell recorded. The carts quieted.\n\nThe note from the Chain said quick Vein under the gate plate—heat moving wrong, a shallow slip that could cut the cart path clean through the Underface if ignored. Persuasion before force. Always persuasion first.\n\nJhace unpacked what he needed: two wedges, seven-degree cut; copper-nib trace stick; bladder of wet. Nothing else. He checked his fray bands and the dull brass ring on his left wrist for crack. Sound.\n\nTiffani tilted the Lume rod, spreading its beam along the seam. The light came back too bright—bad depth. She adjusted the Shade one mark darker until the glare steadied. “Fault’s running shallow,” she said. “Quick vein wants the short path.”\n\n“Short path’s under their wheels,” he answered. “We give it a long one.”\n\nHe took his wedges, set the first two spans north of the gate mouth. A low angle, shallow drive. The second he planted at the shoulder where the seam ducked under the bridge stone. Two measured taps. Wood met resistance, then held.\n\n“Half-beat Slake,” Tiffani said.\n\nHe thumbed the bladder cap, wet the copper nib, flicked the bead clear. He placed it to the face and drew the line from north to shoulder, pressure through the shoulder, not the wrist.
