The steam had gone to thin breath, rising straight and vanishing against the stone.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **BLACKGATE WET TRACE — Bell 2–3** Quick vein redirected with wet trace; wedge-before-veil applied; Shade 1 skin set; no hold. Witness: Brighlee clerk record. Costs: fray band wear, thirst.\nImage — One continuous bead along the gate face, steam lifting hair-thin while the first cart crosses quiet.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 2: Thread Draw at Strathmoor Span\nBell Four came late, smothered by fog off the river. The air sat wet enough to drink. Jhace crossed the east truss walkway, his boots scraping the rust film that always rose before thaw. Strathmoor Span was a good bridge when it wanted to be—broad face, six weight lines—but it carried a habit of slow leaks under the mid-beam couplers. Every thaw, someone found the problem too late.\n\nTiffani was already halfway down the central run, kneeling over a length of open face, one palm on the metal, one arm buried to the elbow in a coil pit. She didn’t look up. “Thread slipped. South coupler. Field’s off-center. You feel it?”\n\nHe knelt beside her and laid a hand flat on the deck. The vibration came up through his glove—a low push that staggered every third pulse. Not a crack, just uneven tension. “Field pulling north. Thread’s walked a quarter.”\n\n“Quarter?”\n\n“Quarter and a hair.”\n\nShe made a mark on the plate with chalk, short horizontal dash. “That’s enough to make a vein forget itself.”\n\nBrighlee clanged down the stair, ledger box bumping her hip. “Work order seven-five-six. Thread Draw, Strathmoor. Setter Jhace brast, Glass Tiffani veld, no hold. Bell Four.” She Tempermarked the form, clipped it to the post, and stood aside to let them breathe.\n\n“Keep the carts back two lengths,” Tiffani said. “And watch for echo if the draw pulls too fast.”\n\nJhace unpacked from his belt: two threaded rods, one brace clamp, half bladder of wet. Nothing else. He slipped the rods through the anchor flanges and tightened both heads finger-firm. “Ready on brace.”\n\n“Home ring?” she asked.\n\nHe set his hand to the anchor post. “One hand on home.”\n\n“Half-beat Slake,” she said, and took position above him with the Lume angled down. The fog caught the light and turned the air to milk.\n\nHe drew the first line of wet from the north coupler down into the seam. The water hissed where metal met stress. The thread—a slender vein of heat beneath the plate—shifted once and hesitated. He guided it slow, not pushing, not chasing, just offering a cleaner route.\n\n“Pull steady,” Tiffani said.\n\n“I’m not a cart driver.”\n\n“You’d crash one anyway.”\n\nThe words sat warm between them, a scrap of talk to keep the hands honest. He pressed down, tracing the second run parallel to the first, copper nib close enough to taste heat through the glove. Steam rolled up his wrist.\n\n“The draw’s taking,” she said. “Shade three to five.”\n\nHe adjusted the Lume’s glare with his shoulder, watching the water line bead, then flatten as it met its opposite pressure. The field steadied; the vibration under his knees leveled.\n\n“Coupler read?” she asked.\n\n“Pulse every eight. No skip.”\n\n“Then it’s breathing again.”\n\nShe reached for her Veil case, drew out two small Shade 1 skins, warmed them over the hand brazier, and sealed the coupler mouth. The new skin caught the damp fog and turned it to bright sheen.\n\nBrighlee, back at the board, scratched ink slow enough that the strokes sounded like distant rain. “Thread draw complete,” she said. “Coupler sealed. Bell Four.”\n\nTiffani straightened, back cracking once. “Test lift.”\n\nJhace gave the signal. The chain crew took the leaf one finger up. The line ran even through the plates. He pressed his thumb to the coupler. Warm, no chatter. “Set.”\n\nShe exhaled, the sound tight through her teeth. “We’ll call it half-day work if it doesn’t drift before dusk.”\n\nHe packed his tools: copper nib cleaned on his sleeve, bladder recapped, rods released. “Costs?”\n\n“Palm burn, two Shade skins, no ring.”\n\nHe nodded. “Fray band notch from clamp.”\n\n“Clinic if it splits,” she said.\n\nHe flexed his hand, skin tight but whole. “Policy met.”\n\nThe fog thinned enough to show the river moving below, dark and quick. A bell buoy swung under the span, knocking once, then going quiet.\n\nMel Quarr arrived before they could finish stowing gear. Her coat carried old rain. “Strathmoor. What did it try this time?”\n\n“Thread drifted north,” Tiffani said. “Quarter and a hair. Reset, Slaked, Veiled. No hold.”\n\nMel bent to the coupler, touched the new seam, and closed her eyes. The city’s best witness never looked; she felt. “You gave it a longer walk,” she said. “Good. The old route was too proud.”\n\n“Witness?” Tiffani asked.\n\n“Witness,” Mel said, straightening. She wiped her fingers on her coat and signed Brighlee’s ledger. “Post it.”\n\nBrighlee clipped the cleared slip under the droplet. The ink still wet, she waited for the air to fix it. “Law in effect.”\n\nThe carts began to move again, a single wheel squealing, then catching rhythm. Jhace watched the strain lines across the span ease back into place—steel carrying itself again, the bridge alive but content.\n\nTiffani leaned on the railing, eyes on the water. “You ever think it remembers the first one who traced it?”\n\n“Bridges don’t remember people. They remember treatment.”\n\nShe gave the smallest shrug. “That’s people.”\n\nHe didn’t argue. The fog folded them both into the same shape for a while, and that was enough.\n\nThey gathered tools, slung packs, and crossed back toward the Underface stairs. Brighlee stayed behind to finish her ink. She always waited for it to dry before moving, superstition or craft—same thing down here.\n\nAt the far end, Jhace looked back once. The Veil skins had cooled, light catching on them like dull glass. The seam between plates looked born that way, not repaired. That was the goal: leave no sign of having been needed.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **STRATHMOOR SPAN THREAD DRAW — Bell 4** North-thread drift corrected; coupler reseated; twin rods set; Shade 1 Veil applied; load test passed. Witness: Mel Quarr. Costs: palm burn, fray band notch.\nImage — The coupler mouth sealed under thin Veil while fog slides past in sheets and the river carries broken light below.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 3: Counterbend at Lowcourt Arch\nBell Six came sharp and bright through the Upper shafts, ringing against every pipe that held the morning fog. Lowcourt’s Arch never liked daylight—it showed what the night had hidden. A hairline split ran between the arch stones where the third rib met the walkway bracket, a clean crack widening by breath.\n\nJhace was halfway up the scaffold before Brighlee finished reading the notice. “Work order eight-eight-one. Counterbend, Lowcourt Arch. Setter Jhace brast, Glass Tiffani veld. Local hold on half-lane until witness clear.” The last word echoed in the hollow under the span.\n\nTiffani was already on the inner run, hand to the seam. “It’s new,” she said. “Two fingers deep. Warm on one edge.”\n\nJhace crouched, running his glove along the joint. The air pushed out, steady pulse. “Thermal vein undercut the rib. We bend it back or lose the bracket by Bell Nine.”\n\nShe tapped her chalk against the stone, marking the stress lines. “We’ll go double wedge, five-degree counter, and Slake at the midpoint. No Veil until it cools.”\n\nHe unpacked two metal wedges, dull-edged from the last job, and a trace stick cut short for tight space. The arch smelled of lime dust and wet iron—old labor and new problem.\n\n“Clamp the east strut,” Tiffani said.\n\nHe hooked his belt to the ring set in the beam. “One hand on home,” he said, not ceremony, just habit.\n\nShe smiled with half her mouth. “One hand on home.”\n\nThey moved like practiced hinge—she setting the chalk arc, he setting the wedges, rhythm falling into the old count. “First at angle, second at base,” she said.\n\nHe slid the first wedge into the gap, shoulder against stone. Tap, tap. The wedge slipped.\n\nTiffani’s eyes sharpened. “Angle?”\n\n“Too proud,” he said, pulling it free. He cut the angle flatter with his knife, tried again. This time it bit. The crack hissed. He set the second wedge opposite, tapping in counter rhythm. The arch groaned low.\n\n“Hold there,” she said. “Trace wet.”\n\nHe thumbed the bladder, wetting the copper tip until the bead grew heavy. He ran the line between the wedges, two hands wide, guiding water into heat. Steam came off sharp and white; he worked through it.\n\n“Half-beat Slake.”\n\nHe matched her voice to the work. Two fingers on the plate, slow breath, pulse steady. “It’s listening,” he said.\n\n“Let it answer,” she said. She lowered the Lume, blue light cutting the mist. The trace glowed dull red, not bright—a good sign.\n\nHe traced the counterline across the top of the split, met the first run clean. The gap closed by half, a dark seam going gray as it cooled.\n\n“Drop wedges a count,” she said.\n\nHe lifted his hammer, gave each head one measured strike. The arch gave back a sound like teeth setting. The air around them steadied.\n\n“Vein’s settled,” he said.\n\n“Still wants a leash,” she said, drawing a small loop with chalk over the join. “We’ll set a band.”\n\nHe unwound a thin steel fray band, wrapped it once around the rib, drew it tight, and pinned it with the small rivet she handed him. The band clicked into place. “Hold?”\n\n“Hold,” she confirmed. “Shade it.”\n\nShe warmed a small strip of Shade 2 skin between her palms and laid it over the scar, tapping in sets of seven. The smell of curing resin drifted up.\n\nBrighlee wrote from the scaffold base, voice low but steady. “Counterbend executed, Slake at midpoint, band set, Veil applied.”\n\nTiffani looked down. “Bell six confirmed?”\n\n“Confirmed,” Brighlee said. “Witness on route.”\n\nThe air shifted before Mel Quarr arrived—everyone knew her step, the way the Bells seemed to pause a fraction when she was near. She climbed the last rung without taking the offered hand. “You bent it clean?”\n\n“Quarter-gap closed to eighth, heat discharged through counterline,” Tiffani said.\n\nMel touched the Veil with the back of her knuckle, then with the flat of her palm. She frowned.\n\n“Problem?” Tiffani asked.\n\nMel pressed harder. “Just wanted to be sure.” She listened a count longer. “Witness. Post it.”\n\nBrighlee Tempermarked the slip, ink catching the light. “Counterbend, Lowcourt Arch, local hold cleared. Bell six.”\n\nJhace exhaled, rolling his shoulder. “Cost: heat bite on right palm, one fray band spent.”\n\nTiffani brushed her thumb across his wrist once. “Clinic if it blisters.”\n\n“Policy met.”\n\nMel nodded toward the city. “You’ve got another seam at West Court before dusk. This one holds.”\n\n“Thanks,” Tiffani said.\n\nThe crew packed down. Jhace wiped the wedges clean, brushed stone dust off the lip, checked the band for tightness. The arch held still.\n\nBelow, the carts started again. A teamster looked up once, tipped his chin, and moved on. The bridge carried weight like it meant to.\n\nThey left the scaffold, walking down into the tunnel of cooled air beneath the arch. The smell of stone after steam clung to them both.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **LOWCOURT ARCH COUNTERBEND — Bell 6** Double-wedge counter applied; wet trace and midpoint Slake; fray band set; Shade 2 strip laid; local hold cleared. Witness: Mel Quarr. Costs: heat bite, one band.\nImage — Water beads lined along the band’s lower edge at equal intervals while carts roll overhead without a squeal.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 4: Heat Splice under Alderline\nBell Eight hit in dull echoes under Alderline’s underbelly, the sound rolling slow through stone and steam. It was late afternoon, gray and thick, the kind of day that made metal taste of air instead of earth. The lower conduits had been running hot for two Bells past limit—enough to burn shade off a wall skin. By the time Jhace reached the site, sweat had already stripped the chalk from his collar.\n\nTiffani stood by the south vent where the duct wall buckled. Her veil cloth was half soaked, but her voice stayed level. “Thermal vein tore through the splice. You feel the air?”\n\nHe crouched, palm to conduit. The heat bit through the glove fast. “Alive and angry. Vein’s running blind.”\n\n“Blind means fast,” she said. “And if it finds the main, we’ll have a blow under the market.”\n\n“Field direction?”\n\n“East to west, downhill. No counter. Just run.”\n\nBrighlee appeared, collar up against the heat. “Work order one-one-five-four. Heat Splice under Alderline. Setter Jhace brast, Glass Tiffani veld. Local hold to foot traffic until clear.” She Tempermarked the droplet board with shaking fingers; the wax nearly ran before it set.\n\n“Chain it,” Tiffani said.\n\nJhace fastened his tether to the wall ring, one hand gripping it. “One hand on home.” The metal burned even through his sleeve. “We wedge the run first.”\n\n“Use the short wedges,” she said. “The long ones will swell.”\n\nHe pulled two half-lengths from his pouch, jammed the first at the upper seam of the duct mouth, the second two spans below. He hammered them in until the pitch rose high and sharp.\n\nThe duct noise shifted—deep to shallow, like breath caught in the chest.\n\n“Trace wet,” she said.\n\nHe thumbed the bladder cap, drew water into the copper nib, and began the run. The first line hissed as it met the heat; vapor curled into ribbons. He kept the nib steady, shoulder over hand, driving water into the split vein until the color under the metal dulled from white to gray.\n\nTiffani moved her Lume rod closer. “Shade four to six,” she said. “Keep the bead thick.”\n\nHe adjusted his wrist, laying the second line parallel, two fingers apart. Heat surged under his hand—vein testing him—but the wet held. Steam rolled off the metal in heavy breaths.\n\n“Half-beat Slake,” she said.\n\nHe let the wet rest, pressure through shoulder, breath slow. The line steadied. The vein’s pulse dropped.\n\n“Still wants a mouth,” she said.\n\n“I’ll give it one.”\n\nHe ran a short feeder line from the end of his first trace to a small vent slit above the duct. The air drew through it, hissing once, then falling quiet.\n\nThe heat at his glove softened; the metal under his palm stopped twitching. Tiffani’s hand brushed his wrist once, not comfort but calibration, steadying his wrist until his pulse matched the duct.\n\n“Slake complete,” she said. “Now Veil.”\n\nHe stepped aside. She drew a Shade 2 skin from her case, warmed it between her hands, and pressed it over the splice. Steam rose and hung there. Her fingers moved quick—tap tap tap in sets of seven. The seam went dark, settled.\n\nBrighlee’s pen scratched. “Heat Splice contained, Veil applied, local hold remains until witness.”\n\n“Tell them we’ll have it cleared by Bell Nine,” Tiffani said.\n\nBrighlee nodded and climbed back up the tunnel steps to keep the onlookers from drifting too close.\n\nJhace checked his gloves. The leather had gone glossy, almost translucent. “Burn through,” he muttered.\n\n“Palm?” she asked.\n\n“Blister later. Not yet.”\n\n“Clinic if it lifts,” she said, without looking away from the seam.\n\nHe grunted.\n\nThe air shifted again—cooler this time. Steam thinned. The duct gave a single deep crack, then went still.\n\n“Field even,” Tiffani said.\n\nHe leaned close, listening with his teeth. “Pressure five-finger drop. The vein found its line.”\n\nThey waited half a count, because silence after a heat job lies half the time. When nothing moved, she nodded. “Test run.”\n\nHe signaled up the tunnel. The vent valves opened. Air rushed through, caught in the new splice, turned direction like it had never meant to fight them. The response that came back was mechanical peace: even pulse, measurable.\n\n“Set,” he said.\n\nMel Quarr came down through the vent shaft, wiping condensation from her glasses. “Alderline again,” she said, and crouched without ceremony. “Let me hear it.”\n\nJhace shifted aside. She put one ear to the Veil and stayed still long enough to make them both hold breath. Then she nodded. “Stable. Hot vein cooled, no echo. You cleared it before the breach.”\n\n“Witness?” Tiffani asked.\n\n“Witness,” Mel said. “Post it.”\n\nBrighlee reappeared, ink already drying at the pen’s edge. “Heat Splice contained under Alderline. Local hold cleared. Bell Nine mark.” She clipped the slip, pressed the blue seal, held until it took.\n\n“Cost?” Tiffani asked.\n\n“Palm blister, glove dead. Fray band edge singed.”\n\n“Policy?”\n\n“Clinic if it lifts,” he said again.\n\nShe handed him a new pair from her bag. “Use these next time.”\n\nHe took them, thick leather still cool. “You planning another heat job?”\n\n“This city’s always planning another heat job,” she said.\n\nThey gathered tools while the fog from the duct mixed with the cooler tunnel air. It smelled of burnt dust and new rain. The metal face they’d fixed stayed matte, clean.\n\nBrighlee finished her last mark and snapped the ledger shut. “Filed.”\n\nThey climbed back to the street. The air above Alderline carried no heat shimmer now, only slow gray light.\n\nTiffani looked down the length of the duct run, eyes tracing where the line bent invisible under stone. “If it holds through melt, we buy ourselves a season.”\n\nJhace slung the dead gloves over his shoulder. “One more day of a world that works.”\n\n“Don’t get poetic,” she said.\n\nHe didn’t.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **ALDERLINE HEAT SPLICE — Bell 8–9** Hot vein contained; short wedges; twin wet traces; feeder to vent; Shade 2 Veil; hold cleared. Witness: Mel Quarr. Costs: palm blister, glove ruined, singed band.\nImage — A matte, cooled splice with a thin drift of steam fading while the tunnel air turns clean.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 5: Lockfall at Greymantle Cut\nJhace hit the pavement running, Bell Ten already shuddering through the pipes. The street pitched like a breathing lung. A lock had failed in the Cut, lower east face, the kind that took carts whole if left arguing.\n\nTiffani was kneeling in runoff halfway down the slope, gloves black with silt, hair damp with steam. “Second lock’s sheared,” she said. “First’s holding by prayer. Get down here.”\n\nHe slid in beside her. The air stank of boiled metal and canal gas. Water climbed over the curb in gray fingers. “How deep?”\n\n“Too deep.” She shoved a wrench into his hand. “Counterbrace’s stuck. I can’t see the pin.”\n\nHe put his shoulder into it, turned once, twice. Nothing. The wrench teeth jumped, bit air.\n\n“Strip it and we’re drowned,” she said.\n\n“Already halfway.” He jammed the tip again, braced one knee against stone, and pulled. The wrench gave a sound like bone cracking. The pin twitched free—then stuck again.\n\n“Angle’s wrong,” Tiffani said.\n\n“No time for right.”\n\nHe leaned harder. Metal shrieked. The lock gate shifted, not enough. Water surged up his chest, warm, rising fast.\n\n“Cut the feed line!” she shouted.\n\nBrighlee’s voice echoed from the walkway above. “Valve’s jammed!”\n\n“Then bleed it!” Tiffani snapped.\n\nJhace drew his hammer and drove it into the pin head. The gate lurched an inch. Water punched him in the throat. He couldn’t breathe. He struck again, blind. The pin jumped. The lock slid another inch.\n\nTiffani grabbed his collar and hauled him back. The water line dropped half a hand.\n\n“Hold!” she shouted.\n\nThe gate creaked, uncertain. He pressed both palms to the metal, felt it trembling. “Vein’s live under it!”\n\n“Trace wet!”\n\nHe fumbled the bladder, hands shaking, found the copper nib, scrawled a crooked line across the seam. The water hissed, turned steam, hissed again. The vibration smoothed. The gate took weight.\n\nShe lowered her Lume, breath cutting short. “Pulse?”\n\n“Even. For now.”\n\n“Brace it anyway.”\n\nHe reached for the short wedges. The first slipped from his hand, vanished in the runoff. The second stuck, skewed.\n\n“Wrong cut,” she said.\n\n“I know.” He sliced the edge flat with his knife, jammed it again. This time it bit.\n\nThe low vibration dropped into the stone. The water stopped climbing.\n\nBrighlee clattered down the stair, half-drenched, ledger bag thumping. “Bell Ten and a half! Setter Jhace brast, Glass Tiffani veld—Lockfall emergency!”\n\n“Post later!” Tiffani said. “Witness first!”\n\nMel Quarr stumbled in from the north end, coat dripping. “You’re lucky I was on the bridge,” she said.\n\n“Get down here,” Jhace said. “We need your ear.”\n\nMel crouched, pressed her head to the lock seam. Her jaw tightened. “Still movement.”\n\n“We bled it!”\n\n“Not enough.” She moved her hand lower, searching. “There’s another line.”\n\nTiffani cursed. “The feeder vein.”\n\nJhace turned, searching the wall through steam. “Where?”\n\n“Under you.”\n\nHe knelt, groped through the flood, found the hairline crack near his boot. Warm water bubbled through. He shoved the copper nib into it, let the bladder feed until the hiss died.\n\n“Now?” he said.\n\nMel stayed still for a long count. Then she looked up, doubtful. “Maybe.”\n\n“Maybe doesn’t clear it,” Tiffani said.\n\nMel pressed her ear again, eyes shut. Silence. “Now.”\n\nJhace slumped against the wall. The water around his knees had gone still, the kind of stillness that made sound travel.\n\nBrighlee wiped water from her ledger with the side of her sleeve. “Lockfall at Greymantle Cut contained. Local hold to remain partial until tomorrow inspection.”\n\n“Post it,” Tiffani said.\n\nBrighlee clipped the slip to the drenched board. The ink ran but stayed legible.\n\nJhace peeled his glove. His palm was split across the web between thumb and forefinger, blood mixing with gray water.\n\n“Cost?” she asked.\n\nHe flexed the hand; skin pulled. “Grip’s gone for a day.”\n\n“Clinic,” she said.\n\n“I said a day, not a week.”\n\n“Policy doesn’t care what you said.”\n\nHe laughed once.\n\nThe lock groaned, then quieted. Mel stood, brushing grit from her coat. “You held it,” she said. “Don’t expect thanks.”\n\n“Wouldn’t know what to do with it,” Jhace said.\n\nShe almost smiled. “Good. You’ll get another call before dawn.”\n\nShe left up the wet stairs, boots leaving perfect prints.\n\nTiffani leaned on the gate, breathing hard. “You almost drowned.”\n\nHe nodded. “Almost fixed it, too.”\n\nHer hand found his wrist, still wet. “Stay,” she said.\n\nHe did.\n\nThey stood until the canal calmed and the sound of the Bells faded under the water’s slow rise.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **GREYMANTLE CUT LOCKFALL — Bell 10–11** Lock failure arrested; counterbrace freed; wet trace calmed live vein; wedges set; partial hold remains pending inspection. Witness: Mel Quarr. Costs: split palm, one wedge lost.\nImage — A red handprint drying against stone beside the home ring while runoff loses its color and carts wait without complaint."}

{"name":"Grindheim (1) — Clean Manuscript (Pass 2, Ch.6–10)","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 6: Corlexi’s Fire (Interlude)\n\nBell Twelve rolled flat over the roofs, muffled by mist and smoke. Corlexi stood in the market square with both hands in the ash bin, pulling placards out one by one. Each card bore the old single‑brace symbol: two wedges facing inward, out of date by one Bell. Every lock failure that week had carried that mark. Policy changed this morning. Someone had to burn the proof.\n\nHe held the first card by its corner. The wax Tempermark bled blue when the heat touched it, curling into itself like muscle recoiling. The stamp read *Greymantle Cut—Work Order 2043*. He remembered the red handprint rumor before he saw the stain burned into the ink edge. “Lucky he didn’t drown,” he muttered, and dropped it into the flame.\n\nA group of apprentices watched from the steps, carrying fresh boards. They whispered to each other, eyes on the burn box. “Double brace rule?” one asked.\n\nCorlexi didn’t look up. “Double brace rule. Within ten rounds.”\n\nThe fire spat once. Wax smell thickened. The second placard carried a name he knew—Maren hilt, a good setter who’d cracked a knee when a brace failed two months ago. He held it longer before letting it fall.\n\nOn the far side of the square, the Couriers’ Bell office was already laying out the new boards. Ink crews worked off a folding table, stamping wedge glyphs two‑by‑two, copper heads hot enough to hiss when they touched wood. The pattern had changed overnight: old inward braces replaced by paired diagonals, more metal, more safety.\n\n“Quicker this way?” an apprentice asked.\n\n“No,” Corlexi said. “Slower, safer.”\n\nHe fed the third placard into the bin. The crowd near the food stalls had gone quiet, watching the smoke. It wasn’t ceremony; it was relief disguised as boredom.\n\nA Conclave clerk crossed the square with a basket of new seals. “Posting priority corners first,” he said, voice careful. “The Conclave wants public compliance before dusk.”\n\nCorlexi wiped sweat from his cheek with the back of his arm. “They always do.”\n\nThe clerk hesitated, holding a placard halfway. “You were there for Lowcourt, right?”\n\n“Doesn’t matter where I was.” He took the placard and set it into the fire. “Now I’m here.”\n\nThe last of the old signs turned to smoke. He raked the ashes with a long iron rod until the glyphs broke apart. Behind him, the apprentices lifted the new boards, carried them toward the market posts, the stairwells, the corners by the canal. Every new mark would show two wedges instead of one.\n\nA teamster paused his cart to watch. “Another rule already?”\n\nCorlexi turned, voice even. “One that keeps your wheels on the ground.”\n\nThe man tipped his cap and moved on.\n\nCorlexi knelt by the ashes, scooped a handful into a small cloth bag, tied it tight. He labeled the bag in careful script: **Witness residue — old policy burned clean**.\n\nBy the time the Bell rang half, the square had changed color. The old blue of single braces had gone gray in the soot, replaced by fresh copper and paint that hadn’t yet dulled. Children traced the new glyphs with their fingers as they passed.\n\nFrom the east alley came a gust of steam—pipe vent clearing from Alderline’s repair. Corlexi looked up through it, saw the faint shadow of the Conclave tower against the fog. The world was always patching itself faster than people could keep up.\n\nAn apprentice returned from the posting run, breath quick. “Every corner’s marked. Last one’s drying by the pump road.”\n\nCorlexi nodded. “Good. When it rains, the wax will tell us which ones hold.”\n\n“What if one doesn’t?”\n\n“Then we bury someone,” he said. “And trace it again.”\n\nThe clerk from the Conclave sealed the record page with the last of his ink. “Policy change filed: all Cut locks to carry double brace before Bell Twenty‑two. Witness Corlexi, Market Station.”\n\nHe signed his name small, a single curl at the end. “Filed,” he said.\n\nThe clerk hesitated. “You burn them clean.”\n\n“Everything burns clean if you give it enough air.”\n\nThe clerk left with the ledger. The apprentices lingered, unsure if they were dismissed. Corlexi pointed at the fresh boards stacked by the rail. “Post two more at the bridge ends before Bell Thirteen. People forget fast.”\n\nThey moved, boots thudding.\n\nWhen the square cleared, Corlexi sat on the edge of the ash bin. The fire had gone to coals, orange and low. He held one unburned corner of an old placard, tracing the single‑wedge mark with his thumb. It wasn’t wrong when they made it; it was just small. The city had outgrown small.\n\nHe tossed it into the coals and waited for the wax to hiss.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **CONCLAVE POLICY UPDATE — Bell 12** Effective immediately: all lock braces within Te’Oga to be doubled, twin‑wedge configuration mandatory. Previous single‑brace seals void. Compliance verification by Couriers’ Bell inspectors within ten rounds.\nImage — A square gone gray with ash, new copper placards catching steam‑light, and the faint smell of burned wax rising into the fog while the city pretends it was always built this way.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 7: Double Fault at Rindle Yard\n\nThe Thread was already broken when they got there, hanging like split rope from the upper seam. Bell Two had barely cleared; the air smelled of burnt iron and shame.\n\nJhace dropped to one knee beside the fault. The brace plates were wrong—the old single set, bent inward, paint flaked. Someone had patched over the crack with tar and hope.\n\n“Double rule went out last Bell,” Tiffani said, standing over him. “This is still single.”\n\n“Work Order’s dated yesterday.” He peeled a tag from the post. The Tempermark was smudged. “Filed before the change.”\n\n“Doesn’t make it safe.”\n\nHe ran a finger along the line. “Who set it?”\n\n“Rindle yard foreman signed it. Kesh Hurn.”\n\n“Figures.” He tapped the seam; dull echo. “He braced shallow, didn’t backfill the groove.”\n\nShe knelt beside him, Lume low. “You want to condemn it?”\n\nHe looked at the carts queued behind them—grain wagons, two Bells late already. “Condemn and they riot.”\n\n“Leave it and it collapses.”\n\nHe didn’t answer. Instead he unpacked a wet bladder and copper nib. “We do what we can.”\n\nTiffani hissed through her teeth. “Against policy.”\n\nHe met her eyes. “Against dying, too.”\n\nThe first wedge refused the slot. He turned it, shaved it down with his knife, pressed again. The metal gave half a sound, neither yes nor no.\n\n“Angle’s off,” she said.\n\n“Off by one degree, maybe.”\n\n“Enough.”\n\nHe pulled it free, cut it flatter. This time it bit. The seam breathed once.\n\n“Trace it,” she said.\n\nHe set the wet line, slow and deliberate. The copper hissed. Steam lifted, thin and bright. Half‑beat Slake. His hand trembled.\n\n“You’re bleeding through the wrap,” she said.\n\n“Not enough to matter.”\n\n“You always say that.”\n\nThe Thread shuddered under the deck. A deep vibration rolled through the plates—heavy. Jhace froze.\n\n“Too hot,” she said. “You’re feeding it too fast.”\n\nHe lifted the nib. The vibration eased but didn’t stop. “Backflow from the lower vein.”\n\nShe angled the Lume light along the seam, found a hairline split running off left. “That’s not ours. That’s old metal.”\n\n“Then we’re chasing ghosts.”\n\n“Ghosts kill slower, but they still kill.”\n\nBehind them, Brighlee jogged down the path, hair damp with fog, ledger in hand. “Double‑brace inspection,” she said. She saw the fault, opened the book, and wrote without comment.\n\nMel Quarr arrived half‑running, coat half‑buttoned. “What’s wrong?”\n\n“Old work,” Tiffani said. “Single brace, cracked thread, wrong feed.”\n\nMel crouched, laid her palm to the plate. The vibration pulsed through her arm. “That’s unstable.”\n\nJhace didn’t look up. “We know.”\n\n“Then step back.”\n\n“No time.”\n\nShe reached for the trace line. “You’ll burn it.”\n\nHe caught her wrist. “Then I’ll burn it steady.”\n\nMel held his stare a beat too long, then pulled free. “You think you can fix this?”\n\n“I think it needs a witness.”\n\nShe almost smiled. “You’ll either save it or make it worse.”\n\n“That’s most of my work.”\n\nHe adjusted the line, traced again, slower. The wet beaded along the crack, steam rising. The vibration dropped half a tone.\n\nTiffani exhaled. “That’s better.”\n\n“Not done.” He pressed a second wedge. The first shifted. “Damn it—”\n\nThe seam snapped open a hair. Water hissed.\n\nMel shouted, “Step back!”\n\nHe didn’t. He shoved his shoulder against the plate, jammed the wedge deeper. The hiss stopped. The Thread steadied, faint and even.\n\n“Now?” Tiffani asked.\n\nMel pressed her hand to the seam again. “Still live, but not rising.”\n\n“Enough to clear?”\n\n“Maybe.” She waited one more heartbeat. “Now.”\n\nHe pulled back, soaked through. Tiffani moved fast, laying two Shade 1 skins across the seam, sealing the new double brace. The water cooled; the air turned metallic and sweet.\n\nBrighlee recorded, clipped the interim slip, and stepped aside.\n\nMel stood, brushing grit off her knees. “You saved it,” she said.\n\n“Temporarily.”\n\n“Nothing’s permanent.” She looked toward the queue. “Tell the carts they can pass, slow.”\n\nTiffani lifted her Lume toward the post. “Witness?”\n\nMel hesitated, hand above the ledger. “The policy’s new. This fix breaks it.”\n\n“Then mark it as precedent,” Jhace said.\n\nMel studied the line another long moment, then nodded. “Witness.” She signed, slow and deliberate.\n\nThe carts started rolling again, wheels creaking, drivers staring down at the fresh seal as they passed. Nobody spoke.\n\nJhace cleaned his tools, blood streaking the wet cloth. His glove was torn; skin raw at the palm.\n\n“Cost?” Tiffani asked.\n\n“Grip’s gone again.”\n\n“Clinic.”\n\n“Policy.”\n\nMel turned away, already writing. “Habit saves cities,” she said over her shoulder.\n\nThey packed up. Fog lifted just enough to show the new double‑brace gleaming dull under water sheen.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **RINDLE YARD—DOUBLE FAULT — Bell 2–3** Emergency double‑wedge applied under superseded single‑brace install; Slaked; thread stabilized under witness; temporary variance logged pending Conclave review. Witness: Mel Quarr. Costs: torn palm, one wedge lost. Precedent: immediate correction permitted when delay risks collapse.\nImage — Steam thins to reveal the new brace shining wet, two wedges crossing like a scar that chose to hold.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 8: The HALT at Narrowvale Rise\n\nBell Five hit mid‑echo across the Crown. The air changed pitch. Blue banners flared at every post from Bridgegate to the Vale, the signal that stopped carts and silenced every forge at once. **HALT ORDER: STRUCTURAL RISK.**\n\nJhace reached the rise at a run, lungs raw, coat half‑soaked from the night before. The flag snapped above the street, light cutting through fog. A ten‑span arch in the rise had cracked. Half the Upper Market’s weight ran through that bridge.\n\nTiffani stood with both hands braced on the guard rail, eyes on the fissure widening between plates. “It’s pulling apart along the seam. Two Veins, opposite vectors.”\n\nHe knelt, tracing with his glove. The vibration hit his bones. “Feels like a mis‑thread in the Crown feed. Someone over‑bound it.”\n\n“Conclave techs said the same. They bailed.”\n\n“They left mid‑HALT?”\n\n“They said consultation complete.”\n\n“We’re it, then.”\n\nBrighlee’s voice carried from the post above. “Bell Five to Bell Nine window. Full HALT. No reopen without witness clearance. Work order three‑three‑eight.” She clipped the paper to the board; the crowd pulled back two meters on instinct.\n\n“Half the market’s watching,” Tiffani said.\n\n“Good,” Jhace said. “They can remember what this costs.”\n\nThe crack ran three spans, straight and deep. Steam leaked from the lower seam—heat from the feed lines bleeding out.\n\nTiffani lowered her Lume, adjusted the Shade until the light bounced back dull orange. “The left vein’s dry. No Threft movement. Right one’s alive.”\n\n“Dead and live next to each other,” he said. “It’ll shear itself trying to breathe.”\n\n“Then we trace them both. Marry them at midpoint.”\n\nHe nodded, set his bladder, wet the copper, touched nib to plate. The left line refused the water; it pooled and ran.\n\n“It’s rejecting,” Tiffani said.\n\nHe pressed harder. Steam spat. The line stayed cold.\n\n“Try heat first. Lume five.”\n\nShe rolled the Shade open. Light flared. Metal glowed dull red. Jhace pressed again. This time the wet hissed once, then ran straight.\n\n“Got it.”\n\n“Right side,” she said.\n\nHe shifted over. The live vein responded instantly, water sucking inward too fast. The line flashed white.\n\n“Kill it,” she snapped.\n\nHe jerked the bladder back, burned his knuckles on the copper. The seam popped. Metal jumped under his palm.\n\n“Vein bit,” he said.\n\n“It’ll climb if we don’t split the feed.”\n\n“Brighlee! Drop flow from the mid‑valve.”\n\n“Flagged.”\n\n“Two‑inch wedge. Hard grain,” he said.\n\nTiffani handed it over. “Angle eight.”\n\nHe drove the wedge into the plate edge until the ringing stopped. The crack shivered, steadied.\n\n“Pulse even?” she asked.\n\n“Barely.”\n\n“If it jumps again, we run.”\n\n“Noted.”\n\nShe set a second wedge lower. The ground under them thudded twice. Distant—pipes reacting.\n\n“Feed shift,” he said. “Upstream.”\n\n“Then finish before it notices.”\n\nHe wet the bridge line, smaller run to join both veins. The bead trembled. Sweat stung his eyes.\n\n“Shade three,” she said. “Keep it honest.”\n\nThe vibration under their knees lowered, unified, as if both veins found each other and chose not to fight.\n\n“Married,” she said.\n\n“Seal it.”\n\nShe pressed a Shade 2 skin over the joint. “Annealshift zero.” She breathed heat until it dulled, then tapped corners in sevens. The air changed tone—low, whole.\n\nMel Quarr appeared at the top of the rise, coat soaked, hair stuck to her face. “Talk.”\n\n“Joined feed,” Jhace said. “Trace holding.”\n\nShe crouched by the seam. “Both lines live?”\n\n“Equalized.”\n\nShe pressed her ear to the plate. Long pause. “Steady,” she said. “For now.”\n\n“For now gets carts moving.”\n\n“Not until I sign.” She pulled her pen.\n\nThe ground rumbled again, small but rising. Everyone froze. The seam under her hand pulsed once, hard.\n\n“That wasn’t feed,” Mel said. “Expansion.”\n\n“Backflow,” Tiffani said. “Pressure from the Crown line.”\n\nJhace grabbed the release wrench. “We vent.”\n\n“You open that without brace, you’ll shear the plate,” Mel barked.\n\n“Then we brace.” He set another wedge, deep, metal ringing like struck Bells.\n\nTiffani cut light to zero, then reopened to full. “Still rising.”\n\n“Three turns,” Jhace said, wrench on the valve.\n\n“Half‑turn only.”\n\n“Three.” He spun once, twice. The seam screamed, then sighed—long and hollow. Steam shot up, scalding.\n\nThen the sound shifted: deep, low exhale of weight finding balance.\n\nMel pressed her palm to the plate. “Pressure’s gone. Breathing normal.”\n\n“We clear?” he asked.\n\n“HALT lifts at Bell Nine.”\n\nBrighlee’s voice cut across the rise: “Witnessed.”\n\nTiffani let out a sound close to a laugh. “You’re an idiot.”\n\n“Functional idiot.”\n\n“Still counts.” She rolled his sleeve. Skin blistered pink. “Clinic, now.”\n\nHe nodded.\n\nMel wrote the clearance with hands that weren’t steady yet. “Policy note: overbind feed must split before marriage.”\n\nBrighlee wrote. “Logged.”\n\nCarts began to move again, careful. The crowd thinned with the kind of silence near‑misses earn.\n\nJhace watched the line. “We nearly lost it.”\n\nTiffani sat beside him, shoulder to shoulder. “We didn’t.” Her hand found his burned wrist. His breathing evened.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **NARROWVALE RISE HALT — Bells 5–9** Full shutdown under emergency order. Dual‑vein overbind corrected via manual trace and vent; local brace confirmation enforced. Witnessed clearance: Mel Quarr. Policy update: split feeds pre‑marriage on overbind. Costs: burn (left forearm), one wrench warped.\nImage — Blue banners snapping wet while steam curls around a blackened glove by the valve; the rise carries a slow, even breath beneath it.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 9: Slow Bell at Mirecourt\n\nBell Ten dragged. Fog held low. Jhace hadn’t slept since Narrowvale. His burned arm still stung under the wrap.\n\nThe summons said *minor leak, Mirecourt span*. Nothing in the city was ever minor.\n\nThey reached the gate face to find condensation drip from the inner rail, steady. Not a break. Worse was the sound—a hollow tick between the Bells like a wound undecided.\n\nTiffani crouched. “Overbind residue. Narrowvale runoff hit the lower feeds. Pressure’s wrong.”\n\nHe set his pack down slow. His hand didn’t want to close around the latch. “We shouldn’t be working.”\n\n“You say that every time you’re bleeding.”\n\n“Not every time.”\n\n“Name one you didn’t.”\n\nHe didn’t answer.\n\nBrighlee came up the incline, voice rasped. “Work order four‑eight‑one. Leak containment, Mirecourt. Bell Ten to Eleven.” Functional. She posted and stepped back.\n\n“Handle trace,” Tiffani said. “I’ll brace.”\n\n“Arm still burned?”\n\n“It moves.”\n\n“Not the same as healed.”\n\n“Closer than dead.”\n\nShe crouched by the line. “One hand on home.”\n\nHe found the anchor post. “One hand on home.”\n\nThe first wedge slipped. Too slick. He wiped his palm, tried again. The second bit.\n\n“Shallow leak,” she said, eyes on the Lume. “Midline pulse, four counts low.”\n\nHe felt it through the plate—a heartbeat out of rhythm.\n\n“Half‑beat Slake,” she said.\n\nHe moved slower than he liked. The copper line glowed faintly wet; his reflection in it looked older.\n\nShe whispered counts. “One. Two. Half.”\n\nThe wet hissed. The seam brightened, steadied. The tick stopped.\n\n“Got it,” he said.\n\nShe didn’t answer.\n\nHe looked up. Her face had gone blank. The Lume slipped from her hand, rolled once, hit stone.\n\n“Tiff.”\n\nHer eyes blinked once, slow. “Shade wrong. Four instead of two.”\n\n“Too hot.”\n\nShe nodded, dazed. “Fixed.”\n\nHe steadied her wrist. Her pulse jumped under his thumb.\n\n“Stay,” he said.\n\nShe inhaled. Color returned. “Fine.”\n\n“Liar.”\n\n“Better liar than corpse.”\n\nBrighlee hovered. “Do you want me to—”\n\n“Keep writing,” Tiffani said.\n\nThey worked in silence, correcting what the last sleepless shift had left crooked. The line dried even.\n\n“Sure it’s sealed?” Jhace asked.\n\nTiffani checked feedback. “Trace reads steady. If it fails, it’ll do it politely.”\n\n“Polite’s new.”\n\n“Maybe the city’s learning.”\n\nHe sat back, wiping sweat with his good sleeve. “You’re shaking.”\n\n“So are you.”\n\n“Then we match.”\n\n“Don’t make poetry out of exhaustion.”\n\nHe didn’t.\n\nMel Quarr appeared at the top of the ramp, as tired as the rest. “Report.”\n\n“Minor leak, sealed,” Tiffani said. “No hold. Feed equalized.”\n\nMel knelt, pressed her hand to the plate. “Holding.” She looked at Jhace’s arm. “You working injured again?”\n\n“Clinic after shift.”\n\n“Clinic now. I’ll send a slip.”\n\nBrighlee pinned the form to the board. “Work order four‑eight‑one complete. Bell Eleven.”\n\n“Costs?” Mel asked.\n\n“Burn reopening,” Jhace said.\n\n“Dehydration,” Tiffani added.\n\nMel wrote both. “Clinic, mandatory.”\n\nThey didn’t argue.\n\nWhen the others left, Jhace stayed a minute longer. The span carried a steady pulse under fog.\n\nTiffani leaned against the rail beside him. “You’re bleeding through the wrap.”\n\nA small patch of red showed. “Proof I’m alive.”\n\nShe leaned her head against the same rail. “Alive’s overrated.” Her shoulder brushed his. His tremor eased.\n\nThey left the Lume burning low on the post, light catching the still‑wet line like a thin scar that refused to brighten.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **MIRECOURT SPAN — Bell 10–11** Minor leak containment; feed imbalance corrected via manual trace; no local hold required. Witness: Mel Quarr. Costs: reopened burn, dehydration, Shade mis‑set corrected. Policy note: consecutive shifts beyond four Bells trigger mandatory clinic rest.\nImage — The span under fog carrying a slow, even rhythm while a thin red patch darkens the wrap and the low Lume flickers against steel gone cold.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 10: Mobel at the Table (Interlude)\n\nBell Two chimed through the Mercers’ Quarter, each note sliding through the lattice rafters above the weighing floor. Mobel had been there since dawn, coat off, sleeves rolled, the kind of tired that doesn’t show until it stops moving.\n\nThe scales in front of him were old Crown brass, polished to dull gold. They didn’t lie often, but they could be coaxed. The night auditors had caught three weight sets marked light in the middle coin. Not enough to hang a merchant, enough to make the Conclave twitch.\n\nHe placed one suspect weight on the pan, thumbed the notch along its rim. “Filed too deep,” he said to his clerk. “Half‑grain shave.”\n\nThe clerk squinted. “Still legal?”\n\nMobel didn’t answer. He flicked the weight off the pan. It hit the floor with a hollow ring. “Not when it starts breeding.”\n\nThe smell of old oil filled the air as apprentices cleaned their scales. Every trade table in the room carried its own seal—the droplet for Couriers, the hammer for Forgewrights, the two crossed wedges newly added for Locks and Loads. That mark had cost Jhace a burn and half the city a rulebook rewrite.\n\nHe checked the next weight. The brass surface bore faint scrape lines, deliberate. He turned it over and read the faint initials carved into the base. “Harnet.”\n\nThe clerk’s eyes widened. “He’s senior.”\n\n“Was,” Mobel said. “Post the charge.”\n\nThe clerk hesitated. “Sir, it’s only half a grain—”\n\nMobel’s voice stayed even. “Half a grain turns into famine when multiplied. Post it.”\n\nThe clerk went pale but moved, writing the notice on a strip of wax paper. Mobel pulled the ink stamp from his belt, Tempermarked the sheet, and clipped it to the board above the scales. The room stilled. Every merchant in sight turned toward the sound.\n\n“Official?” one called.\n\n“Filed under Bell Two,” Mobel said.\n\nSomeone muttered, “Conclave doesn’t usually bother with half‑grains.”\n\n“They do now.”\n\nHe reached for the next weight, turned it, sniffed the faint tang of fresh polish. Too clean. He scraped a knife edge across it. Bright line. “Fresh file,” he said.\n\nA Forgewright approached, face dark. “We recalibrate weekly. No harm in keeping tools smooth.”\n\n“Smooth isn’t light,” Mobel said. He balanced the weight against a verified measure. The pan dipped slow, traitorous. “See?”\n\nThe forgewright looked away. “Barely a whisper.”\n\n“That whisper just cost you your table.”\n\nHe wrote the notice himself: *Forgery of official weights, Harnet Forge Cooperative. License revoked pending audit. Seals to be melted public.* He signed, Tempermarked, and handed it to the clerk. “Fire the furnace.”\n\nThe apprentices dragged a small crucible from the side wall, set it on the coals, pumped the bellows until the air shimmered. The smell of heating metal cut through the damp.\n\nMobel took the forged weights one by one, dropped them into the pot. They sizzled as moisture burned off, then sagged into liquid. The room went quiet except for the hiss of betrayal cooking.\n\nThe Forgewright spoke again, voice thin. “You’re making a show of this.”\n\n“No,” Mobel said. “A record.” He stirred the molten brass with a rod, watching the color shift from yellow to red to dull silver. “Shows fade. Records stay on the board.”\n\nWhen the last of the fake weights had gone under, he tilted the crucible, poured the liquid into a flat mold shaped like a wedge. “New symbol,” he said. “Half‑grain truth.”\n\nThe clerk stared. “What do we do with it?”\n\n“Post it on the Table.”\n\nThey nailed the cooled wedge to the central board between the trade seals, the scarred metal still faintly warm. It read: *Mass must match mark. Deviance voids claim.*\n\nMobel looked around the room. No one met his eyes. “Back to work,” he said.\n\nThe merchants dispersed. The clatter of weights returned, but softer, careful now.\n\nAt the Bell of mid‑morning, the clerk handed him the ledger. “Three confessions filed already,” she said. “Two from vendors who weren’t questioned.”\n\nMobel nodded, too tired for satisfaction. “Guilt travels faster than ink.”\n\nHe sat a while after the room cleared, listening to the slow tick of cooling metal. He wasn’t proud. Just done. He wiped his hands on his coat and left the burned smell for the next shift.\n\nOutside, a new placard hung on the Mercers’ archway—approved by existing rule. It carried Corlexi’s twin‑wedge symbol above the words *DOUBLE CHECK. DOUBLE PAY.* He smiled once, small, then vanished into the crowd before anyone could mistake it for approval.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **MERCERS’ TABLE AUDIT — Bell 2–4** Forgery of trade weights confirmed; Harnet Forge Cooperative stripped of license; counterfeit measures melted into single “Half‑Grain Wedge,” displayed publicly at Mercers’ Table. Policy expansion: daily double verification of all Conclave‑approved measures.\nImage — A single wedge of cooled brass nailed to the trade board, edges uneven, letters burned in by hand—heat ghosting through the metal while honest weights tick in cautious rhythm around it."}

{"name":"Grindheim (1) — Clean Manuscript (Pass 2, Ch.11–15)","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 11: Batch Marks at Chalkway Sump\n\nBell One slid thin along the underpipes. The sump room at Chalkway stank of iron and steeped cloth. Condensation ran down the wall in a slow sheet. Tiffani stood ankle-deep in runoff with the Lume already up, light skimming a seam where yesterday’s repair wore a perfect edge—except the Veil lip had lifted a fingernail on the downstream corner.\n\n“Edge lift,” she said. “No reason for it.”\n\nJhace pressed a knuckle to the joint. The plate felt cool, honest. “Field’s even.”\n\nShe ran a thumbnail under the lifted lip. The skin flexed, then tore clean along its own weave. “Veil delam.”\n\n“Bad batch?”\n\n“Or someone stored it hot.”\n\nBrighlee’s voice echoed from the hatch. “Work order nine-nine-two. Veil failure, Chalkway Sump. Local hold to foot traffic.” She clipped the slip inside the hatch mouth and stayed put. Functional today.\n\nTiffani peeled another edge. The skin tore with no stretch, like old paper. “Shade mark reads one,” she said, squinting at the faint stamp. “Lot L/P–43.”\n\n“Lonce & Parr,” Jhace said. “Crown supplier.”\n\nShe nodded once. “We fix the seam first.”\n\nHe set a wedge midline to quiet any wander, then thumbed the bladder and drew a short wet along the seam where the Veil had lifted. Half-beat Slake. Steam rose and vanished. The line took at once. No fault in the plate.\n\nTiffani warmed a new Shade 1 from her kit—different stamp, a local mark—and laid it in with seven taps per edge. The lip sealed tight, no haze.\n\n“Test,” Jhace said.\n\nShe ran the Lume along it. Light came back flat. “Set.” Her eyes went to the torn skin in her palm. “We’re not done.”\n\nHe followed her gaze. “You think it’s wider?”\n\n“We check every Veil we laid yesterday near the Crown feeds.”\n\nHe grimaced. “That’s eight sites.”\n\n“Better than losing a span.”\n\nThey took Chalkway’s ladder up to the side run and crossed three corners where the pipes were numbered in old paint. At the second site, a Veil on the lower lip had bubbled like a blister. Tiffani cut it with her knife. The skin split along straight grain.\n\n“Same lot,” she said.\n\nJhace swore softly. “Pulling it.”\n\nHe wedged shallow, damped the seam, and Tiffani replaced the skin. They moved fast. At the third site, the Veil was intact but tacky to the touch.\n\nShe tested the edge with a fingernail. The lip smeared. “Heat in storage,” she said. “Lonce & Parr kept it too close to a main.”\n\n“Post recall?”\n\n“Not yet. We need three marks.”\n\nBy Bell Two they had five. Each failure carried the same tiny lot stamp under the corner curl: L/P–43. Tiffani logged the codes on a scrap. Brighlee wrote behind them without being asked, ink making small, precise sounds.\n\nAt the sixth site a teamster waited with a handcart loaded with Crown-branded skins. “Delivery for Conclave stores,” he said.\n\nTiffani didn’t answer. She cut a corner off the top skin. It tore like chalky bark.\n\n“Where from?” Jhace asked.\n\n“Lonce & Parr,” the teamster said, shifting like he knew he’d brought a problem to the wrong door.\n\nTiffani held up the torn piece. “Take it back.”\n\n“I’m paid to deliver to store.”\n\n“Then you can tell them you weren’t allowed.”\n\nHe glanced at Jhace. Jhace stared at the cart until the man glanced away. “Fine,” the teamster muttered, and turned the cart.\n\nThey finished the rounds just as Mel Quarr caught them at the seventh corner. “You’re walking a pattern,” she said.\n\n“Tainted Veil lot,” Tiffani said, handing her the scrap. “L/P–43. We replaced six. Two more not yet laid, in a cart headed back.”\n\nMel rolled a bit of the torn skin between her fingers. It turned to crumbs. “I can’t clear what I didn’t see fail.”\n\n“Then witness the replacements,” Jhace said. “Or come back tomorrow to a crack.”\n\nMel met his eyes a long count, then nodded. “Show me one.”\n\nThey took her to the bubbled Veil on the lower lip. Tiffani lifted it clean with a knife, exposing the good trace below.\n\n“Plate’s honest,” Jhace said, and drew a short wet to refresh the line. Steam flicked, then went still.\n\nTiffani laid a Shade 1 from a known good lot. Tap, tap, seven per edge.\n\nMel pressed her palm to the seal and stayed still. “This holds,” she said. “Post replacements for all L/P–43 placed within the last two Bells. I’ll sign.”\n\nBrighlee Tempermarked the note and clipped it beside the hatch. “Lot quarantine,” she said under her breath, like the words had their own weight.\n\n“Cost?” Tiffani asked Jhace as they packed.\n\nHe flexed his wrapped hand. Blood had dried brown along one edge. “Grip’s back. Knee’s gone.”\n\n“Clinic.”\n\n“Policy.”\n\nThey left the sump cleaner than they found it and walked back into the Underface where steam threw long, soft shadows. By Bell Three, the rumor had reached the Crown. Runners began pulling L/P–43 from Conclave shelves without waiting for orders.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **LOT QUARANTINE NOTICE — Bell 3** Veil Shade 1 lot L/P–43 (Lonce & Parr) found delaminating under normal load. Immediate recall and replacement mandated. All placements within two Bells to be inspected and re-sealed. Witness: Mel Quarr. Signatories: Tiffani veld, Jhace brast. Behavior change: all incoming Veil lots to be thermal-stored and sample-tested prior to issue.\nImage — A neat line of torn Veil corners pinned to a board by the hatch, each marked L/P–43, while a single new skin cools flush and quiet on the seam below.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 12: Skew Line at Wains Cut\n\nBell Seven carried dust. The Cut at Wains threw noise in strange directions, the kind that hid faults until they were underfoot. Tiffani and Jhace made the east stair in twenty-seven breaths, short and counted, then stopped at a joint where the floor plates met the wall collar. The line ran straight for two spans and then drifted a hair toward the drain.\n\n“Skew,” Jhace said. “Someone chased a leak with dry trace.”\n\nTiffani crouched, Lume low. The line was pretty and wrong—neat, thin, and shiny. “Whitequench’s cousin,” she said. “Looks good. Lies.”\n\nBrighlee arrived with the ledger already open. “Work order one-two-one-eight. Line correction, Wains Cut. Local hold on foot.” Functional again; no garnish.\n\nA supervisor from the Cut eased close, hands empty, eyes hungry. “We need it open,” he said. “Our crew kept it neat.”\n\n“Neat isn’t safe,” Tiffani said. “Back two meters.”\n\nHe didn’t move. Jhace did, a half-step that put his shoulder between Tiffani and the man. The supervisor took the hint and faded.\n\n“Angle?” Jhace asked.\n\n“Two degrees toward drain for three spans,” Tiffani said, tapping chalk marks. “Then straight to the corner. We pull the lie and give it a path.”\n\nHe set two wedges lightly to keep the plate from talking while they corrected the line. “Half-beat Slake,” he said, more to himself than to her.\n\nShe cut the wet into the white. The false line lifted like shed skin when the real pressure touched it. It came away in narrow curls that stank faint of solvent. “Shop trace,” she said. “They painted it.”\n\nJhace swore. “Who?”\n\n“Same lot mark from the Veil recall,” Brighlee said without looking up. “Lonce & Parr sells more than skins.”\n\nTiffani nodded once, tight. “We post it later. Fix it now.”\n\nShe traced the true line with a bead heavy enough to sink into the plate’s shallow grain. Jhace fed from his bladder, steady. They Slaked on the half-beat, then bridged the turn with a small counterline to keep the path honest.\n\n“Pulse?” he asked.\n\nShe listened, mouth open. “Level.”\n\n“Veil?”\n\nShe warmed a Shade 1 from a Crown lot that had passed her knife test twice this morning and pressed it down. Tap, tap. The seal went dull.\n\nThe supervisor edged closer again. “So we’re clear?”\n\n“No,” Tiffani said, not looking up. “We’re safe. Clear when the witness signs.”\n\nMel Quarr came on the last word as if conjured by impatience. She crouched, laid her palm to the line, and went still. Her nose wrinkled. “I hate the smell of shop trace.”\n\nTiffani held up a curl. “We’re posting a ban.”\n\n“Good,” Mel said. “This holds.” She wrote the signature on Brighlee’s page. “Post your quarantine across product, not lot. Lonce & Parr doesn’t get to sell glue to hold down lies.”\n\nThe supervisor tried again. “We needed the lane.”\n\n“You need a city,” Jhace said. “This keeps it.”\n\nMel stood. “Send a runner to Mercers’ Table. Mobel likes melting false measures.”\n\n“Already on the page,” Brighlee said, clipping the SHOP TRACE BAN beside the hatch. Hands paused, then moved.\n\n“Cost?” Tiffani asked.\n\nJhace flexed his hand. “Grip back. Knee worse.”\n\n“Clinic after Bell Nine,” Mel said. “I’ll write the slip so you can’t argue.”\n\nHe didn’t try.\n\nThey cleared the hold. The Cut’s supervisor kept his eyes on his boots while the first few workers stepped past the post, glancing down at the new, dull line as if it might speak.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **WAINS CUT LINE CORRECTION — Bell 7–8** Dry “shop trace” removed; true wet trace laid and Slaked; counterline at turn. Veil (tested Crown lot) applied. Witness: Mel Quarr. Policy expansion: ban on cosmetic trace compounds; all suppliers to provide heat-and-wet certification; notice to Mercers’ Table for enforcement. Costs: setter knee strain. Behavior change: supervisors keep two-meter stand-off during live correction.\nImage — A thin curl of false trace laid on the bench beside the hatch, gray and brittle, while the corrected line below it sits dull, straight, and uninteresting in the way that keeps floors up.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 13: Thirteen Corners\n\nBell Eleven began with a miscount. The Crown clock stuttered on the second note, and in the Underface three corners away, a drip slowed exactly in time. Jhace didn’t hear any of that, but he felt the city tighten, the way steel answers steel.\n\nThe first call hit Brighlee’s board as a smear: *Plate lift at Farwell.* Before she finished posting, a runner brought *Veil peel at Rookwell.* Then *Coupler chatter at Gray Arch.* The paper stacked crooked. She didn’t panic. She sorted. “You split,” she said. “Tiffani north. Jhace west.”\n\nThey didn’t argue. He took the west bag and ran.\n\nFARWELL\nTwo wedges already in, wrong places, a crewman white-faced and sweating. “It started to whine—”\n\n“Don’t use that field,” Jhace said. He shoved the man back with his hip, set a proper wedge at the shoulder, low and flat. The plate’s flutter dropped. He traced a short wet from the bow to the anchor, Slaked on the half, and nodded. “Now breathe.” He was gone before thanks could form.\n\nROOKWELL\nTiffani arrived to find the Veil lifting in three fingers along a turn. Lot stamp: not L/P–43. Something else. She pried the corner, found grit under the lip—poor clean. “Who set this?” she asked. No one answered. She fixed it anyway: lift, wipe, wet, seal. She left the torn skin on the post where shame could see it.\n\nGRAY ARCH\nJhace hit the coupler at a fast jog and felt the chatter through his teeth. He didn’t wait for light. He laid both palms, counted the pulse, then drove a brace rod through the flange before he took tools out of the bag. When he finally looked up, a junior from the night shift was trying to look small. “I tightened to proud,” the kid said.\n\nJhace didn’t waste words. He loosened, reset, Slaked. “Watch,” he said. The kid did.\n\nBRIDGEGATE SIDE RUN\nTiffani found a drip at the side run that wasn’t a leak. It was condensation from a cooler line above, falling on a Veil edge. She moved the drip with a copper tap line, no fanfare, and wrote a single word on Brighlee’s page when the clerk arrived: *misread.*\n\nLOWCOURT RETURN\nJhace climbed the scaffold he already knew well. The band they’d set days ago held. He just wrote, *Holding,* and moved.\n\nBLACKGATE ONRAMP\nTiffani caught the updraft of carts before she saw the seam. The old wet trace was intact; the bow at the mouth was shallow and true. But a friend line on the upper lip had begun to wander. She ran a single correction line with the bead barely there. It answered.\n\nWATER TUNNEL FOUR\nJhace dropped into the tunnel where the air had the clean cold taste of new iron. A faint pulse wandered in the main. He set wedges, traced a little, no artistry. It took. He grinned despite himself. “Even the city gets tired,” he said to no one.\n\nRINDLE YARD RETURN\nTiffani came back to the double brace they’d forced into being. It held, edges stained with old water, proud and calm. She wrote on the post: *Precedent holds.*\n\nCHALKWAY SUMP\nJhace checked the replacement Veil. The good lot still read dull and tight. The pinned corners of L/P–43 above the hatch looked like insects. He left them.\n\nWAINS CUT\nTiffani passed the bench where the false trace curl still sat. Someone had put a nail through it for spite. She didn’t remove it.\n\nNARROWVALE APPROACH\nJhace paused. The HALT banner was gone, rolled and stored. The seam still carried the marriage like a scar that knew its place. He touched the line once with the back of his fingers and moved on.\n\nMIRECOURT\nTiffani found only fog. The leak they’d fixed stayed fixed. She wrote *steady* and turned.\n\nGREYMANTLE CUT\nJhace waited at the last step and listened. The lock below moved water in a slow, obedient draw. He didn’t go down. He didn’t need to.\n\nThe round took three Bells. They met again where they’d started, at Brighlee’s board. The paper stack had become a neat column with times and marks.\n\n“Count?” Jhace asked.\n\n“Thirteen corners,” Brighlee said. “No failures.” Her fingers were stained gray from page edges. “One junior wrote himself up.”\n\nTiffani tilted her head toward Gray Arch. “Good.”\n\nMel Quarr stepped out of the press of bodies, hair stuck to her face, coat open. “I read your marks as I walked,” she said. “This is what it looks like when a city learns.”\n\nJhace stretched his burned arm with slow care. “Or when it chooses not to forget.”\n\nMel’s mouth twitched. “Don’t get poetic.”\n\nHe didn’t.\n\nShe signed the top sheet without kneeling this time. “I’ll witness the round as a set. Post the list.”\n\nTiffani wiped her knife. “Costs?”\n\nJhace looked at his hand. The split had closed, but the skin was thick and new. “Grip returning. Knee still wrong.”\n\n“Dry mouth,” Tiffani said. “Low sugar.”\n\n“Clinic,” Mel said. “Both of you. Salee’s posted new slips.”\n\nThey didn’t argue.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **ROUND REPORT — Thirteen Corners — Bells 11–14** Farwell plate correction; Rookwell Veil reseal; Gray Arch coupler reset and Slake; Bridgegate drip redirected; Lowcourt band inspected; Blackgate friend line corrected; Water Tunnel Four damped; Rindle precedent confirmed; Chalkway post-check; Wains false-trace display maintained; Narrowvale marriage check; Mirecourt steady; Greymantle lock observed. Witness: Mel Quarr, set-level. Behavior change: junior self-report procedure used at Gray Arch; display of failed materials maintained at public benches. Costs: fatigue, persistent knee strain, minor burn stiffness.\nImage — Thirteen small chalk ticks down the board’s right edge, each crossed when cleared, chalk dust bright on Brighlee’s knuckles.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 14: Salee’s Slip (Interlude)\n\nBell Three at the Clinic rang like the edge of a cool bowl. Salee stood behind the counter with a stack of blue slips and a stamp that smelled faintly of lemon oil. Workers came in pairs or alone, shoulders straight until they saw the color and then they sagged.\n\n“Name,” she said to the first.\n\n“Jhace brast,” Brighlee answered for him, pushing him forward by the elbow, not looking at his face like dignity required.\n\nSalee didn’t blink. “Burn, persistent. Knee strain.” She wrote fast, neat. “Rest prescription: one Bell immediate, two after shift. Paid.”\n\nJhace started to object. Salee slid the slip into his hand without touching his skin. “Policy,” she said. “Posted.”\n\nHe looked at the wall where the placard hung beside the clinic door. The words were plain: *Mandatory rest after consecutive shifts exceeding four Bells. Paid. Enforcement: Clinic stamp overrides work order.*\n\nTiffani stood to the side, color back in her face but eyes ringed. Salee wrote her a second slip: *Dehydration, Shade error corrected. Rest: one Bell. Paid.*\n\nA foreman edged up, hat in hands. “We’re light on crew.”\n\nSalee stamped a third slip and handed it to him. “Then reschedule. That’s the point.”\n\nHe opened his mouth. Closed it. Took the paper.\n\nMel Quarr signed the margin of the new placard with a small, precise hand. “Witness,” she said, not loud, but no one missed it.\n\nCorlexi passed through with a satchel of old clinic forms headed for the burn barrel. He touched the edge of the new sign like it might be hot. “About time,” he said.\n\nMobel from the Mercers’ Table arrived with a tin of boiled sweets and set them on the counter. “For compliance,” he said. “Not as bribe.”\n\nSalee cracked a smile with one side of her mouth. “Accepted.”\n\nShe stamped without pause for an hour. The counter filled with the small sounds of paper, the big silence of people not arguing. At the half-Bell, she replaced the ink pad with a fresh one and posted the second part of the policy beneath the first: *Repeat offenders: mandatory two-Bell rest. Dock illegal.*\n\nA boy from the Underface brought his mother’s note: *Breath short in cold.* Salee stamped that too, then handed the boy a sweet and a folded copy of the rest placard. “Post this at her workroom,” she said. “High where she can see it.”\n\nHe nodded, small and serious.\n\nWhen the Bell chimed the quarter, Tiffani and Jhace sat side by side on a bench under the window. They didn’t speak. Their shoulders dropped at the same moment like a weight setting. Salee watched without looking like she was watching and decided not to put that on any form.\n\nShe finished the stack, wiped the stamp clean, and posted the day’s count on a small slate: *Rest slips issued: 38. Refusals: 0.*\n\nOutside the clinic, the city sounded exactly the same as before. Inside, it didn’t.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **CLINIC REST POLICY POSTING — Bell 3** Mandatory paid rest after four consecutive Bells of work; clinic stamp overrides open work orders; repeat offenders to two-Bell mandatory rest; posting witnessed by Conclave Setter Mel Quarr. Behavior change: immediate compliance observed across trades; foreman scheduling adjusted under clinic authority.\nImage — Blue slips stacked in a tray, edges damp from fresh ink, while a single boiled sweet sits untouched on the counter next to Salee’s stamp.\n\n---\n\n# Chapter 15: Small Fix at Lantern Stairs\n\nBell Six came soft along Lantern Stairs, filtered through laundry lines and morning cook smoke. The call was a polite one: *minor creak at the mid-landing brace.* No one rushed. They went anyway.\n\nTiffani reached the landing first and set her palm on the brace plate. A fine vibration ticked under the skin, more habit than hazard.\n\n“Friend line wandered,” she said.\n\nJhace stood beside her, hand on the home ring without being told. “Quarter span, uphill.”\n\nShe angled the Lume. Light came back level. “Vein’s behaved. Just forgotten.”\n\nHe set a short wedge to keep the plate from narrating, wetted the copper nib, and drew a quiet line from the brace edge into the main seam. The bead formed, held, dulled. He Slaked on the half-beat without saying it aloud. Steam was a thin breath in cool air.\n\n“Veil?” he asked.\n\n“Small one,” she said, warming a palm-wide Shade 1 and laying it so the friend line couldn’t go wandering again. Tap, tap, tap, in sevens. The sound of it matched the stairs’ shallow shift as someone passed above them carrying a bucket.\n\nBrighlee stood at the post and wrote a single sentence. Functional. “Lantern Stairs friend line corrected. Bell Six.”\n\nMel Quarr didn’t come. She didn’t have to. The city didn’t need a witness to remember a good habit. Still, Tiffani stood another count with her palm on the plate, listening. “Level,” she said.\n\n“Set,” he said.\n\nThey packed slow. Small job. Quiet fix.\n\nAt the top of the stairs the street widened into a small landing where a window leaked warm kitchen air. Someone inside was making broth; the smell soaked into their coats. Tiffani set her case on the low wall and unlaced his cuff, fingers sure of the knot because she’d tied it yesterday when his hands shook.\n\n“Stay,” she said, eyes on the thread as she teased it free.\n\nHe could feel the tremor looking for a reason to start. It didn’t find one. Breath evened. Street noise carried in from two corners away, ordinary as coins.\n\nShe set his hand on the stone ledge until the new line cooled. They went back down to look once more at the seam. It was dull and nothing to look at. Perfect.\n\nThey unhooked from the ring, and the stairs carried people again, one at a time, careful without being told.\n\n\nContinuity:\nArtifact — **LANTERN STAIRS — Bell 6** Friend line correction at mid-landing brace; small Veil added; no hold required. Clerk Brighlee recorded; no witness required under maintenance code. Costs: none beyond time. Behavior change: foot traffic slowed one step without signage.\nImage — A thin bead sitting flat under a palm-wide skin while a bucket crosses the landing above and the scent of broth threads the cool air."}

[ASSISTANT]
Ch. 11–15 are on the canvas, scrubbed and tightened:

- Music-field verbs purged (“sing” at Farwell → “whine”), no cute metaphors.
- Brighlee kept in one mode per scene.
- Any ledger-like labels remain in Continuity only.
- Consent beats show result, zero commentary.
- Added the posted SHOP TRACE BAN at Wains.
- Killed that lingering “Behavior changed.” stage whisper and let the action do it.

If anything else is squeaking, it’s not the prose.
